Proof of the Impossibility of Change in Allah
Sanusi proves that change cannot occur in Allah or His attributes using wujub al-qidam and baqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why change is impossible in Allah and His attributes, based on the twin necessities of pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa).

Core Idea
Allah cannot change because He has always existed and will always exist — any change would imply non-existence before or after.

Sanusi has already established that Allah's essence and attributes are necessarily pre-eternal and everlasting. In this passage, he uses those two attributes to directly prove that change is impossible. He considers all logical possibilities — any change must be either from non-existence to existence or vice versa — and shows that each is negated by qidam and baqa.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed logical style.
2
Identify the two categories of change
The passage presents two possibilities for any change: from non-existence to existence, or from existence to non-existence. Find where these are mentioned.
These are the only two logical possibilities for change.
3
Understand how each is prevented
See how wujub al-qidam prevents the first kind, and wujub al-baqa prevents the second. The definitions given are key: qidam negates prior non-existence, baqa negates subsequent non-existence.
This shows that change is impossible for both essence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا كله ظاهر ، ولنزده بياناً فنقول :
أما وجه استحالةِ التغير على الذاتِ العليَّةِ وعلى صفاتها : فلأنَّهُ إِنْ كانَ مِنْ عدم إلى وجود .. فوجوبُ القدم للذات الكريمة ولجميعِ صفاتها يمنع ذلكَ ؛ لأنَّهُ عبارةٌ عن سلبِ العدم السابق على الوجودِ ، وإنْ كانَ مِنْ وجودِ إلى عدم .. فوجوبُ البقاء لهما يدفعُهُ ؛ لأنَّه عبارةٌ عن سلبِ العدم اللاحق بعد الوجودِ ، وقد سبق في العقيدة ذكرُ برهانِ وجوب القدم والبقاء للذات العليَّةِ ولصفاتها
This is all apparent, and to increase clarification we say: As for the aspect of the impossibility of change upon the Supreme Essence and upon its attributes: because if it were from non-existence to existence, then the necessity of pre-eternity (wujub al-qidam) for the noble essence and all its attributes prevents that; because it is an expression of the negation of prior non-existence to existence. And if it were from existence to non-existence, then the necessity of everlastingness (wujub al-baqa) for both repels it; because it is an expression of the negation of subsequent non-existence after existence. And the proof of the necessity of pre-eternity and everlastingness for the Supreme Essence and for its attributes has preceded in the creed.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage provides the central proof for the impossibility of change in Allah and His attributes. It begins by stating that the impossibility of change is clear, then elaborates. The argument is dichotomous: any change must be either from non-existence to existence (origination) or from existence to non-existence (annihilation). The first is impossible because the attribute of wujub al-qidam (necessity of pre-eternity) negates any prior non-existence; indeed, qidam is defined as the negation of non-existence preceding existence. The second is impossible because wujub al-baqa (necessity of everlastingness) negates any subsequent non-existence; baqa is defined as the negation of non-existence after existence. The author notes that the proof for these two attributes has already been given earlier in the creed (referring to the standard arguments for Allah's eternity and everlastingness). Thus, change is categorically impossible for Allah's essence and attributes.

Key Terms
وجوب القدم
وجوب البقاء
تغير
ذات
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Change could occur in some attributes while the essence remains unchanged.
Correction: The argument explicitly applies to both essence and attributes; both are covered by qidam and baqa, so no change in either is possible.

Checkpoint
In the proof of impossibility of change, what does wujub al-qidam prevent?
A) Change from existence to non-existence.
B) Change from non-existence to existence.
C) Both types of change.
D) Only change in attributes.

Key Takeaway
Change in Allah is impossible because His pre-eternity negates any prior non-existence and His everlastingness negates any subsequent non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that Allah cannot change in any way. How does this challenge your daily experience of people, circumstances, and even your own self, which are constantly changing? Are you resting your heart on Someone who is absolutely unchanging, or are you still seeking stability in things that shift? Today, commit to one area where you will consciously rely on Allah's constancy instead of your own fluctuating feelings.
?
If Allah's attributes—including His mercy, justice, and wrath—are unchanging, what does that mean for how you approach Him after sin? Do you assume His mercy 'increases' when you repent? While His mercy is vast, it is eternally the same. Does this truth deepen your hope or make you more serious about obedience? What will you change in your repentance to reflect this understanding?
?
Consider the impossibility of change in Allah’s attributes: He was always perfectly merciful, just, and powerful—even before creation. How does this affect your view of creation? Did He need the world to express His attributes? If not, then why did He create? Let this lesson move you from a transactional view of worship to a posture of sheer gratitude. Starting now, thank Allah for His perfect, unchattering attributes—not for what He gives, but for who He is.

Why Divine Knowledge Cannot Be Acquired
Sanusi explains why divine knowledge cannot be acquired and discusses kasb's meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the impossibility of acquired knowledge for Allah, including the two definitions of kasb and the debate about the need for prior consideration.

Core Idea
Acquired knowledge is inherently temporal; Allah's knowledge is eternal and unchanging.

This passage tackles a subtle point about divine knowledge. Sanusi explains that acquired knowledge (kasb) is necessarily temporal, so it cannot apply to Allah's eternal knowledge. He then explores the definitions of kasb and a related debate about the necessity of prior consideration (nazar).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two key claims: (1) acquired knowledge is temporal, (2) Allah's knowledge is eternal.
The argument builds on these two premises.
2
Identify the two definitions of kasb
Sanusi gives two interpretations of acquired knowledge: knowledge from consideration (nazar) or knowledge linked to temporal power. Note which one he says is the original meaning.
Both definitions imply temporality.
3
Understand the debate on prior consideration
The passage presents two opinions on whether prior consideration is necessary for acquiring knowledge. Sanusi sides with Imam al-Haramayn. Focus on the reasoning: because the substance's acceptance of knowledge is inherent, and consideration would contradict it.
This refines our understanding of kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ استحالة الكسب على علمهِ تعالى ، وتأويلُ ما يوهم حدوث العلم ] وأما ما ذكرتُ من استحالة الكسب على علمهِ تعالى : فظاهرُ ؛ لأنَّ العلم الكسبي لا يكون إلا حادثاً ، وعلمُهُ جل وعلا قديم لا يتجدَّدُ ، وإنَّما قلنا : إنَّ الكسبيَّ لا يكون إلا حادثاً ؛ لأنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يُفسَّرَ بالعلم الحاصل عن النظرِ ؛ وهو الذي غلب عليه العرفُ ، أو بما تعلقت القدرة الحادثة، ولا يخفى تجدده وحدوثه على كلا التفسيرين ، وهذا الثاني هو معناه الأصلي ، وهل يستلزم سبق النظرِ عقلاً أو عادةً ؛ فيجوز في العقلِ إحداث علم وإحداث قدرةٍ عليهِ مِنْ غيرِ تقدُّمِ نظر ؟ قولان ، والثاني مذهبُ إمام الحرمين ، وهو الحق ؛ لأنَّ قبول الجوهر للعلم والقدرة عليه نفسي له ، وتقدم النظرِ لا يصلح أن يكونَ شرطاً للقدرة على العلم؛ لأنَّ القدرة مقارنة للعلم ، والنظرُ ينافيه ، ولا يصلحُ أنْ يكونَ شرطُ الشيء ما لا يُوجَدُ إلا حالَ عدمه
[Impossibility of acquisition (al-kasb) for His knowledge, may He be Exalted, and interpretation of what suggests temporality of knowledge] And as for what I mentioned of the impossibility of acquisition for His knowledge, may He be Exalted: it is apparent; because acquired knowledge (al-ilm al-kasbi) cannot be except temporal, and His knowledge, may He be Mighty and Majestic, is eternal, does not renew. And we only said that the acquired cannot be except temporal because it is either interpreted as knowledge resulting from consideration (al-nazar); and this is what custom has prevailed, or as that to which the temporal power (al-qudra al-haditha) is attached; and its renewal and temporality are not hidden according to both interpretations. And this second is its original meaning. And does it necessitate precedence of consideration intellectually or customarily? So is it intellectually permissible to bring into existence knowledge and bring into existence power over it without prior consideration? Two opinions. The second is the school of Imam al-Haramayn, and it is the truth; because the substance's acceptance of knowledge and power over it is inherent to it, and the precedence of consideration cannot be a condition for power over knowledge; because power is concurrent with knowledge, and consideration contradicts it, and it cannot be that the condition of a thing is something that only exists in the state of its absence.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage addresses the impossibility of acquired knowledge (kasb) applying to Allah's knowledge. It begins by stating this impossibility is apparent because acquired knowledge is necessarily temporal (hadith), while Allah's knowledge is eternal and does not renew. The author then explains why acquired knowledge is necessarily temporal: it is defined either as knowledge resulting from consideration (nazar) – which is the customary meaning – or as that to which a temporal power is attached. Both interpretations entail renewal and temporality. The original meaning of kasb is the second, i.e., the attachment of temporal power. Then a subsidiary question arises: does acquired knowledge necessarily require prior consideration (nazar) as an intellectual or customary condition? Two opinions exist. The author adopts the view of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) that prior consideration is not a necessary condition; it is intellectually permissible for knowledge and power to be created without prior consideration. The proof given is that the substance's (jawhar's) acceptance of knowledge and power is inherent (nafsi) to it. Prior consideration cannot be a condition for power over knowledge because power is simultaneous with knowledge, while consideration contradicts it (since consideration is a process that ends when knowledge is attained). Furthermore, a condition of a thing cannot be something that only exists when the thing is absent.

Key Terms
كسب
علم كسبي
حادث
قديم
نظر
قدرة حادثة
جوهر
نفسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that acquired knowledge could be reconciled with Allah's eternity if kasb is defined in a special way.
Correction: The passage shows that both definitions of kasb entail temporality, so it cannot be attributed to Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the original meaning of 'kasb' according to the passage?
A) Knowledge resulting from consideration (nazar).
B) That to which temporal power is attached.
C) The customary usage.
D) Eternal knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Acquired knowledge is impossible for Allah because it is inherently temporal, while His knowledge is eternal and unchanging.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You possess acquired knowledge that is temporal and dependent on causes. Have you ever clung to an opinion as if it were eternal, refusing to seek fresh guidance from Allah? How does the contrast between your temporal knowledge and Allah's eternal knowledge compel you to hold your convictions with humility and openness?
?
The passage teaches that power over knowledge is concurrent with knowledge, not preceded by endless consideration. In your life, do you delay acting on what you know because you feel you need more reasoning? What would it look like to trust that Allah has placed in you the capacity to act rightly without requiring prior certainty?
?
Sanusi argues that the substance has an inherent capacity to accept knowledge and power. If that is true for the physical substance, how much more for your soul? Do you truly believe that Allah has endowed you with the innate capacity to know Him and obey Him, without needing external justification? What would change if you lived from that conviction today?

Impossibility of Acquired Knowledge on God's Knowledge (Part 1)
Sanusi defines acquired knowledge and asserts its temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of acquired knowledge (kasb) and why it is necessarily temporal, thus impossible for God.

Core Idea
Acquired knowledge is always temporal, but God's knowledge is eternal.

Sanusi begins by stating the impossibility of attributing acquired knowledge (kasb) to Allah. He explains that acquired knowledge is inherently temporal (ḥādith), while Allah's knowledge is eternal and does not renew. He then defines acquired knowledge in two ways, both leading to temporality, and clarifies which definition is original.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the structure: Sanusi begins with a claim, then defines, then clarifies.
2
Identify the two definitions of kasb
Find the two ways acquired knowledge is defined: (1) knowledge resulting from reasoning, (2) knowledge to which temporal power pertains. Note which is the original meaning.
The second definition is the original linguistic meaning.
3
Understand the implication of temporality
Ask yourself why both definitions imply temporality. Why can't these apply to Allah? Because His knowledge is eternal and does not renew.
This is the key reason for impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما ذكرت من استحالة الكسب على علمه تعالى : فظاهر ؛ لأن العلم الكسبي لا يكون إلا حادثاً ، وعلمه جل وعلا قديم لا يتجدد ، وإنما قلنا : إن الكسبي لا يكون إلا حادثاً ؛ لأنه إما أن يفسر بالعلم الحاصل عن النظر ؛ وهو الذي غلب عليه العرف ، أو بما تعلقت القدرة الحادثة، ولا يخفى تجدده وحدوثه على كلا التفسيرين ، وهذا الثاني هو معناه الأصلي ، وهل يستلزم سبق النظر عقلاً أو عادة ؛ فيجوز في العقل إحداث علم وإحداث قدرة عليه من غير تقدم نظر ؟ قولان ، والثاني مذهب إمام الحرمين ، وهو الحق ؛ لأن قبول الجوهر للعلم والقدرة عليه نفسي له ، وتقدم النظر لا يصلح أن يكون شرطاً للقدرة على العلم ؛ لأن القدرة مقارنة للعلم ، والنظر ينافيه ، ولا يصلح أن يكون شرط الشيء ما لا يوجد إلا حال عدمه . وأما عدم اشتراط النظر في العلم .. فللاتفاق على أن العلم النظري يجوز أن يقع ضرورياً . وإذا عرفت استحالة الكسب على علمه تعالى ؛ لإيذانه بسبق الجهل ، واتصاف الذات العلية بوصف حادث .. عرفت أن ما وقع في الكتاب والسنة موهماً ظاهره حدوث العلم وكسبه يجب القطع بأن ظاهره غير مراد
As for what I mentioned regarding the impossibility of acquired knowledge (al-kasb) upon His knowledge, may He be exalted: it is apparent, because acquired knowledge can only be temporal, and His knowledge, may He be glorified and exalted, is eternal and does not renew. We only say that acquired knowledge can only be temporal because it is either interpreted as knowledge resulting from reasoning (al-naẓar)—which is the prevalent customary meaning—or as that to which temporal power is attached, and its renewal and temporality are not hidden on both interpretations. This second is its original meaning. Does it necessitate the precedence of reasoning intellectually or customarily, so that it is intellectually permissible to bring about knowledge and bring about power over it without prior reasoning? There are two opinions. The second is the doctrine of Imam al-Ḥaramayn, and it is the truth, because the substance's receptivity to knowledge and power over it is intrinsic to it, and the precedence of reasoning cannot be a condition for power over knowledge, because power is simultaneous with knowledge, and reasoning contradicts it. It is not valid for a condition of a thing to be something that does not exist except in the state of its absence. As for the non-conditionality of reasoning in knowledge, it is by consensus that theoretical knowledge may occur as necessary. And if you have understood the impossibility of acquired knowledge upon His knowledge, may He be exalted—because it implies the precedence of ignorance and the attribution of a temporal attribute to the exalted essence—then you understand that what occurs in the Book and the Sunnah that apparently suggests the temporal occurrence and acquisition of knowledge must be decisively understood as its apparent meaning not being intended.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a proof and a methodological rule. Sanusi begins by stating the impossibility (istiḥāla) of acquired knowledge (al-kasb) on God's knowledge. He explains that acquired knowledge is necessarily temporal (ḥādith), while God's knowledge is eternal and does not renew. He then defines acquired knowledge in two ways: (1) knowledge resulting from reasoning (al-naẓar), which is the common usage, and (2) knowledge to which a temporal power (qudra ḥāditha) pertains. Both interpretations entail temporality. Sanusi notes that the second is the original meaning of kasb. Next, he discusses whether acquired knowledge requires antecedent reasoning—intellectually or customarily—or whether it can occur without such reasoning. He mentions two opinions, and asserts that the correct view, that of Imam al-Ḥaramayn, is that antecedent reasoning is not a condition. The reason is that the substance's (jawhar's) receptivity to knowledge and power is intrinsic; reasoning is simultaneous with knowledge, not prior, and cannot be a condition that is absent when the conditioned exists. He also appeals to consensus that theoretical knowledge can become necessary. Finally, Sanusi draws the conclusion: since kasb is impossible for God, any scriptural passage (Qur'an or Sunnah) that suggests temporal occurrence or acquisition must be interpreted metaphorically; its apparent meaning is not intended.

علم كسبي
حادث
قديم
النظر
القدرة الحادثة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'acquired knowledge' (kasb) always means knowledge gained through reasoning.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that its original meaning is knowledge to which temporal power pertains.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the original meaning of acquired knowledge (kasb)?
A) Knowledge resulting from reasoning
B) Knowledge to which temporal power pertains
C) Knowledge that is eternal
D) Knowledge without condition

Key Takeaway
Acquired knowledge (kasb) is always temporal, so it cannot be attributed to Allah whose knowledge is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek knowledge about Allah through your own reasoning or experiences, do you unconsciously treat that knowledge as if it were absolute and eternal, forgetting that all human knowledge is temporal and fallible? How does this lesson call you to humble yourself before the eternal knowledge of Allah?
?
Your life is filled with 'acquired knowledge'—facts, opinions, and conclusions you've drawn. Which of these do you hold most tightly, as if they were timeless truths? Have you ever considered that clinging to temporal knowledge might distract you from submitting to Allah's eternal knowledge? What would it look like to let go of one such certainty this week?

The Condition of Reasoning and Interpreting Apparent Verses
Sanusi argues that reasoning is not a condition for knowledge and establishes the rule for interpreting apparent verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether reasoning is a condition for acquired knowledge, the correct position, and the rule for interpreting scriptural passages that seem to imply temporality in God's knowledge.

Core Idea
Reasoning is not a condition for knowledge; apparent verses must be interpreted metaphorically.

In the second part of his argument, Sanusi discusses whether acquired knowledge requires prior reasoning. He presents two opinions and supports the view of Imam al-Haramayn that reasoning is not a condition. He then appeals to consensus that theoretical knowledge can become necessary. Finally, he draws the crucial conclusion: any scriptural verse that seems to suggest temporal acquisition of knowledge for God must be interpreted figuratively.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the condition of reasoning
Read the Arabic and translation from 'Wa hal yastalzim' to the end. Pay attention to the question and the two opinions.
2
Analyse the argument against reasoning as a condition
Sanusi argues that reasoning cannot be a condition because it is simultaneous with knowledge, not prior. Understand the point about the substance's intrinsic receptivity.
Imam al-Haramayn's view is presented as correct.
3
Consider the consensus on theoretical knowledge
Note that theoretical knowledge can occur as necessary, meaning without prior reasoning. This supports the view that reasoning is not a condition.
This is a key proof.
4
Apply the rule to scriptural interpretation
Understand the final conclusion: any verse that seems to imply temporal acquisition for Allah must be interpreted figuratively. Its apparent meaning is not intended.
This safeguards divine transcendence.

Interpretation of the Verse 'That God May Know' as Recompense
Sanusi interprets the Qur'anic verse about testing so that God may know, affirming God's eternal knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Qur'anic phrase 'so that God may know' must be interpreted as referring to recompense based on God's eternal knowledge, not as implying any change in God's knowledge.

Core Idea
God's eternal knowledge never changes; 'that God may know' means 'that God may recompense.'

When reading the Qur'an, we sometimes encounter verses that, taken literally, might seem to suggest that God's knowledge increases or changes with events. Sanusi addresses one such verse and provides a crucial interpretation (ta'wil) that safeguards the doctrine of God's eternal, unchanging knowledge. In this lesson, you will examine his argument and see how he resolves the apparent tension.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation once without analyzing. Let the author's own structure and flow come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two verses cited
Notice how the first verse (Surah al-Ankabut 3) presents the apparent problem, while the second (Surah al-Mulk 14) serves as a supporting proof for God's perfect knowledge.
The first verse is the object of interpretation; the second is a proof that God's knowledge is all-encompassing.
3
Understand the rational argument
Sanusi uses a rhetorical question ('How can that be?') to show that the literal reading is impossible because God's knowledge is eternal and encompasses everything.
4
Grasp the interpretation
The key move: the word 'knowledge' in the verse is used figuratively to mean 'recompense' (yujāzī) that occurs according to God's eternal knowledge.
This preserves the verse's meaning without contradicting the attribute of eternal knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك كقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَلَقَدْ فَتَنَّا الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِهِمْ فَلَيَعْلَمَنَّ اللَّهُ الَّذِينَ صَدَقُوا وَلَيَعْلَمَنَّ الْكَذِبِينَ ﴾ [العنكبوت : ٣] ، فليس المراد أنه تجدد له تعالى بالفتنة علم بالصادق والكاذب من خلقه ، كيف وعلمه جل وعلا أزلي محيط بكل معلوم، وعلى وفق علمه القديم وإرادته النافذة تجري أحكام الكائنات كلها ؟! ﴿ أَلَا يَعْلَمُ مَنْ خَلَقَ وَهُوَ اللَّطِيفُ الْخَبِيرُ ﴾ [ الملك : ١٤] وتأويل الآية : أن المراد الإخبار بأنه تعالى يجازي المكلفين بما علمه منهم أزلاً من خير أو شر ، فأطلق العلم على الجزاء المتأخر عن وقوع أمارته من خير أو شر ؛ لأن وقوع ذلك كله على وفق علمه جل كله
That is like His saying, the Exalted: 'And We have certainly tested those before them, so that God may know those who are truthful and that He may know the liars' [Surah al-ʿAnkabut 3]. It is not intended that through the trial knowledge of the truthful and the liar among His creation was renewed for Him, the Exalted. How can that be, while His knowledge, may He be glorified and exalted, is eternal, encompassing every known thing, and all affairs of existents occur according to His eternal knowledge and His penetrating will?! 'Does He who created not know, and He is the Subtle, the Aware' [Surah al-Mulk 14]. The interpretation (taʾwīl) of the verse is that the intended meaning is the informing that He, the Exalted, recompenses the morally responsible according to what He knew of them from eternity of good or evil. So knowledge was used to mean the recompense that occurs after the manifestation of its indicator of good or evil, because all that occurs is in accordance with His knowledge, may He be exalted in entirety.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation (taʾwīl) of a scriptural verse that might suggest God's knowledge occurs temporally. Sanusi cites the Qur'anic verse from Surah al-Ankabut: 'We have tested those before them so that God may know the truthful and the liars.' He argues that the verse does not mean that God's knowledge of the truthful and liars is new (mujaddad) or occurs after the test. He reinforces this with a rational question: how could that be, given that God's knowledge is eternal (azalī) and encompasses everything? He then quotes another verse (Surah al-Mulk 14) as a supporting proof: 'Does He who created not know, and He is the Subtle, the Aware.' Sanusi then provides the specific interpretation: the phrase 'so that God may know' actually means 'so that God may recompense' (yujāzī) based on His eternal knowledge of their deeds. The word 'knowledge' here is used figuratively to refer to the recompense that follows the appearance of its sign (amāra), which is the action itself. The entire occurrence is according to His eternal knowledge.

Correction: Sanusi clarifies it is a metaphorical expression for recompense based on eternal knowledge.

B) That God rewards or punishes according to His eternal knowledge
C) That God tests to discover who is truthful
D) That God's knowledge is limited to what is revealed

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging; verses that seem to indicate new knowledge are interpreted as referring to recompense based on that eternal knowledge.

Impossibility of Acquired Knowledge for Allah
Sanusi proves that acquisition is impossible for Allah and interprets a Quranic verse figuratively.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why acquired knowledge (kasb) is impossible for Allah and how to interpret Quranic verses that seem to suggest otherwise.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge is eternal and cannot be acquired; apparent references are metaphorical.

In this passage, Sanusi establishes a key principle of Sunni theology: Allah's knowledge is eternal and unchanging. He then applies this principle to interpret a Quranic verse that might be misunderstood as implying that Allah gains new knowledge. This lesson will guide you through his rational argument and his figurative interpretation of the verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic source and its literal English translation to get a sense of Sanusi's argument and the verse in question.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'kasb' and 'majaz'.
2
Understand the principle
Focus on why acquisition (kasb) is impossible for Allah. Sanusi argues it would imply prior ignorance and a temporal attribute, which are incompatible with Allah's perfection.
3
Examine the Quranic verse
Look at the verse from Surah Al-Ankabut (29:3) and consider how its apparent meaning might be misunderstood as suggesting Allah acquires new knowledge.
The phrase 'faya'lamannallah' is key.
4
Learn the metaphorical interpretation
Sanusi explains that 'knowledge' in the verse actually refers to recompense based on His eternal knowledge. Understand the metaphor of naming the effect after the cause.
This is a common linguistic device (majaz).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا عدمُ اشتراطِ النظرِ في العلم .. فللاتفاق على أنَّ العلم النظري يجوز أن يقع ضرورياً . وإذا عرفت استحالة الكسب على علمه تعالى ؛ لإيذانه بسبقِ الجهل ، واتصاف الذاتِ العليَّةِ بوصف حادثٍ .. عرفْتَ أنَّ ما وقع في الكتاب والسنَّةِ موهماً ظاهرُهُ حدوثَ العِلْمِ وكسبَهُ يجبُ القطعُ بأَنَّ ظاهره غير مراد. وذلك كقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَلَقَدْ فَتَنَّا الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِهِمْ فَلَيَعْلَمَنَّ اللَّهُ الَّذِينَ صَدَقُوا وَلَيَعْلَمَنَّ الْكَذِبِينَ ﴾ [العنكبوت : ٣]، فليس المرادُ أَنَّهُ تجدَّدَ لَهُ تعالى بالفتنة علم بالصادقِ والكاذبِ مِنْ خلقِهِ ، كيف وعلمُهُ جلَّ وعلا أزلي محيط بكلِّ معلوم، وعلى وَفْقِ علمه القديم وإرادته النافذة تجري أحكام الكائناتِ كلها ؟! ﴿ أَلَا يَعْلَمُ مَنْ خَلَقَ وَهُوَ اللَّطِيفُ الْخَبِيرُ [ الملك : ١٤] وتأويلُ الآية : أنَّ المراد الإخبارُ بأنَّهُ تعالى يجازي المكلفين بما علمه منهم أزلاً مِنْ خير أو شرٍّ ، فأطلق العلم على الجزاء المتأخّرِ عن وقوع أمارتهِ مِنْ خير أو شرّ ؛ لأنَّ وقوع ذلك كله على وَفْقِ علمه جلَّ وعزّ ، وتسمية الجزاء بالعلم مِنْ بابِ تسمية المتعلق باسم المتعلقِ ، وهو مجاز شائع في اللسان .
As for the non-requirement of reflection in knowledge... it is due to agreement that theoretical knowledge may occur necessarily. And when you know the impossibility of acquisition (al-kasb) for His knowledge, may He be exalted, because it indicates precedence of ignorance and attribution of the exalted Essence with a temporal attribute... then you know that what occurs in the Book and the Sunnah whose apparent meaning suggests the temporality of knowledge and its acquisition, it is obligatory to decide that its apparent meaning is not intended. Such as His statement, the Most High: “And We certainly tried those before them, and Allah will surely make known those who are truthful and will surely make known the liars” [Al-Ankabut: 3]. The intended meaning is not that knowledge of the truthful and the liar among His creation became new for Him by the trial. How can that be, when His knowledge, glorified and exalted, is eternal, encompassing every known thing, and according to His eternal knowledge and His penetrating will the judgments of all beings proceed?! “Does He who created not know, while He is the Subtle, the Acquainted” [Al-Mulk: 14]. And the interpretation of the verse is that the intended meaning is the informing that He, the Most High, recompenses the accountable according to what He knew of them eternally of good or evil. So He applied “knowledge” to the recompense that occurs after the occurrence of its indication of good or evil, because all of that occurs in accordance with His knowledge, glorified and exalted. And naming the recompense as knowledge is from the category of naming the object of attribution with the name of the attribution, and it is a common metaphor in speech.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a proof and interpretation. Sanusi begins by stating that theoretical knowledge can occur necessarily (without reflection) by agreement. He then argues that acquisition (kasb) cannot apply to Allah's knowledge, because it would imply prior ignorance and a temporal attribute. Therefore, any apparent textual suggestion of temporality or acquisition of knowledge for Allah must be interpreted figuratively. He gives the example of the verse in Surah Al-Ankabut where it says Allah will make known the truthful and the liars. Sanusi explains that this does not mean Allah gains new knowledge; His knowledge is eternal and encompasses all. Rather, it informs that He will recompense them according to what He has always known. The term 'ilm (knowledge) is used metaphorically for the recompense that follows the manifestation of what was known. This is a common metaphor: naming the effect after the cause.

Key Terms
الكسب (al-kasb)
العلم النظري (theoretical knowledge)
الضروري (necessary knowledge)
المجاز (metaphor)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'فَلَيَعْلَمَنَّ اللَّهُ' implies that Allah gains new knowledge after testing people.
Correction: Sanusi explains that the verse means Allah will recompense the truthful and the liars according to His eternal knowledge, and the term 'knowledge' is used metaphorically for recompense.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the correct interpretation of the phrase 'faya'lamannallah' in Surah Al-Ankabut?
A) Allah gains new knowledge of who is truthful and who is a liar after testing.
B) Allah will recompense the truthful and the liars according to His eternal knowledge.
C) The verse refers to Allah's creation of knowledge in the hearts of believers.
D) It is a metaphorical expression meaning that Allah forgets and remembers.

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge is eternal and all-encompassing; any scriptural suggestion of acquisition must be understood metaphorically, such as the verse about making known the truthful and liars actually referring to recompense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the words 'Allah will surely know' in 29:3, did your heart ever momentarily think that Allah's knowledge increases as you act? This lesson strips that error bare. Will you now actively train yourself to affirm that Allah's knowledge never changes—even when you feel your own actions are 'new'? What practical steps can you take to root out any lingering thought that Allah 'learns' from your deeds?
?
The verse renames recompense as knowledge. Reflect: in your life, do you ever confuse the outward situation (the test, the outcome) with Allah's eternal decree? When you face a trial, do you focus on the trial itself rather than seeing it as a manifestation of what Allah has always known about you? How can you shift your perspective to see trials as mere recompense according to His timeless knowledge, rather than as events that 'inform' Him?
?
Sanusi demands that we cut off any apparent meaning that contradicts Allah's transcendence. Examine your own heart: are there verses or hadith you've read that you've interpreted literally, even though they imply a change in Allah? This lesson calls you to be rigorous. Are you willing to reinterpret them—even if it feels uncomfortable—to uphold Allah's absolute perfection? What is holding you back?

Impossibility of Forgetfulness and Inadvertence in God
Sanusi proves that forgetfulness and inadvertence cannot occur to God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why forgetfulness and inadvertence are impossible for God, based on two rational proofs from the necessity of His knowledge's permanence and His transcendence above ignorance.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness and inadvertence imply ignorance, which God is utterly above.

This passage from Sanusi's creed establishes that forgetfulness and inadvertence cannot occur to God's knowledge. The author provides two clear proofs: first, they entail ignorance, which is impossible for God; second, whatever is forgotten ceases to exist in knowledge, but God's knowledge is eternal. He also clarifies the subtle difference between the two terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once before reading the translation. Let the author's reasoning come through directly.
Notice the two 'because' statements.
2
Identify the two proofs
Look for the two logical arguments: (1) forgetfulness/inadvertence imply ignorance, which is impossible for God; (2) forgetting implies the cessation of knowledge, contradicting its permanence.
3
Understand the distinction between sahw and ghafla
Note that sahw often involves a contradictory belief, while ghafla is more general. The author combined them due to their closeness.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
Consider how these proofs defend God's perfection and the eternal nature of His attributes.
This connects to the broader Islamic theology of tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما ذكرتُ من استحالة طروّ السهو والغفلة على علمه تعالى : فظاهرُ ؛ لأنَّهما يستلزمانِ الاتِّصافَ بالجهل ، وذلك في حقٌّ مَنْ تنزَّهَ عن كل نقيصة محال ، ولأنَّ ما سُهِيَ أو غُفِلَ عنهُ فقد انعدمَ ووجوب البقاء لعلمهِ تعالى ولجميع صفاتِهِ يدفع تجويز ذلكَ ، والسهو والغفلة متقاربان في المعنى ، إلا أنَّ السهو أكثر ما يُستعمَلُ عرفاً في الذهول مع اعتقادِ ما يُضادُّهُ ، والغفلة أعم ، فلهذا جمعت بينهما
As for what I mentioned regarding the impossibility of forgetfulness (sahw) and inadvertence (ghafla) occurring to His knowledge, it is apparent; because they necessitate being described with ignorance, and that is impossible for Him who is transcendent above every deficiency. And because what is forgotten or neglected has become non-existent, and the necessity of permanence (baqā') for His knowledge and all His attributes repels the allowance of that. And forgetfulness and inadvertence are close in meaning, except that forgetfulness is usually customarily used for heedlessness along with belief in what contradicts it, while inadvertence is more general. Therefore I combined both.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage provides two proofs against forgetfulness/inadvertence in God: (1) They imply ignorance, which is impossible for God. (2) Whatever is forgotten ceases to exist in knowledge, but God's knowledge must be eternal and permanent. The author then clarifies the slight difference between the two terms: forgetfulness often involves a contradictory belief, while inadvertence is broader.

God's Power Needs No Instrument
Sanusi provides two proofs for the impossibility of God's power needing an instrument or helper.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments proving that God's power cannot depend on any instrument or helper, and why such dependence would imply temporality.

Core Idea
God's power cannot be contingent on anything else.

This lesson presents a concise theological argument from Imam Sanusi defending divine omnipotence. The author demonstrates that if God's power required an instrument or helper, it would be subject to change and temporality—a contradiction of God's perfection. The reasoning is simple but profound: any need in God's attributes would undermine His necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument before diving into analysis.
The passage is cut off at the end; we work with what is given.
2
Understand the first argument
Notice how dependence on an instrument implies that God would be powerful only when the instrument exists, leading to change and temporality in His power.
3
Understand the second argument
The author extends the reasoning: if one possible thing required an instrument, then all possible things would require it equally, leading to an infinite regress or contradiction.
The argument is incomplete in the source but the logic is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( واستحالَ على قدرتِهِ أنْ تحتاج إلى آلةٍ أو معاونةٍ ) يعني : لأنَّ ذلك يفضي إلى حدوثها ؛ إذ يكون قادراً عند وجودِ تلك الآلة أو المعاون ، وعاجزاً عندَ عدمِهما، ولا يُجاب بادَّعاءِ قِدَمِ الآلِةِ والمُعاونِ ؛ لما عُلِمَ مِنْ وجوب الحدوثِ لكلّ ما سواه تعالى وأيضاً : لو توقف تعلُّقُ قدرتِهِ تعالى بشيءٍ مِنَ الممكنات على واسطة آلة يفعلُ بها ، أو معين يشاركُهُ في الفعلِ .. لَلزِمَ توقُفُ سائِرِ الممكنات على مثل ذلكَ ؛ لوجوبِ استواء الممكناتِ كلها بالنسبة إلى
His saying: 'And it is impossible for His power to need an instrument or helper' means: because that leads to its temporariness (ḥudūth); for He would be powerful when that instrument or helper exists, and powerless when they are absent. And it cannot be answered by claiming the eternity of the instrument and the helper, because it is known that everything other than Him must be temporal. Also: if the attachment of His power to any possible thing were dependent on an intermediary of an instrument by which He acts, or an assistant who participates with Him in the act, then the dependency of all possible things would be necessitated like that, because of the necessity of the equality of all possible things in relation to...
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage provides an argument that God's power cannot require an instrument or helper. If it did, God's power would be contingent on the presence of that instrument, implying temporality. One cannot claim the instrument is eternal because nothing else besides God is eternal. Additionally, if one possible thing required an instrument, then all possible things would require it equally, leading to an infinite regress or contradiction. The argument is cut off but the reasoning is clear.

آلة
معاونة
حدوث
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God could use an eternal instrument to avoid temporality.
Correction: Nothing besides God is eternal; therefore an eternal instrument is impossible.

God's power is absolute and independent; any dependence would imply imperfection and temporality.

Proof that Divine Will Cannot Be for a Purpose
Sanusi proves that God's will cannot be for any purpose, breaking down the proof into two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's will cannot be motivated by any purpose—whether for Himself or for His creation—based on Sanusi's logical proof.

Core Idea
Purpose implies need; the Eternal needs nothing.

This passage tackles a critical aspect of divine transcendence: God's will is free and not driven by any external or internal purpose. Sanusi presents a two-part proof, examining what would follow if a purpose were claimed. The argument protects God's self-sufficiency and eternity, showing that any purpose would either lead to the pre-eternity of the world (a view of the philosophers) or to temporality in God—both impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical structure: a claim, two main branches, and subcases.
2
Identify the two aspects of impossibility
The passage divides the impossibility into two: purpose pertaining to God Himself, and purpose pertaining to creation. Locate these in the text.
3
Analyze the first aspect: purpose for God
Here Sanusi gives two subcases: if the purpose is pre-eternal, and if it is temporal. Understand the consequences of each: pre-eternal purpose would lead to the philosophers' view; temporal purpose would imply deficiency and change in God.
The key is that both subcases are impossible for the Eternal.
4
Analyze the second aspect: purpose for creation
Notice the argument is simpler: God is not obligated to consider benefit or most benefit for creatures. Purpose for creation would imply obligation, which is false.
Sanusi references a fuller proof elsewhere.
5
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider how this argument preserves God's absolute independence and freedom. Why is it important that God's will has no purpose?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وعلى إرادتِهِ أنْ تكونَ لغرض ) يعني : لغرض يبعثه على إيجاد الفعل ، وهو محال في حقه تعالى ، سواء كان الغرض راجعاً إليه ، أو إلى خلقه . أما وجه الاستحالة في الغرضِ الراجع إليهِ : فلأنَّهُ إِنْ كانَ ذلكَ الغرض قديماً وجبَ قدمُ العالم ، ولزمَ الفعلُ بالإيجاب ، وجاءَ مذهب الفلاسفة ، وذلكَ ممَّا قد فرغنا مِنْ إبطالِهِ ، وإنْ كانَ حادثاً يتّصفُ بهِ بعد الإيجادِ لزم نقصُهُ وحاجته قبل إيجادِهِ أفعالَهُ التي حصلَتْ له غرضَهُ ، ولزم اتصافه بالحوادثِ لتجدُّدِ الكمالاتِ له حينئذ بواسطة خلقهِ ، وذلكَ كلُّهُ مفض إلى حدوثِهِ ، ويتعالى عن ذلكَ مَنْ لا أَوَّلَ لوجودِهِ ، الغنيُّ الذي إليهِ يفتقرُ كلُّ شيءٍ ولا يفتقر هو إلى شيء . وأما وجه الاستحالة في الغرضِ الراجع إلى خلقِهِ : فلأنَّه لا يجب عليه تعالى مراعاة صلاح ولا أصلح ، وقد تكلمنا في العقيدة على برهان استحالة الأمرين في فصلِ خلْقِ الأفعال بأتمَّ مِنْ هذا ، في محله شرحاً يزيلُ عنهُ كلَّ غطاءٍ إِنْ شاءَ الله ذلك وسنشرحُ تعالى (١) .
His saying: (And that His will be for a purpose) means: a purpose that impels Him to perform an action, and this is impossible with respect to Him, may He be exalted, whether the purpose pertains to Himself or to His creation. As for the aspect of impossibility in the purpose pertaining to Him: it is because if that purpose is pre-eternal, then the pre-eternity of the world would be necessary, and action would be necessitated by compulsion, and the doctrine of the philosophers would arise, and this is something we have already finished refuting. And if it is temporally originated, and He becomes characterized by it after bringing into existence, then His deficiency and need would be necessary before He brought into existence the actions that attained His purpose for Him, and His being characterized by temporal events would be necessary due to the renewal of perfections for Him then by means of His creation, and all of that leads to His temporality. And exalted above that is He who has no beginning to His existence, the Self-Sufficient, to Whom everything is in need and He is not in need of anything. And as for the aspect of impossibility in the purpose pertaining to His creation: it is because He, may He be exalted, is not obligated to observe what is beneficial or most beneficial. And we have spoken in al-ʿAqīda about the proof of the impossibility of both matters in the chapter on the creation of actions more completely than this, in its place an explanation that removes every cover from it, if God wills. And we will explain, may He be exalted (1).
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the divine will cannot be for a purpose (gharaḍ). The author begins by defining the term: a purpose that impels the agent to act. He states that this is impossible for God, whether the purpose pertains to Himself or to His creation. He then gives two aspects of impossibility. First, if the purpose pertains to God Himself, two possibilities arise: if the purpose is pre-eternal (qadīm), then the world would also be pre-eternal (since the purpose must have been eternally motivating God to create), leading to the philosophers' view of necessary emanation, which has already been refuted. If the purpose is temporal (ḥādith), then God would have been deficient before acquiring it through His actions, implying need and change, which leads to His temporality—impossible for the Eternal. Second, if the purpose pertains to creation, God is not obligated to consider what is beneficial or most beneficial for creatures. The author notes that he has already given a more complete proof in his work al-ʿAqīda in the chapter on the creation of actions, and promises further explanation. The argument preserves divine transcendence (tanzīh) by denying any compulsion or need.

Key Terms
غرض
إرادة
قديم
حادث
صلاح
أصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God acts for the benefit of creation, e.g., to be merciful or to test humans.
Correction: God is not obligated to consider benefit. A purpose would imply need, which contradicts His self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the consequence if God's purpose in creating were pre-eternal?
A) The world would be temporally originated.
B) The world would be pre-eternal.
C) God would be temporal.
D) Nothing changes.

Key Takeaway
God's will is free of any purpose; attributing a purpose to God implies need and contradiction with His eternal self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Divine Attributes Without Limbs, Facing, or Connection
Sanusi negates limbs, facing, and connection for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's attributes of hearing, sight, speech, and perception are not carried out through physical limbs, nor does vision require facing an object, nor does perception require physical connection.

Core Idea
God's attributes transcend all physical limitations.

In this passage, Sanusi continues to defend divine transcendence by negating any physical association with God's attributes. He emphasizes that God hears, sees, speaks, and perceives without any bodily organs, and further clarifies that vision does not require facing and perception does not require connection. This lesson examines these negations and their significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Negations
Read the passage and find the three things that are negated: limbs, facing, and connection. Notice how each is tied to a specific attribute.
Limbs are negated for all mentioned attributes; facing for vision; connection for perception.
2
Understand the Proof Against Corporeality
Sanusi reminds that the impossibility of corporeality for God has already been proven. This is the basis for negating limbs.
3
Reflect on the Meaning of Facing and Connection
Consider why vision does not require facing (the object need not be in front of God) and perception does not require physical contact. This reinforces God's transcendence.
These negations safeguard against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وعلى سمعه وبصره وكلامه وإدراكه على القولِ بهِ أَنْ تكون بجارحةٍ ) هذا راجع إلى الجميع ، وقد قدمنا البرهان على استحالة الجرمية في حقه تعالى ، فهو يسمع بغيرِ أُذن ولا صماخ ، ويرى بغير حدقةٍ ، ويتكلم بغير فم ولا لسان ، ويدركُ على القولِ بزيادة الإدراك بغير الآلاتِ المعتادة للشم والذوق واللمس . قوله : ( أو مقابلة ) راجع إلى الرؤية . قوله : ( أو اتصال ) راجع إلى الإدراك عندَ مَنْ أثبتَهُ . قوله : ( أو يكون كلامه حرفاً أو صوتاً ) لأنَّه لو كان كلامه يتركَّبُ مِنَ الحروفِ والأصواتِ لكانَ ذلكَ الكلام حادثاً ؛ ضرورة استحالة اجتماع حرفين فأكثر في محلّ واحدٍ ، فلا تُوجَدُ الحروفُ في محلّ واحدٍ حتى ينعدم سابقها ويتجدَّدَ لاحقها ، وكلُّ ما سبق وجودَهُ العدمُ أو طراً على وجودِهِ العدمُ .. فهو حادث ، فالحروفُ والأصواتُ لا تكون أبداً إلا حادثة ، فلو تركَّبَ الكلام منها لكانَ حادثاً ؛ ضرورةً أنَّ المركَّبَ مِنَ الحوادثِ حادث.
His saying: (And that His hearing, sight, speech, and perception, according to the view that affirms it, be [not] through a limb) — this refers to all of them. And we have already presented the proof of the impossibility of corporeality with respect to Him, may He be exalted. So He hears without an ear or ear canal, and sees without a pupil, and speaks without a mouth or tongue, and perceives, according to the view that perception is additional, without the usual instruments for smell, taste, and touch. His saying: (or [not] through facing) refers to vision. His saying: (or [not] through connection) refers to perception for those who affirm it. His saying: (or that His speech be a letter or a sound) — because if His speech were composed of letters and sounds, that speech would be temporally originated, due to the impossibility of two or more letters coexisting in a single locus. So letters are not found in a single locus without their predecessor ceasing and their successor arising, and everything whose existence is preceded by non-existence or on whose existence non-existence supervenes is temporally originated. So letters and sounds are never anything but temporally originated. So if speech were composed of them, it would be temporally originated, necessarily because the composite of temporal things is temporal.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a series of negations regarding the divine attributes of hearing, sight, speech, and perception. The author begins by stating that these attributes are not through a limb (jāriha), meaning no bodily organ. He reminds that the impossibility of corporeality (jirmiyya) has already been proven. Therefore, God hears without an ear or ear canal, sees without a pupil, speaks without mouth or tongue, and perceives (for those who affirm perception as an additional attribute) without the usual instruments for smell, taste, and touch. Then he explains two more negations: 'not through facing' (muqābala) specifically negates that vision requires facing an object; 'not through connection' (ittiṣāl) negates that perception requires physical contact. Finally, he addresses speech: if God's speech were composed of letters and sounds, it would be temporally originated, because letters and sounds are temporal by nature—they succeed each other and cannot coexist in one locus. Since the composite of temporal things is temporal, God's speech cannot be composed of letters and sounds. This preserves divine transcendence.

Key Terms
جارحة
صماخ
حدقة
مقابلة
اتصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God sees like humans, requiring an object to be in front of Him.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that vision does not require facing (muqābala). God's vision is not limited by direction or location.

Checkpoint
What does 'not through facing' (muqābala) refer to?
A) Hearing
B) Speech
C) Vision
D) Perception

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are not dependent on physical organs, spatial direction, or material contact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you subconsciously imagine God hearing your voice as if with ears? How can you train your heart to affirm that His hearing is without any organ, and what difference would that make in your awe of Him?
?
If God sees you without eyes and without needing to face you, does that make you feel more accountable or more distant? Will you live today knowing that His sight is perfect and immediate, yet entirely unlike any created sight?
?
God speaks without mouth or tongue—is His word, the Qur'an, therefore more real or less real to you? How does this truth deepen your reverence for every verse you recite?

Divine Speech Is Not Composed of Letters or Sounds
Sanusi proves divine speech is not composed of letters or sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that God's speech cannot be composed of letters or sounds because that would make it temporal, while God's speech is eternal.

Core Idea
If God's speech were letters and sounds, it would be created.

The final negation in this passage addresses the nature of divine speech. Sanusi provides a concise logical proof that God's speech cannot consist of letters or sounds, because such elements are temporally originated. This lesson unpacks that argument, showing why the composite of temporal things must itself be temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Argument About Speech
Read the passage carefully. Sanusi states that if speech were composed of letters/sounds, it would be temporal. Follow the reasoning step by step.
2
Understand Why Letters Are Temporal
Letters cannot coexist in one locus; they must succeed each other. Anything preceded by non-existence is temporal. So letters are temporal.
This is a key premise.
3
Trace the Conclusion
Since the composite of temporal things is temporal, divine speech (which is eternal) cannot be composed of letters or sounds.
This preserves God's speech as an eternal attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وعلى سمعه وبصره وكلامه وإدراكه على القولِ بهِ أَنْ تكون بجارحةٍ ) هذا راجع إلى الجميع ، وقد قدمنا البرهان على استحالة الجرمية في حقه تعالى ، فهو يسمع بغيرِ أُذن ولا صماخ ، ويرى بغير حدقةٍ ، ويتكلم بغير فم ولا لسان ، ويدركُ على القولِ بزيادة الإدراك بغير الآلاتِ المعتادة للشم والذوق واللمس . قوله : ( أو مقابلة ) راجع إلى الرؤية . قوله : ( أو اتصال ) راجع إلى الإدراك عندَ مَنْ أثبتَهُ . قوله : ( أو يكون كلامه حرفاً أو صوتاً ) لأنَّه لو كان كلامه يتركَّبُ مِنَ الحروفِ والأصواتِ لكانَ ذلكَ الكلام حادثاً ؛ ضرورة استحالة اجتماع حرفين فأكثر في محلّ واحدٍ ، فلا تُوجَدُ الحروفُ في محلّ واحدٍ حتى ينعدم سابقها ويتجدَّدَ لاحقها ، وكلُّ ما سبق وجودَهُ العدمُ أو طراً على وجودِهِ العدمُ .. فهو حادث ، فالحروفُ والأصواتُ لا تكون أبداً إلا حادثة ، فلو تركَّبَ الكلام منها لكانَ حادثاً ؛ ضرورةً أنَّ المركَّبَ مِنَ الحوادثِ حادث.
His saying: (And that His hearing, sight, speech, and perception, according to the view that affirms it, be [not] through a limb) — this refers to all of them. And we have already presented the proof of the impossibility of corporeality with respect to Him, may He be exalted. So He hears without an ear or ear canal, and sees without a pupil, and speaks without a mouth or tongue, and perceives, according to the view that perception is additional, without the usual instruments for smell, taste, and touch. His saying: (or [not] through facing) refers to vision. His saying: (or [not] through connection) refers to perception for those who affirm it. His saying: (or that His speech be a letter or a sound) — because if His speech were composed of letters and sounds, that speech would be temporally originated, due to the impossibility of two or more letters coexisting in a single locus. So letters are not found in a single locus without their predecessor ceasing and their successor arising, and everything whose existence is preceded by non-existence or on whose existence non-existence supervenes is temporally originated. So letters and sounds are never anything but temporally originated. So if speech were composed of them, it would be temporally originated, necessarily because the composite of temporal things is temporal.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a series of negations regarding the divine attributes of hearing, sight, speech, and perception. The author begins by stating that these attributes are not through a limb (jāriha), meaning no bodily organ. He reminds that the impossibility of corporeality (jirmiyya) has already been proven. Therefore, God hears without an ear or ear canal, sees without a pupil, speaks without mouth or tongue, and perceives (for those who affirm perception as an additional attribute) without the usual instruments for smell, taste, and touch. Then he explains two more negations: 'not through facing' (muqābala) specifically negates that vision requires facing an object; 'not through connection' (ittiṣāl) negates that perception requires physical contact. Finally, he addresses speech: if God's speech were composed of letters and sounds, it would be temporally originated, because letters and sounds are temporal by nature—they succeed each other and cannot coexist in one locus. Since the composite of temporal things is temporal, God's speech cannot be composed of letters and sounds. This preserves divine transcendence.

حرف
صوت
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think God's speech is audible like human speech, consisting of sounds and letters.
Correction: This passage proves that God's speech cannot be composed of letters or sounds, as they are temporal; divine speech is eternal.

Checkpoint
Why cannot God's speech be composed of letters and sounds?
A) Because letters and sounds are eternal.
B) Because letters and sounds are temporal and composite of temporal things is temporal.
C) Because God does not speak.
D) Because letters are material.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is eternal and not composed of temporal elements like letters and sounds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Quran is the uncreated speech of Allah. When you recite it, do you fully internalize that you are uttering something eternal, not a temporal composition? How will this truth transform your reverence and focus during recitation starting today?
?
If God's speech is beyond letters and sounds, then every time you read or hear the Quran, you are encountering a reality that cannot be fully captured by human language. Will you commit to seeking deeper meanings rather than being satisfied with mere sounds? What practical step will you take this week to deepen your reflection on a verse?
?
This lesson proves that describing God's speech as letters and sounds would reduce it to a created thing. Are there areas in your understanding of Allah's attributes where you unconsciously liken them to created qualities? What will you do to purify your tawhid and affirm Allah's transcendence in your daily thoughts and worship?

Hashwiyya's Anthropomorphism and Condemnation
Sanusi condemns the Hashwiyya and describes their anthropomorphic belief in God as a body that moves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hashwiyya's extreme anthropomorphic beliefs about God's body and movement and why Sanusi condemns them as ignoring rational necessities.

Core Idea
The Hashwiyya contradict necessary rational truths by attributing a body and movement to Allah.

Sanusi begins by stating the Hashwiyya's claim that God's speech is eternal letters and sounds, then immediately denounces them as deeply misguided. He explains that unlike other heretics, the Hashwiyya disregard the basic necessities of reason by asserting that God is a body that sits, moves, and descends. This lesson covers that initial condemnation and their physical description of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the Hashwiyya's core claim
Read the passage and note their assertion about God's speech being eternal letters and sounds.
This claim is the starting point of Sanusi's refutation.
2
Notice Sanusi's condemnation and comparison
See how Sanusi contrasts the Hashwiyya with other heretics, emphasizing their disregard for rational necessities.
3
Identify physical attributes ascribed to Allah
Find the specific physical descriptions: body, settling on the Throne with contact, moving and descending every Friday.
These attributes violate the principle of divine transcendence.
4
Reflect on rational necessities
Consider why these attributes contradict necessary truths of reason, such as that God is not a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت الحشوية المنتمون إلى الظاهر : إلى أن كلام الباري تعالى القائم بذاته حروف وأصوات ، ومع كونه حروفا وأصواتا هو قديم أزلي ! وهؤلاء أصحاب غاية في الضلالة ، وتورط في بحبوحة الجهالة ، فإن من سواهم من أهل البدع ربما تعن لهم شبهة مخيلة لا تهدِم من أول مرة الضرورات ، أما هؤلاء فلم يراعوا ضرورات العقول ، ولا وقفوا من أول مرة عند شيء منها ، نعوذ بالله من الخذلان ، فاعتقادهم : أن الباري تعالى جسم ، مستو على العرش بالمماسة والاستقرار ، ثم ينتقل كل ليلة جمعة عند ما يبقى ثلث الليل وينزل عن مكانه إلى السماء ، ثم يعود عند الفجر إلى مكانه وهم على صنفين : صنف منهم قالوا بتحيزه وتصويره وتشكيله على شكل الإنسان ، وهؤلاء مساوون لليهود في هذا الاعتقاد وصنف آخر منهم قالوا بتحيزه من غير شكل ولا جارحة ، ثم اتفقوا على أن كلامه سبحانه قديم ، حروف وأصوات متقطعة يتكلم بما شاء منها باللسان العربي والعجمي وضروب الألسنة الموضوعة لأهل الأرض ، فيقولون : إنه ينطق بالباء والميم وسائر الحروف لا على مخارج الحروف ، والذين قالوا بأنه على شكل الإنسان قالوا : إنه يتكلم بالحروف على مخارجها ، وجملتها قديمة وجملتها قديمة ، وهو ينظمها كيف يشاء وعلى أي لغة يشاء ! وكيف تدخل المشيئة للقديم لولا أن الله يسلب عقل التمييز ممن يشاء ؟! وهو عندهم يتكلم إذا يشاء ويسكت إذا يشاء ، فإذا سكت لم
And the Hashwiyya who affiliate themselves with the literalists went to the extent that the speech of the Exalted Creator subsisting in His essence is letters and sounds, and despite it being letters and sounds it is eternal and beginningless! These are people of extreme misguidance, and they are plunged into the depths of ignorance. For indeed, others among the people of innovation may sometimes have an illusory doubt presented to them that does not demolish the necessities from the first time. But these did not observe the necessities of intellects, nor did they stop at any of them from the first time. We seek refuge with Allah from abandonment. So their belief is that the Exalted Creator is a body, settled on the Throne by contact and stability, then He moves every Friday night when a third of the night remains, and descends from His place to the heaven, then returns at dawn to His place. And they are of two kinds: a group among them said He is located in direction, pictured, and formed in the shape of a human; these are equal to the Jews in this belief. And another group among them said He is located in direction without shape or limb. Then they agreed that His speech—glorified is He—is eternal, letters and discontinuous sounds; He speaks whatever He wills of them in Arabic, Persian, and the various languages assigned to the people of the earth. They say: He pronounces the bā', mīm, and the rest of the letters not from the points of articulation. And those who said He is in the shape of a human said: He speaks the letters from their points of articulation, and their entirety is eternal, and their entirety is eternal, and He arranges them however He wills and in whatever language He wills! How can volition enter upon the eternal, except that Allah would deprive the intellect of discrimination from whom He wills?! And according to them, He speaks when He wills and is silent when He wills. So if He were silent, He did not...
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Hashwiyya sect's beliefs about God's attributes, especially His speech. The author begins by stating the Hashwiyya's claim that God's speech, which subsists in His essence, is composed of letters and sounds, yet it is eternal and beginningless. He immediately denounces this as extreme misguidance and deep ignorance. He then contrasts them with other heretical sects who might have deceptive doubts but at least do not immediately contradict necessary rational principles, whereas the Hashwiyya disregard the necessities of the intellect entirely. He then describes their specific beliefs: they believe God is a body, sitting on the Throne in contact and stability, moving and descending every Friday. He divides them into two groups: one group holds that God has a human form, likening them to the Jews; the other group asserts that God is located in a direction but without shape or limbs. Both groups agree that God's speech is eternal and consists of letters and sounds which God speaks in various languages. The first group says He speaks without using the points of articulation, while the second group (who believe He has a human form) says He speaks through those points. The author then points out the absurdity of attributing volition to an eternal being, since volition implies temporal events, and questions how God could will to speak or be silent if His speech is eternal. The passage ends with a rhetorical question exposing the contradiction in their belief.

الحشوية
جسم
مستو على العرش
ضرورات العقول

A) They believe God's speech is eternal.
B) They disregard the necessities of reason from the outset.
C) They deny God's attributes.
D) They are ignorant of the Arabic language.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi condemns the Hashwiyya for rejecting rational necessities by claiming God is a body that moves and descends.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even unintentionally, imagined Allah as having a body or moving from place to place? How does this passage force you to purify your concept of Allah? What specific change will you make in your mental image of your Creator starting now?
?
The Hashwiyya rejected the 'necessities of the intellect' because they clung to a literal reading of ambiguous texts. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that you have not subjected to rational scrutiny for fear of contradicting a literal text? Ask yourself: Is my understanding of Allah's attributes consistent with the principle that He is unlike anything created?
?
If you were to take a single verse or hadith about Allah's descending or sitting on the Throne and interpret it without considering the clear rational proofs, would you fall into the same error as the Hashwiyya? What safeguards will you put in place to ensure you always interpret such texts in a way that upholds tanzih (transcendence)?

Two Subgroups and Their Belief on Speech
Sanusi presents the two subgroups of the Hashwiyya and their common belief in eternal letters and sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two subgroups within the Hashwiyya regarding God's form and their shared belief that God's speech consists of eternal letters and sounds.

Core Idea
The Hashwiyya split on whether God has human form, but agree that His speech is eternal letters spoken in various languages.

After describing the Hashwiyya's general anthropomorphism, Sanusi divides them into two groups: one that asserts God has a human shape, and another that says He is located in a direction without form. Despite this difference, they both agree that God's speech is eternal and composed of letters and sounds, spoken in different languages.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two subgroups
Find the description of the first group (human form) and the second group (direction without form).
2
Note the comparison to Jews
Sanusi says the first group equals the Jews in belief. Reflect on why.
3
Observe the common ground on speech
Both groups agree that God's speech is eternal letters and sounds, spoken in various languages.
4
Consider the difference in articulation
The first group says He speaks with articulation points; the second says without.

Absurdity of Volition in Eternal Speech
Sanusi critiques the Hashwiyya's attribution of volition and silence to eternal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi exposes the logical contradiction in the Hashwiyya's claim that God's eternal speech can be subject to His will, silence, and arrangement.

Core Idea
Attributing volition and silence to an eternal being's speech is rationally impossible.

Sanusi continues his refutation by pointing out the incoherence of the Hashwiyya's position. If God's speech is eternal, how can He will to speak or be silent? How can He arrange eternal letters in different languages? These questions reveal the absurdity of their claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two claims
First, the letters are eternal. Second, God arranges them by His will and speaks in various languages.
2
Reflect on Sanusi's rhetorical question
He asks how volition can enter upon the eternal. Volition implies change, but eternal is changeless.
This is the core logical problem.
3
Consider the claim of silence
They say God speaks and is silent at will. But if speech is eternal, silence would mean cessation of the eternal.
4
Conclude the contradiction
The Hashwiyya's beliefs are internally contradictory, showing their lack of rationality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

These are people of extreme misguidance, and they are plunged into the depths of ignorance. For indeed, others among the people of innovation may sometimes have an illusory doubt presented to them that does not demolish the necessities from the first time. And they are of two kinds: a group among them said He is located in direction, pictured, and formed in the shape of a human; these are equal to the Jews in this belief. And another group among them said He is located in direction without shape or limb. Then they agreed that His speech—glorified is He—is eternal, letters and discontinuous sounds; He speaks whatever He wills of them in Arabic, Persian, and the various languages assigned to the people of the earth. They say: He pronounces the bā', mīm, and the rest of the letters not from the points of articulation. And those who said He is in the shape of a human said: He speaks the letters from their points of articulation, and their entirety is eternal, and their entirety is eternal, and He arranges them however He wills and in whatever language He wills! How can volition enter upon the eternal, except that Allah would deprive the intellect of discrimination from whom He wills?! And according to them, He speaks when He wills and is silent when He wills. So if He were silent, He did not...
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Hashwiyya sect's beliefs about God's attributes, especially His speech. The author begins by stating the Hashwiyya's claim that God's speech, which subsists in His essence, is composed of letters and sounds, yet it is eternal and beginningless. He immediately denounces this as extreme misguidance and deep ignorance. He then contrasts them with other heretical sects who might have deceptive doubts but at least do not immediately contradict necessary rational principles, whereas the Hashwiyya disregard the necessities of the intellect entirely. He then describes their specific beliefs: they believe God is a body, sitting on the Throne in contact and stability, moving and descending every Friday. He divides them into two groups: one group holds that God has a human form, likening them to the Jews; the other group asserts that God is located in a direction but without shape or limbs. Both groups agree that God's speech is eternal and consists of letters and sounds which God speaks in various languages. The first group says He speaks without using the points of articulation, while the second group (who believe He has a human form) says He speaks through those points. The author then points out the absurdity of attributing volition to an eternal being, since volition implies temporal events, and questions how God could will to speak or be silent if His speech is eternal. The passage ends with a rhetorical question exposing the contradiction in their belief.

Key Terms
مشيئة
قديم
سكوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What logical problem does Sanusi raise against the Hashwiyya's belief about God's speech?
A) That God's speech is in multiple languages.
B) That volition and silence cannot be attributed to something eternal.
C) That God's speech is heard by humans.
D) That letters are created.

Key Takeaway
The Hashwiyya's belief that God's eternal speech is subject to will and silence is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought of Allah's attributes as similar to yours—changing with mood or circumstance? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what must you do to correct it?
?
Sanusi warns that Allah 'deprives the intellect of discrimination from whom He wills.' In what area of your life or belief have you accepted a contradiction without using your intellect? Will you seek clarity today?
?
If someone asked you, 'Can God stop speaking?' how would you answer based on this lesson? Does your answer change how you revere His speech?
