The Anthropomorphists' Belief about God's Body
Sanusi describes the mujassimah's belief in a corporeal God and divides them into two groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the anthropomorphist belief that God is a body and the two subgroups within them.

Core Idea
Some anthropomorphists gave God a human form; others only spatial occupation.

Sanusi begins by describing the shocking beliefs of the mujassimah, who claimed that Allah is a body in contact with the Throne and physically moves. He then shows that they split into two groups: one that fully anthropomorphizes and another that only asserts finitude without form. This lesson explores these two positions and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the strong opening phrase seeking refuge.
2
Identify the two groups
The passage explicitly says they are of two kinds. Find the description of each group.
3
Reflect on the comparison to Jews
Sanusi says one group is equal to the Jews. Consider why this comparison is significant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعوذ بالله من الخذلان، فاعتقادهم: أن البارئ تعالى جسم، مستو على العرش بالمماسة والاستقرار، ثم ينتقل كل ليلة جمعة عند ما يبقى ثلث الليل وينزل عن مكانه إلى السماء، ثم يعود عند الفجر إلى مكانه. وهم على صنفين: صنف منهم قالوا بتحيزه وتصويره وتشكيله على شكل الإنسان، وهؤلاء مساوون لليهود في هذا الاعتقاد. وصنف آخر منهم قالوا بتحيزه من غير شكل ولا جارحة. ثم اتفقوا على أن كلامه سبحانه قديم، حروف وأصوات متقطعة يتكلم بما شاء منها باللسان العربي والعجمي وضروب الألسنة الموضوعة لأهل الأرض. فيقولون: إنه ينطق بالباء والميم وسائر الحروف لا على مخارج الحروف. والذين قالوا بأنه على شكل الإنسان قالوا: إنه يتكلم بالحروف على مخارجها، وجملتها قديمة، وهو ينظمها كيف يشاء وعلى أي لغة يشاء! وكيف تدخل المشيئة للقديم لولا أن الله يسلب عقل التمييز ممن يشاء؟! وهو عندهم يتكلم إذا يشاء ويسكت إذا يشاء. فإذا سكت لم ينعدم كلامه، ولكنه صمت وأكنه! تعالى الله عن قولهم.
We seek refuge with Allah from abandonment. Their belief: that the Creator, exalted, is a body, established on the Throne by contact and settling; then He moves every Friday night when one third of the night remains and descends from His place to the heaven, then returns at dawn to His place. They are of two kinds: a kind among them said He is finite, shaped, and formed in the shape of a human; these are equal to the Jews in this belief. And another kind among them said He is finite without shape or limb. Then they agreed that His speech, glorified be He, is eternal, [consisting of] discrete letters and sounds; He speaks whatever He wills of them in the Arabic tongue, the non-Arabic, and the various languages placed for the people of the earth. So they say: He pronounces the bā', the mīm, and the rest of the letters not from the points of articulation of the letters. And those who said He is in the form of a human said: He speaks letters from their points of articulation, and their totality is eternal, and He arranges them as He wills and in whatever language He wills! And how can volition enter upon the eternal unless Allah strips the discerning intellect from whom He wills?! And in their view He speaks when He wills and is silent when He wills. So if He is silent, His speech does not cease, rather He is silent and conceals it! Exalted is Allah above their statements.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is a descriptive and polemical account of the creed of the anthropomorphists (mujassimah). Sanusi begins by stating the core belief: that God is a body in contact with the Throne, and physically moves on Fridays. He then divides them into two groups: one that attributes to God full human form (like the Jews), and another that affirms spatial occupation without shape or limbs. Despite their disagreements, both groups agree that God's speech is eternal and composed of discrete letters and sounds. Sanusi points out the absurdity: if speech is eternal, how can it be subject to God's will in choosing languages? He further notes that in their view, God can be silent without losing His speech—a contradiction. Sanusi concludes by exclaiming that God is far above their statements.

Correction: Sanusi shows there are two groups: one that attributes human form, and another that only affirms spatial occupation without form.

Key Takeaway

The Anthropomorphists' Belief about God's Speech
Sanusi critiques the anthropomorphist doctrine of eternal speech composed of letters and sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the anthropomorphists conceive of God's speech as eternal letters and sounds, and the internal contradictions in their view.

Core Idea
They claimed God's speech is eternal yet composed of discrete sounds arranged by will.

After describing the anthropomorphists' view of God's body, Sanusi turns to their doctrine of divine speech. Both groups agreed that God's speech is eternal and consists of letters and sounds. However, they disagreed on articulation, and Sanusi highlights several absurdities: if the speech is eternal, how can it be subject to divine will? And if God can be silent, does His speech cease? This lesson unpacks these problematic claims.

3

Refuting the Claim that the Recited Qur'an is God's Uncreated Speech
Sanusi refutes the claim that the recited Qur'an is the uncreated speech of God dwelling in the reciter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi considers the anthropomorphist claim about the recited Qur'an to be repugnant and how he analogizes it to Christian incarnation.

Core Idea
Claiming the recited Qur'an is God's speech in the reciter leads to a heresy similar to Christian incarnation.

Sanusi continues his refutation of anthropomorphist doctrines by addressing a particularly extreme claim: that when a person recites the Qur'an, the sound produced is the very uncreated speech of God, dwelling in the reciter. This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum by comparing this view to the Christian belief in the incarnation of the divine attribute of knowledge in Jesus. The difference, Sanusi notes, is that Christians restricted this to one person, while the anthropomorphists apply it to every reciter.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the strong opening word 'شنيع' (repugnant).
2
Identify the two claims
The anthropomorphists make two claims: (1) the recited sound is God's speech dwelling in the reciter, and (2) the letters of the mushaf are God's speech itself. Find these in the text.
Both claims are presented as absurdities.
3
Understand the analogy to Christianity
Sanusi compares these claims to the Christian belief in the incarnation of the eternal attribute of knowledge in Jesus. Note the crucial difference: Christians limit this to one person, while the anthropomorphists apply it to every reciter.
This analogy shows the extreme nature of the anthropomorphist view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن شنيع مذهبهم: أن القارئ إذا قرأ من كتاب الله تعالى آية.. فالذي يسمع منه هو الكلام القائم بالله سبحانه، وقد وجد في محل هذا القاري ولم ينتقل عن ذات الإله! وزعموا أن حروف المصحف عين كلام الله تعالى من غير أن ينتقل أيضا عن ذاته! وهذا قول النصارى بتدرع عيسى بالصفة الأزلية التي هي العلم من غير أن تفارق الإله، ولكن النصارى خصصوا بذلك واحدا من الخلق، وهو عيسى عليه السلام، وهؤلاء حكموا بذلك في حق كل قارئ يتلو آية من آيات الله!
And among the repugnant [aspects] of their doctrine: that when the reciter recites a verse from the Book of Allah, what is heard from him is the speech subsisting in Allah, and it has come to exist in the place of this reciter without having transferred from the essence of the God! And they claimed that the letters of the mushaf (written copy) are the very speech of Allah, without it having transferred from His essence either! This is the statement of the Christians concerning the incarnation of Jesus with the eternal attribute that is knowledge, without it separating from the God; but the Christians restricted that to one of creation, namely Jesus, peace be upon him, while these [anthropomorphists] have ruled that regarding every reciter who recites a verse from the verses of Allah!
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage presents a specific absurdity (شنيع) of the anthropomorphist doctrine: the claim that the sound produced by a reciter is the very uncreated speech of God, dwelling in the reciter without leaving God. Sanusi analogizes this to the Christian belief that the eternal attribute of knowledge incarnated in Jesus, but he notes a crucial difference: Christians confined this to a single person (Jesus), whereas the anthropomorphists apply it to every Quran reciter. This is a reductio ad absurdum, showing that such a view leads to the heretical conclusion that every reciter is a kind of incarnation of God's speech.

النصارى

Misconception: The sound of a person reciting the Qur'an is the uncreated speech of God itself, dwelling in the reciter.
Correction: Sanusi refutes this by comparing it to Christian incarnation, showing it leads to attributing divinity to every reciter.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the anthropomorphist claim that the recited Qur'an is God's speech dwelling in the reciter by equating it to the Christian doctrine of incarnation, but notes the former is even more extreme.

The Rational Impossibility of Eternal Letters
Sanusi demonstrates the logical impossibility of letters being both eternal and originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi deems the doctrine of eternal letters as rationally impossible and how it contradicts necessary truths.

Core Idea
If letters are eternal, they cannot be new; if new, they cannot be eternal.

Sanusi now addresses a further absurdity from those who claim that the Qur'anic letters are eternal yet can be newly written. He exposes the logical contradiction in this claim and shows how it leads to even more extreme positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Sanusi's argument against the claim of eternal letters.
Notice the strong language: 'exit from the realm of reason'.
2
Identify the core contradiction
Sanusi points out that claiming letters are eternal while non-existence precedes and follows them is self-contradictory.
Eternal means no beginning; non-existence preceding implies a beginning.
3
Analyze the iron ingot example
Sanusi gives an example: iron ingots are originated, so how can letters formed from them become eternal? Trace the logical steps.
This illustrates the absurdity of something changing from originated to eternal.
4
Reflect on the extreme view
Sanusi reports that some claim written letters are God Himself. Consider why this is taken as a literal absurdity.
This would imply multiple gods in multiple places.
5
Connect to rational necessity
See how this argument reinforces the necessity of distinguishing between creator and creation.
The doctrine violates the principle of non-contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحكم بقدم حروف وأصوات تتجدد والعدم سابق لها ولاحق، وكون الشيء الواحد يحل محلين.. خروج من دائرة العقل، وجحد من الضرورات، وكيف يوسم بالعقل من يقول: إن الحروف إذا صيغت من زبر الحديد حتى يفهم منها آيات من كتاب الله تعالى.. فهي بأعيانها عين كلام الله تعالى، وكانت إذ كانت زبرا حادثة، فلما صارت حروفا انقلبت قديمة! وأطلقت طائفة منهم القول بأن المكتوبة الدالة على اسم الله سبحانه هي الله المعبود بحق، وإن كتبت في أماكن فهو واحد في أماكن!
And the judgment that letters and sounds are eternal while non-existence precedes them and follows them, and that one thing can be present in two places — [this is] an exit from the realm of reason and a denial of necessities. And how can one be characterized by intellect who says: 'When letters are fashioned from iron ingots such that by them verses from the Book of Allah are understood, then they are in their very essence the speech of Allah, and they were, when they were ingots, originated; so when they became letters they turned into eternal!' And a group among them explicitly stated that the written [object] that indicates the name of Allah is Allah Himself worshipped rightfully, and if it is written in places, He is one in places!
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
In this passage, Sanusi presents further logical absurdities of the anthropomorphist doctrine. First, he points out the contradiction in claiming that letters and sounds are eternal while also being subject to temporal origination (their non-existence both precedes and follows them). Second, he notes the impossibility of one thing being in two places simultaneously. Then he gives a concrete example: someone claims that letters made from iron ingots (which are originated) become eternal when formed into words. Sanusi finds this so irrational that he questions how such a person can be considered rational. Finally, he reports an extreme view that the written letters themselves (e.g., the word 'Allah') are identical to God, which is a form of idolatry.

Key Terms
قدم حروف وأصوات
العدم سابق ولاحق
زبر الحديد
أعيانها
المكتوبة الدالة على اسم الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the claim 'the written letters are God' is merely a metaphorical exaggeration.
Correction: Sanusi treats this claim as a literal statement that violates reason and the oneness of God.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi say about the claim that letters made from iron become eternal once they form words?
A) It is a plausible argument
B) It is a well-known proof
C) It is an exit from the realm of reason
D) It is accepted by many scholars

Key Takeaway
Sanusi demonstrates that the doctrine of eternal letters violates rational necessity and leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi says that holding contradictory beliefs about God is 'an exit from the realm of reason.' Where in your own life do you find it tempting to believe something about God that does not hold up to rational scrutiny? What would it cost you to bring that belief into line with reason?
?
If you were to apply Sanusi's method of exposing absurdity to your own understanding of God's attributes, what would you find? Are there any beliefs you hold that, if examined closely, lead to contradictions? Will you examine them today?
?
The anthropomorphists allowed their reverence for the Qur'an to lead them into an irrational claim. In what ways might your love for certain religious practices or figures cause you to abandon reason? What step will you take to ensure your intellect remains submitted to truth, not sentiment?

First Proof: Command Without Will in Commanding Disbelievers
Sanusi presents the first proof that command is distinct from will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of commanding disbelievers demonstrates that God's command is distinct from His will.

Core Idea
God's command does not always imply His will; the disbelievers are commanded but not willed to believe.

In this lesson, we examine the first proof presented by Sanusi to show that the divine attributes of command (amr) and will (irada) are distinct. Using the case of commanding disbelievers to believe, he argues that if command entailed will, disbelief would not persist. This argument also relies on a logical premise and the consensus of the early Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if He had willed, it would have occurred'.
2
Identify the key components
Find the command to disbelievers, the conditional premise, the conclusion that God did not will their belief, and the support from Salaf consensus.
3
Understand the logical structure
Trace the logic: God commanded → belief did not occur → therefore God did not will it. If He had willed it, it would have occurred. Otherwise, a deficiency would arise.
The argument relies on the principle that God's will is always effective.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this matters: it shows that God's attributes are distinct and does not diminish His power or sovereignty.
This prevents misunderstanding that God's command and will are identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأوّلُ : أَنَّ الله تعالى أمر الكفَّارَ بالإيمان والعصاة بالطاعة ، ولم يُرِدْ منهم ذلك ؛ إذ لو أرادَ ذلكَ لوقعَ ، وإلا لزمَ النقصُ بنفوذ مشيئة العبدِ بدونِ مشيئة الله تعالى ، وقدِ اتَّفق السلفُ قبل ظهور البدع على أنَّ ما شاءَ اللهُ كانَ ، وما لم يشأ لم يكن .
The first: that Allah Most High commanded the disbelievers to believe and the sinners to obey, but He did not will that from them; because if He had willed that, it would have occurred, otherwise deficiency would follow by the penetration of the servant’s will without the will of Allah Most High, and the Salaf agreed before the emergence of innovations that what Allah wills is, and what He does not will is not.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that God's command (amr) is distinct from His will (irada). The proof uses the example of God commanding disbelievers to believe and sinners to obey, yet those commanded do not actually comply. If the command entailed will, then the commanded act would necessarily occur, because God's will is effective. Since it does not occur, the command cannot be identical to will. The argument includes a premise: if God had willed their compliance, it would have happened; otherwise, a deficiency would arise from a creature's will opposing God's. The passage also invokes the consensus of the Salaf that whatever God wills comes to pass, and what He does not will does not.

Key Terms

إرادة
مشيئة
نقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's command and will are identical or that God lacks power over disbelief.
Correction: Command and will are distinct attributes; God has power over all but chooses not to will belief for some.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that command (amr) is distinct from will (irada)?
A) God commands what He loves.
B) God commanded the disbelievers to believe but did not will their belief, as it did not occur.
C) Will is only for actions of the willer.

Key Takeaway
God's command does not necessarily entail His will; this is proven by the fact that He commands disbelievers to believe without willing it.

?

The second proof: command and will target different objects
Sanusi enumerates the second proof for distinctness of command and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that command and will are distinct, based on the different objects they pertain to.

Core Idea
Command is for another's action; will is for one's own.

Sanusi continues his argument that divine command and divine will are separate attributes. The second proof looks at the objects of each: command is directed to the actions of others, while will — understood as volition, not appetite — is an attribute of the willer himself. This simple distinction shows that they cannot be the same thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the precise wording: 'command pertains to the action of another, while will pertains only to the action of the willer.'
The phrase 'not in the sense of appetite and love' is crucial.
2
Identify the key difference
Contrast the object of command (action of another) with the object of will (action of the willer). Ask yourself: can a single attribute have two different objects in this way?
3
Reflect on the implication
If command and will were identical, they would have to share the same object. Since they do not, they must be distinct. This is the core of the argument.
This argument relies on a basic principle of logic: if two things are identical, they have the same properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنَّ الأمر يتعلَّق بفعل الغيرِ ، والإرادة ـ لا بمعنى الشهوة والمحبة ـ لا تتعلق إلا بفعل المريد . الثالثُ : أَنَّ مَنْ حَلَفَ : ليقضينَ غريمَهُ دينَهُ غداً إِنْ شَاءَ اللهُ ، فتمكَّنَ مِنْ قضائِهِ ولم يقضه.. لم يحنث ، معَ أَنَّ اللهَ قد أمرَهُ بذلك ، فلو تضمَّنَ الأمرُ الإرادة لكانَ قد شاءَ اللهُ قضاءَهُ ، فكانَ يجب أن يحنث ، ولم يحنث بالإجماع
The second: that command pertains to the action of another, while will — not in the sense of appetite and love — pertains only to the action of the willer. The third: that whoever swore, 'I will definitely pay my debtor his debt tomorrow if God wills,' and he was able to pay it but did not pay it, he does not break his oath, even though God commanded him to do so. So if command entailed will, then God would have willed his payment, and he would have been obligated to break his oath, but he does not break his oath by consensus.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents two further proofs. The second proof argues based on the nature of command and will: command is directed to the action of another, while will (as volition, not appetite) is directed to the action of the willer himself. Since God's command is to others, but His will is His own attribute, they are distinct. The third proof uses an example from oaths. If a person swears to pay a debt tomorrow conditioned on 'if God wills,' and he is able to pay but does not, he does not break his oath by consensus. Yet God has commanded him to pay (since delaying payment to a capable person is injustice). If command implied divine will, then God would have willed the payment, so if the debtor did not pay, he would be opposing God's will, and his oath would be broken. Since it is not broken, command does not entail will.

Key Terms
أمر
إرادة
شهوة
مريد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'will' here includes appetite or love, making it similar to command which also involves desire for the action to occur.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly excludes that sense; will here means volition, which is an attribute of the agent, not a desire for another's action.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, to whose action does command pertain?
A) The action of the commander
B) The action of another
C) The action of the willer
D) The action of no one

Key Takeaway
Command and will are distinct because command is directed to another's action while will is an attribute of the willer relating to his own action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself reluctant to obey a command of Allah, do you ever stop to consider that your will might be at odds with His command? What does this tell you about the state of your heart before Him?
?
This lesson shows that command targets another's action while will targets one's own. How does this affect your understanding of your responsibility when you hear a divine command? Are you treating it as a mere suggestion, or as a directive from One whose will is entirely independent and authoritative?
?
If God's will is never subject to command, what does that imply about your own attitude toward the commands of Allah? Do you resent being commanded because it highlights that your will is not autonomous? How can you cultivate submission (islam) in light of this truth?

The third proof: the oath example
Sanusi presents the third proof using a conditional oath example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a conditional oath demonstrates that command does not entail divine will.

Core Idea
A conditional oath shows: God’s command does not imply His will.

The third proof uses a concrete legal scenario. A person swears to pay a debt tomorrow if God wills, but then fails to pay even though he is able. By scholarly consensus, he does not break his oath. Yet God commanded him to pay (since failing to pay a debt when able is unjust). If God's command automatically implied God's will that the action occur, then God would have willed the payment, and the failure to pay would have contradicted God's will, breaking the oath. Since the oath is not broken, command and will must be distinct.

Guided Path
1
Understand the oath scenario
Read the passage and imagine the situation: a person swears 'by God I will pay my debtor tomorrow if God wills.' He is able to pay but does not. The key is that his oath is conditional on God's will.
The condition 'if God wills' (إن شاء الله) is important because it ties the outcome to divine will.
2
Identify God's command
God commanded the person to pay the debt (since delaying payment to a capable person is injustice). So there is a divine command to pay.
This command is general, not specifically about the oath.
3
Consider the implication if command entailed will
If command included divine will, then God would have willed that the person pay. In that case, if the person does not pay, he opposes God's will, and the condition 'if God wills' would be false, so the oath would be broken (hanth).
The passage says 'so if command entailed will, then God would have willed his payment, and he would have been obligated to break his oath.'
4
Reach the conclusion
But by consensus (ijma), the person does not break his oath. Therefore, command does not entail will.
The conclusion is that command and will are distinct attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنَّ الأمر يتعلَّق بفعل الغيرِ ، والإرادة ـ لا بمعنى الشهوة والمحبة ـ لا تتعلق إلا بفعل المريد . الثالثُ : أَنَّ مَنْ حَلَفَ : ليقضينَ غريمَهُ دينَهُ غداً إِنْ شَاءَ اللهُ ، فتمكَّنَ مِنْ قضائِهِ ولم يقضه.. لم يحنث ، معَ أَنَّ اللهَ قد أمرَهُ بذلك ، فلو تضمَّنَ الأمرُ الإرادة لكانَ قد شاءَ اللهُ قضاءَهُ ، فكانَ يجب أن يحنث ، ولم يحنث بالإجماع
The second: that command pertains to the action of another, while will — not in the sense of appetite and love — pertains only to the action of the willer. The third: that whoever swore, 'I will definitely pay my debtor his debt tomorrow if God wills,' and he was able to pay it but did not pay it, he does not break his oath, even though God commanded him to do so. So if command entailed will, then God would have willed his payment, and he would have been obligated to break his oath, but he does not break his oath by consensus.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents two further proofs. The second proof argues based on the nature of command and will: command is directed to the action of another, while will (as volition, not appetite) is directed to the action of the willer himself. Since God's command is to others, but His will is His own attribute, they are distinct. The third proof uses an example from oaths. If a person swears to pay a debt tomorrow conditioned on 'if God wills,' and he is able to pay but does not, he does not break his oath by consensus. Yet God has commanded him to pay (since delaying payment to a capable person is injustice). If command implied divine will, then God would have willed the payment, so if the debtor did not pay, he would be opposing God's will, and his oath would be broken. Since it is not broken, command does not entail will.

Refuting Objections on Command and Will
Sanusi presents objections to the proof that command is distinct from will and refutes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes objections that claim command (amr) can exist without will (irada) and that news (khabar) is reducible to knowledge of syntactic form.

Core Idea
Apparent command without will is not real command; it's pretense, and 'want' can mean desire, not volition.

Sanusi has argued that command (amr) and will (irada) are distinct attributes. Now he addresses objections from opponents who try to show command existing without will, or who reduce news to mere linguistic form. These refutations solidify the Ash'ari position by clarifying the nature of real command and internal speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the three objections and how each is answered.
The first objection involves a ruler testing a slave-owner; the second is a linguistic argument; the third concerns the nature of news.
2
Analyze the first objection and refutation
Identify the scenario: a ruler rebukes a slave-owner, then commands him to beat the slave while intending his disobedience. How does Ibn al-Tilimsani refute this? Focus on the distinction between appearing to command and actually commanding.
He says the ruler's excuse is based on pretense, not on real command.
3
Examine the second objection and refutation
The objection: one can say 'I want you to do this but I do not command you.' Sanusi says this is weak because 'want' can mean love or desire, not volitional will. Notice how he reinterprets the phrase.
This preserves the distinction between amr and irada.
4
Understand the third refutation
The third objection tries to reduce internal news (khabar nafsi) to knowledge of syntactic form. Sanusi gives two reasons: (1) form varies while internal meaning is constant; (2) the same formula can express news or demand while internal meaning differs.
This shows that internal speech is not reducible to external form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : ولأنَّ المعاتَبَ مِنْ جهة السلطان على ضرْبِ عبدِهِ إذا اعتذر بأنَّهُ يخالفُهُ ، فلم يصدقُهُ ، فأراد تمهيد عذرِهِ.. فَإِنَّهُ يأمرُهُ بحضرتِهِ ويريد مخالفتَهُ ، فإذا أمرَهُ فقد تحقق الأمر بدونِ الإرادة . قالَ ابنُ التَّلِمْسانيّ : ( وهذا لا حُجَّةَ فيهِ ، فَإِنَّ عذرَهُ يتمهَّدُ بِإِظهارِ أنَّهُ آمَرُ ، ولا يتوقَّفُ على أنَّهُ آمر حقيقةً ، ومثله لازم للأشعرية في الطلب اليقيني الذي أثبتوه ) . قالوا : ومِنَ الدليل على المغايرة : أنَّهُ يحسنُ أَنْ يُقالَ : ( أريدُ منك فعل هذا ولا آمرُكَ بهِ ) ، ولو كان كلُّ آمر مريداً . . لتناقض وهذا أيضاً ضعيفٌ؛ لأنَّهُ يمكنُ أنْ يُحمَلَ قوله : ( أريدُ منكَ ) على أنّي أحبُّ ذلكَ أو أشتهيه ، فلا ينافي ذلك نفْي الأمرِ . وأما ردُّ الخبرِ إلى العلم بنظم الصيغة : فباطل أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ نظم الصيغة يختلف باختلافِ الصيغ الدالة على المعنى ، والخبرُ النفسي لا يختلفُ ، ولأنَّ الصيغة الواحدة قد تُستعمَلُ في الخبرِ والطلب معاً ، والعلمُ بنظمِهما لا يختلفُ ، وما في النفس يختلفُ
They said: And because the one who is rebuked by the ruler for beating his slave, when he excuses himself saying that the slave disobeys him, and the ruler does not believe him, so he wants to prepare his excuse... then he commands him in his presence while intending his disobedience. So when he commands him, command is realized without will. Ibn al-Tilimsani said: 'This is no proof, for his excuse is prepared by showing that he is commanding, and it does not depend on his actually being a commander, and the like is necessary for the Ash'aris in the certain demand which they affirmed.' They said: And from the proof of difference: that it is proper to say: 'I want from you to do this but I do not command you to do it,' and if every commander were a willer... then there would be contradiction. And this is also weak, because his saying 'I want from you' can be interpreted as 'I love that' or 'I desire it,' so it does not negate the absence of command. As for reducing news to knowledge of the composition of the formula, it is also false, because the composition of the formula differs according to the different formulas indicating meaning, while the internal news does not differ, and because a single formula may be used for both news and demand, and knowledge of their composition does not differ, while what is in the soul differs.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage contains objections to the proofs that command is distinct from will, and their refutations. First, an objection from the example of a ruler rebuking a slave-owner: the ruler commands the owner to beat the slave in his presence while actually intending the opposite (to test him). This shows command without will. Ibn al-Tilimsani responds that this is not a valid proof, because the ruler's excuse is based on appearing to command, not actually commanding, and a similar issue arises for the Ash'ari concept of 'certain demand' (al-talab al-yaqini). Second, the objection that one can say 'I want you to do this but I do not command you' is answered by interpreting 'want' as love or desire, not volitional will. Third, the attempt to reduce news (khabar) to knowledge of the syntactic form is refuted because the form changes while internal meaning does not, and a single formula can be used for news and demand while internal meaning differs.

Key Terms
أمر
إرادة
طلب يقيني
خبر
صيغة
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Command can exist without will, as shown by the ruler's example.
Correction: The ruler's command is not real; it is only a pretense to test the owner. Real command requires genuine will.

Checkpoint
How does Ibn al-Tilimsani refute the objection about the ruler commanding his slave-owner?
A) He agrees it proves command without will.
B) He says the ruler's excuse is based on appearing to command, not on actually commanding.
C) He says the ruler does not really utter a command.
D) He says the objection applies only to non-Ash'aris.

Key Takeaway
Objections against the distinction between command and will are refuted by clarifying that apparent command without true intention is not real command, and internal speech is not reducible to external linguistic form.

?

?

The Four Existences and the Distinction Between Recitation and the Recited
Sanusi introduces the four existences to distinguish eternal divine speech from its created manifestations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari doctrine distinguishes between the eternal speech of Allah and its created expressions (recitation, writing, etc.) through the concept of the four existences.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is eternal; our recitation and writing are created acts pointing to it.

Sanusi has just established the Ash'ari position on divine speech. Now he addresses a natural question: if the Quran is eternal, how can it be recited and written in time? In this passage, he introduces the notion of the four existences to show that created acts like recitation and writing are distinct from the eternal divine speech they indicate. This distinction clarifies the Salaf's language and protects against anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Recognize that the Salaf described the Quran as preserved in hearts, recited by tongues, and written in codices. Without proper understanding, this could be misinterpreted as Allah's speech literally dwelling in creatures.
Sanusi refutes the idea of hulul (indwelling) here.
2
Learn the four existences
A thing can exist in four modes: in external entities (a'yan), in minds (adhhan), on tongues (lisan), and in writing (banan). This is a key conceptual tool.
These four are general categories for any thing.
3
Apply to the Quran
Using the four existences, see that recitation (tilaawah), reading (qira'ah), and writing (kitabah) are created acts, while the recited (matluww), read (maqru'), and written (maktub) refer to the eternal speech of Allah.
The first of each pair is hadith (created), the second is qadim (eternal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت مذهب أهل الحق في كلام الله تعالى عرفت أن إطلاق السلف على كلام الله أنه محفوظ في الصدور، مقروء بالألسنة، مكتوب في المصاحف... لا يحمل على الحلول الذي فرغنا من بيان استحالته، بل لما كانت هذه الأشياء دالة على كلامه جل وعلا... أطلق عليها كلامه؛ من باب إطلاق اسم الدال على المدلول، وأطلق على أنه موجود فيها؛ أي: فهماً وعلماً؛ لأن الشيء له وجودات أربع: وجود في الأعيان، ووجود في الأذهان، ووجود في اللسان، ووجود بالبنان؛ وهو الكتابة. وبهذا تعرف: أن التلاوة غير المتلو، والقراءة غير المقروء، والكتابة غير المكتوب؛ لأن الأول من كل قسمين حادث، والثاني قديم؛ وهو كلام الله جل وعلا، والتلاوة والقراءة والكتابة متناهية، والمقروء والمتلو والمكتوب لا نهاية له. وبالجملة: فالإطلاقات اللفظية تابعة للنقل من حيث إطلاقها
And when you know the doctrine of Ahl al-Haqq concerning the speech of Allah, you know that the Salaf's application to the speech of Allah that it is preserved in the hearts, recited by tongues, written in codices... is not to be understood in terms of indwelling (hulul), whose impossibility we have already explained. Rather, since these things indicate His speech, may He be exalted, they are called His speech by way of applying the name of the indicated to the indicator. And it is said that it exists in them, i.e., understanding and knowledge, because a thing has four existences: existence in external entities, existence in minds, existence on the tongue, and existence by the fingers, which is writing. And by this you know that recitation (tilaawah) is other than the recited (matluww), reading (qira'ah) is other than the read (maqru'), writing (kitabah) is other than the written (maktub), because the first of each pair is created, and the second is eternal—and that is the speech of Allah, may He be exalted. Recitation, reading, and writing are finite, while the read, the recited, and the written are infinite. In summary, verbal applications are dependent on transmission (naql) with respect to their application.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and distinction. It begins by informing that once one grasps the Ash'ari doctrine of divine speech, one understands that the Salaf's descriptions of the Quran as preserved in hearts, recited, and written do not imply that Allah's speech indwells those things (a refutation of hulul). Instead, these things are indicators (adilla) of His speech, so they are called 'speech' metaphorically as the name of the indicated is applied to the indicator. Then the author introduces the concept of four existences: existence in external entities (a'yan), in minds (adhhan), on the tongue (lisan), and by writing (banan). This explains how the Quran exists in multiple modes while the eternal speech itself remains distinct. Consequently, one can distinguish: recitation (tilaawah) is not the same as the recited (matluww), reading (qira'ah) is not the same as the read (maqru'), writing (kitabah) is not the same as the written (maktub). The first of each pair is created (hadith), while the second is eternal (qadim)—the speech of Allah. The created acts are finite, but the eternal speech is infinite. Finally, the author notes that verbal applications (like calling writing 'Quran') are based on transmitted usage (naql), not on literal meaning.

Key Terms
وجود
وجود أعيان
وجود أذهان
وجود لسان
وجود بنان
تلاوة
متلو
قراءة
مقروء
كتابة
مكتوب
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Recitation of the Quran is eternal because it is the Quran.
Correction: Recitation is a human act, created and finite; only the speech of Allah that is recited is eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between recitation (tilaawah) and the recited (matluww)?
A) They are identical.
B) They are both eternal.
C) Recitation is created and the recited is eternal.
D) Recitation is eternal and the recited is created.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's eternal speech is distinct from all created acts that express it, such as recitation and writing, which are finite and created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you consciously distinguish between your finite voice and the infinite speech it points to? How does that awareness change the way you approach each verse?
?
If the Quran's eternal speech is beyond your physical words and pages, why do you sometimes feel that your recitation or writing of it is 'holy' in itself? Where does that reverence truly belong—to your act or to what it indicates?
?
The Salaf called the mushaf 'Quran' by convention, not literally. Does your attachment to the physical book sometimes cause you to forget that the real Quran is an eternal attribute of Allah? How can you shift your heart from the pages to the One who speaks through them?

The Four Modes of Existence and God's Speech
Sanusi explains the four existences and the distinction between created and eternal in God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four modes of existence and why the recitation, reading, and writing of the Quran are created, while the eternal speech of Allah is uncreated.

Core Idea
The Quran is eternal in meaning, but its recitation is created.

Sanusi addresses a subtle but critical point: how can the eternal speech of Allah be described as 'preserved in hearts' and 'written in copies'? He explains that these physical manifestations are not the eternal speech itself but indicators of it. This lesson unpacks the four modes of existence and the distinction between the created and uncreated aspects of God's speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the four existences listed and the three pairs at the end.
Pay attention to the terms 'tilawa', 'qira'a', and 'kitaba'.
2
Identify the distinction
Recognize that recitation (tilawa) is created, while the recited (matluw) is eternal. The same applies to reading/writing.
The first of each pair is created, the second eternal.
3
Understand the error avoided
Understand that this guards against the heresy of hulul (indwelling) – the belief that the eternal speech literally dwells in created things.
The Salaf's language is metaphorical: the name of the indicated is applied to the indicator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجودات الأربعة

وإذا عرفت مذهب أهلِ الحقِّ في كلامِ اللهِ تعالى عرفْتَ أَنَّ إطلاق السلف على كلامِ اللهِ أَنَّهُ محفوظ في الصدور ، مقروء بالألسنة مكتوب في المصاحف .. لا يُحمَلُ على الحلول الذي فرغنا مِنْ بيانِ استحالته ، بل لمَّا كانَتْ هذهِ الأشياء دالةً على كلامِهِ جَلَّ وعلا . . أُطلِقَ عليها كلامُهُ ؛ مِنْ بابِ إطلاق اسم الدال على المدلول ، وأُطلِقَ على أنَّهُ موجود فيها ؛ أي: فهماً وعلماً ؛ لأنَّ الشيء له وجودات أربع : وجود في الأعيانِ ، ووجود في الأذهان ، ووجود في اللسان ، ووجود بالبنان ؛ وهو الكتابة

وبهذا تعرف : أنَّ التلاوة غيرُ المتلوّ ، والقراءة غير المقروء ، والكتابة غير المكتوبِ ؛ لأنَّ الأَوَّلَ مِنْ كُلِّ قسمين حادث ، والثاني قديم ؛ وهو كلامُ الله جل وعلا، والتلاوة والقراءة والكتابة متناهيةٌ ، والمقروء والمتلو والمكتوب لا نهاية له
The Four Existences

When you have known the doctrine of the people of truth concerning God's speech, you know that the Salaf's application (of the term 'God's speech') to God's speech as being preserved in hearts, recited by tongues, written in copies... is not to be understood as indwelling (hulul) whose impossibility we have finished explaining; rather, because these things indicate His speech, may He be glorified and exalted, His speech is applied to them by way of applying the name of the indicated to the indicator. And it is applied that He exists in them, i.e., as understanding and knowledge, because a thing has four existences: existence in external objects, existence in minds, existence in the tongue, and existence by the fingertips—which is writing.

And by this you know that recitation (tilawa) is not the recited (matluw), reading (qira'a) is not the read (maqru'), and writing (kitaba) is not the written (maktub); because the first of each pair is created (hadith), and the second is eternal (qadim)—which is God's speech, may He be glorified and exalted. Recitation, reading, and writing are finite, while the recited, the read, and the written are infinite.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal definition and proof. Sanusi begins by stating that the Salaf's description of God's speech as preserved, recited, and written does not imply that the eternal speech indwells those things (hulul), which has been refuted. Instead, because these things indicate God's speech, they are metaphorically called 'God's speech' by applying the name of the indicated (the eternal speech) to the indicator (the recitation, etc.). He then introduces the four modes of existence (external, mental, verbal, written) to clarify how the same entity can exist in different modes. Applying this to God's speech: the eternal speech (qadim) is the meaning in God's essence, while its recitation, writing, and reading are created (hadith) and finite. Thus, the recitation (tilawa) is not the recited (matluw), etc. This guards against the error of treating the physical copies as the eternal speech itself.

متلو
قراءة
مقروء
كتابة
مكتوب
حادث
قديم
وجودات أربع
حلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Recitation of the Quran is the eternal speech itself.
Correction: Recitation is created, while the recited (the meaning) is eternal.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the relationship between the recitation (tilawa) of the Quran and the eternal speech (kalam) of God?
A) They are identical in every respect.
B) The recitation is the eternal speech itself.
C) The recitation is created, while the eternal speech is uncreated.
D) Both are created.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's recitation, writing, and reading are created, but the eternal speech of Allah they indicate is uncreated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever pause to consider that your voice and breath are created, while the meaning you are uttering is eternal? How does that awareness change the way you approach every letter?
?
The Salaf described the Quran as preserved in hearts, recited by tongues, written in copies. But Sanusi warns us not to imagine God's speech literally dwelling in those things. Have you ever caught yourself thinking of the mushaf as if it were Allah Himself? What does it mean for you to hold a copy of the Quran with the understanding that it indicates, but is not, His eternal speech?
?
Sanusi says the recited (matluw) has no end—it is infinite. Your recitation, on the other hand, is finite and ends. When your recitation ends, does the Quran's eternal meaning remain present in your heart? If not, what does that say about your relationship with the Quran?

Silence and the Eternity of God's Speech
Sanusi defines silence as non-existence of speech and refutes the Hashwiyya claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing silence to Allah is impossible, as it would make His speech temporal.

Core Idea
Silence is nothing but the absence of speech, which for Allah would destroy eternity.

The Hashwiyya sect claimed that God can be silent, implying His speech is not always existent. Sanusi refutes this by first establishing that God never ceases speaking, then showing that silence logically entails the non-existence of speech.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Hashwiyya claim
Learn that the Hashwiyya attribute silence to Allah, implying His speech is not eternal. Sanusi declares God transcendent above this.
2
Define silence
Silence is simply the non-existence of speech, not a positive attribute or state of concealment.
3
Examine two scenarios
If silence occurs before speech, non-existence precedes speech; if after, non-existence occurs to speech. Both lead to temporality.
This is the core logical argument.
4
Conclude temporality
Both scenarios negate the eternity of speech and prove it is temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفي ما ذهبت إليه الحشوية مِنْ نسبة السكوت له تعالى ] قوله : ( أو يطرأ عليه سكوت ( إشارة إلى مذهب الحشويَّةِ الذينَ وصفوا كلامَهُ تعالى بالسكوتِ ، تعالى الله عن قولهم علوّاً كبيراً ، بل لم يزل سبحانَهُ مُتكلّماً ولا يزالُ ؛ إذ لو جازَ أنْ يسكت جلَّ وعلا عن كلامِهِ لجازَ أنْ يتّصفَ كلامُهُ بالعدمِ ، وذلك يُوجِبُ حدوثه ، وما ادعاه الحشويَّةُ مِنْ كُمُونِ الكلامِ معَ السكوتِ . . هوس لا حاصل له ؛ إذْ لا معنى للسكوت إلا انعدام الكلام ، فإنْ كانَ السكوتُ قبل وجودِ الكلامِ لزمَ سَبْقُ العدمِ عليهِ ، وذلكَ نفي لقدمِهِ وإثبات لحدوثِهِ ، وإنْ كان بعد وجودِ الكلام فقد طرأ على الكلام العدمُ ، وذلك ينفي بقاءَهُ وإذا انتفى البقاء انتفى القدمُ ؛ لما عرفتَ أَنَّ كلَّ ما ثبت قدمُهُ استحال عدمه ، وينعكس بعكس النقيض الموافقِ إلى : أنَّ كلَّ ما لم يستحل عدمه لم يثبت قدمُهُ ، وإذا انتفى القدمُ أيضاً لزمَ ضدُّهُ الذي هو الحدوث وبالجملة : فالسكوتُ يستلزم عدم الكلام السابق ، وتجدُّدَ الكلام اللاحق ، فيكون اللاحق حادثاً بغير وسط ، والسابق حادثاً بواسطة أنَّ ما لحقه العدم لزم أن يسبقه العدمُ ، وإذا لزم مِنَ السكوتِ حدوث الكلامِ لزمَ منهُ حدوثُ الذاتِ الموصوفة بهِ ؛ لما عرفتَ أنَّ قيامَ الصفةِ الحادثة بشيءٍ . . يُوجِبُ حدوث ذلكَ الشيء ، ودعوى الاتِّصافِ بذلكَ لِمَنْ تنزَّةَ عن الحدوثِ في ذاتِهِ وجميع صفاتِهِ جلَّ وعلا . . كفر لا محالة . وما ورد في الحديثِ ممَّا يخالف هذا الذي قرَّرناه . . فمؤوّل ، ومنه ما ورد في الحديثِ : « إِنَّ اللهَ يُسْمِعُ النَّاسَ يَوْمَ الْقِيَامَةِ قَائِلاً يَقُولُ : يَقُولُ اللهُ سُبْحَانَهُ : أَنْصِتُوا كَمَا أَنْصَتُ لَكُمْ ، أَنَا الْيَوْمَ ظَالِمٌ إِنْ جَاوَزَنِي ظُلْمُ ظَالِم
Refuting what the Hashwiyya went to in attributing silence to Him, may He be exalted. His saying: 'or silence may occur to Him' is a reference to the doctrine of the Hashwiyya who described His speech, may He be exalted, with silence — exalted is Allah above their words, a great exaltation. Rather, He, glorified, has never ceased to be speaking and will never cease. For if it were possible for Him, mighty and majestic, to be silent regarding His speech, then it would be possible for His speech to be characterized by non-existence, and that necessitates its temporality. And what the Hashwiyya claim of the concealment of speech with silence is nonsense with no result. For there is no meaning of silence except the non-existence of speech. If the silence is before the existence of speech, then non-existence precedes it, and that is a negation of its eternity and a proof of its temporality. And if it is after the existence of speech, then non-existence has occurred to speech, and that negates its permanence. And if permanence is negated, then eternity is negated, because you know that everything whose eternity is established, its non-existence is impossible. And by the contrapositive it follows that everything whose non-existence is not impossible, its eternity is not established. And if eternity is also negated, then its opposite, which is temporality, follows. In summary: silence entails the non-existence of previous speech and the renewal of subsequent speech; thus the subsequent is temporal without an intermediary, and the previous is temporal by means of the fact that what is followed by non-existence must have been preceded by non-existence. And if silence necessitates the temporality of speech, then it necessitates the temporality of the essence described by it, because you know that the standing of a temporal attribute in something necessitates the temporality of that thing. And the claim of being described with that for one who is exalted above temporality in His essence and all His attributes, may He be glorified and exalted, is disbelief without doubt. And whatever comes in hadith that contradicts what we have established is interpreted metaphorically. Among it is what came in the hadith: 'Indeed, Allah will make people hear on the Day of Resurrection, an announcer saying: Allah says: Be silent as I was silent to you. Today I am an oppressor if the oppression of an oppressor passes by Me.'
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Hashwiyya sect who claim that God can be silent (sukut) with respect to His speech. The author begins by declaring God transcendent above such a notion and affirms that God has never ceased to be speaking (lam yazal mutakalliman). The core argument is that silence logically entails the non-existence ('adam) of speech. If silence precedes speech, then speech is preceded by non-existence, negating its eternity (qidam) and proving its temporality (huduth). If silence follows speech, then non-existence occurs to speech, negating its permanence (baqa'), and since permanence is a necessary condition of eternity, eternity is also negated. Using a logical contrapositive: if something's non-existence is possible, it cannot be eternal. Thus speech would be temporal in either case. The author then argues that a temporal attribute (sifa haditha) in a subject necessitates the temporality of that subject. Therefore, if speech were temporal, God's essence would also be temporal, which is impossible. Attributing temporality to God is disbelief (kufr). Finally, any apparent hadith that seems to imply silence must be interpreted metaphorically (mu'awwal). The author gives an example hadith about God saying 'Be silent' on the Day of Judgment, indicating that such texts are not to be taken literally.

الحشوية
الكلام
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that silence is a positive attribute that can coexist with speech or occur without affecting its eternity.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that silence is merely the absence of speech, and if it occurs, it necessarily makes speech temporal.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of silence according to Sanusi?
A) A positive attribute of God
B) The non-existence of speech
C) A state of contemplation
D) The concealment of speech

Key Takeaway
Silence cannot be attributed to Allah because it would render His speech temporal, contradicting His eternal nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's speech is eternal and never silent, what does that mean for how you approach the Qur'an? Do you truly treat it as His eternal word speaking to you right now, or as a book from the past? How can you change that starting today?
?
When you feel that God is silent in your life, how does this lesson challenge that feeling? Does your feeling contradict the reality of His eternal speech? How will you remind yourself of this truth in moments of spiritual dryness?
?
If silence for God would imply temporality, then His attributes are permanent and unchanging. In what area of your life do you need to trust that God's mercy, justice, or guidance is still present and active, even when you don't perceive it? What practical step can you take to act on that trust?

The Logical Implications: Silence Leads to Temporality of the Essence
Sanusi uses logical contrapositives to show silence leads to temporal essence and addresses hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical chain from silence to the temporality of God's essence and the necessity of interpreting contrary hadith.

Core Idea
If speech were temporal, the essence would also be temporal, which is pure disbelief.

Building on the previous lesson that silence would make speech temporal, Sanusi now presents the logical extension: temporal attributes necessitate a temporal essence. He also addresses the apparent contradicting hadith by affirming the principle of metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Review the contrapositive argument
If non-existence is possible, then eternity is negated. This logical rule is applied to speech.
2
Understand the summary of silence
Silence entails non-existence of old speech and renewal of new speech, making both temporal.
3
Recognize the implication for the essence
A temporal attribute makes the subject temporal; thus if speech is temporal, the essence must also be temporal.
4
Acknowledge the strong conclusion
Attributing temporality to Allah is disbelief (kufr).
5
Apply the principle of ta'wil to hadith
Any hadith that seems to imply silence for Allah must be interpreted metaphorically.
Example hadith is given but incomplete in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفي ما ذهبت إليه الحشوية مِنْ نسبة السكوت له تعالى ] قوله : ( أو يطرأ عليه سكوت ( إشارة إلى مذهب الحشويَّةِ الذينَ وصفوا كلامَهُ تعالى بالسكوتِ ، تعالى الله عن قولهم علوّاً كبيراً ، بل لم يزل سبحانَهُ مُتكلّماً ولا يزالُ ؛ إذ لو جازَ أنْ يسكت جلَّ وعلا عن كلامِهِ لجازَ أنْ يتّصفَ كلامُهُ بالعدمِ ، وذلك يُوجِبُ حدوثه ، وما ادعاه الحشويَّةُ مِنْ كُمُونِ الكلامِ معَ السكوتِ . . هوس لا حاصل له ؛ إذْ لا معنى للسكوت إلا انعدام الكلام ، فإنْ كانَ السكوتُ قبل وجودِ الكلامِ لزمَ سَبْقُ العدمِ عليهِ ، وذلكَ نفي لقدمِهِ وإثبات لحدوثِهِ ، وإنْ كان بعد وجودِ الكلام فقد طرأ على الكلام العدمُ ، وذلك ينفي بقاءَهُ وإذا انتفى البقاء انتفى القدمُ ؛ لما عرفتَ أَنَّ كلَّ ما ثبت قدمُهُ استحال عدمه ، وينعكس بعكس النقيض الموافقِ إلى : أنَّ كلَّ ما لم يستحل عدمه لم يثبت قدمُهُ ، وإذا انتفى القدمُ أيضاً لزمَ ضدُّهُ الذي هو الحدوث وبالجملة : فالسكوتُ يستلزم عدم الكلام السابق ، وتجدُّدَ الكلام اللاحق ، فيكون اللاحق حادثاً بغير وسط ، والسابق حادثاً بواسطة أنَّ ما لحقه العدم لزم أن يسبقه العدمُ ، وإذا لزم مِنَ السكوتِ حدوث الكلامِ لزمَ منهُ حدوثُ الذاتِ الموصوفة بهِ ؛ لما عرفتَ أنَّ قيامَ الصفةِ الحادثة بشيءٍ . . يُوجِبُ حدوث ذلكَ الشيء ، ودعوى الاتِّصافِ بذلكَ لِمَنْ تنزَّةَ عن الحدوثِ في ذاتِهِ وجميع صفاتِهِ جلَّ وعلا . . كفر لا محالة . وما ورد في الحديثِ ممَّا يخالف هذا الذي قرَّرناه . . فمؤوّل ، ومنه ما ورد في الحديثِ : « إِنَّ اللهَ يُسْمِعُ النَّاسَ يَوْمَ الْقِيَامَةِ قَائِلاً يَقُولُ : يَقُولُ اللهُ سُبْحَانَهُ : أَنْصِتُوا كَمَا أَنْصَتُ لَكُمْ ، أَنَا الْيَوْمَ ظَالِمٌ إِنْ جَاوَزَنِي ظُلْمُ ظَالِم

?
This lesson states that any hadith implying silence must be interpreted metaphorically. Have you ever held onto a literal reading of a hadith about Allah that contradicts rational proofs? What does that reveal about your trust in the scholars' method of ta’wīl? How will you ensure your faith in Allah's attributes is based on both revelation and sound reason?
?
If the logical conclusion of attributing silence to Allah is that His essence is temporal, then what does your own tendency to feel that Allah is 'not speaking' or 'silent' in your trials reveal about your understanding of His attributes? How will you correct this in your worship and reliance on Him?

The Proof That Silence in God Implies Creation
Sanusi proves that silence in God implies temporality of speech and essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing silence to Allah logically entails that His speech (and thus His essence) is created, and why such a claim is considered kufr.

Core Idea
Silence is not a divine attribute; it proves speech is temporal, and a temporal attribute makes its subject temporal.

This passage presents a crucial rational proof in Islamic theology. Sanusi demonstrates that if silence (sukūt) were attributed to God, it would logically entail that His speech is created, and since speech is an attribute of God, it would imply that God Himself is created. This shows why Muslims affirm that God speaks eternally.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of silence
The passage begins by defining silence as the absence of speech. This is key: silence is not a positive attribute but a lack.
2
Examine the two possibilities of silence
Sanusi considers silence before speech and after speech. Each leads to a contradiction with God's eternity.
3
Grasp the final implication
The proof concludes that silence forces speech to be temporal, and since attributes subsist in the essence, the essence becomes temporal. Attributing this to Allah is kufr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا معنى للسكوت إلا انعدام الكلام ، فإن كان السكوت قبل وجود الكلام لزم سبق العدم عليه ، وذلك نفي لقدمه وإثبات لحدوثه ، وإن كان بعد وجود الكلام فقد طرأ على الكلام العدم ، وذلك ينفي بقاءه وإذا انتفى البقاء انتفى القدم ؛ لما عرفت أن كل ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه ، وينعكس بعكس النقيض الموافق إلى : أن كل ما لم يستحل عدمه لم يثبت قدمه ، وإذا انتفى القدم أيضاً لزم ضده الذي هو الحدوث وبالجملة : فالسكوت يستلزم عدم الكلام السابق ، وتجدد الكلام اللاحق ، فيكون اللاحق حادثاً بغير وسط ، والسابق حادثاً بواسطة أن ما لحقه العدم لزم أن يسبقه العدم ، وإذا لزم من السكوت حدوث الكلام لزم منه حدوث الذات الموصوفة به ؛ لما عرفت أن قيام الصفة الحادثة بشيء يوجب حدوث ذلك الشيء ، ودعوى الاتصاف بذلك لمن تنزه عن الحدوث في ذاته وجميع صفاته جل وعلا كفر لا محالة.
There is no meaning for silence (sukūt) except the absence of speech (kalām). If silence precedes the existence of speech, then non-existence (al-ʿadam) necessarily precedes it, and that is a negation of its eternity (qidam) and an affirmation of its temporality (ḥudūth). If it comes after the existence of speech, then non-existence has occurred to speech, and that negates its permanence (baqāʾ). When permanence is negated, eternity is negated; because you know that whatever has been established as eternal, its non-existence is impossible. And it reflects by the contrapositive of the consistent opposite to: everything whose non-existence is not impossible is not established as eternal. When eternity is also negated, its opposite which is temporality necessarily follows. In summary: silence necessitates the absence of previous speech and the renewal of subsequent speech. Thus the subsequent speech occurs without an intermediary, and the previous speech occurs via an intermediary, because what non-existence occurs to must have been preceded by non-existence. If the temporality of speech necessarily follows from silence, then the temporality of the essence described by it necessarily follows; because you know that the subsistence of a temporal attribute in something necessitates the temporality of that thing. To claim that attribute for the One who is transcendent above temporality in His essence and all His attributes, may He be glorified and exalted, is unbelief (kufr) without doubt.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof demonstrating the impossibility of attributing silence (sukūt) to God. It begins by defining silence as the absence of speech. Then it examines two possibilities: (1) silence before speech exists, which implies that speech is preceded by non-existence, so it is temporal (ḥādith); (2) silence after speech exists, which implies that speech ceases to exist, negating its permanence and thus its eternity. Since if something is eternal, its non-existence is impossible. The contrapositive is that if non-existence is possible, it is not eternal. Therefore, if silence occurs, speech must be temporal. Furthermore, a temporal attribute in a subject necessitates the temporality of the subject itself. Therefore, attributing silence to God, who is eternal, is kufr.

Key Terms
السكوت
الكلام
القدم
الحدوث
العدم
البقاء
الصفة الحادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think silence is a positive attribute that can be attributed to God.
Correction: Silence is the absence of speech, not a positive trait. Attributing it to God implies a change in His speech, leading to temporality.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, what does the occurrence of silence after speech imply about the speech?
A) It proves the speech is eternal.
B) It proves the speech has ceased to exist, negating its eternity.
C) It proves the speech is temporal only if silence precedes it.
D) It has no implication.

Key Takeaway
Attributing silence to Allah is logically impossible because it would make His speech temporal, and a temporal attribute necessitates a temporal essence, which contradicts Allah's eternity and is therefore kufr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you secretly believed that Allah's silence in your prayers meant He was absent or indifferent? How does this proof expose the danger of that thought?
?
If you truly internalized that Allah's speech is eternal and never silent, how would that change the way you approach the Qur'an in your daily life?
?
What attribute of Allah do you treat as temporary or conditional in practice, even while professing His eternity? Will you repent from that today?

Interpreting Hadith on God's Silence and Moses
Sanusi interprets hadith about God's silence and the verse of Moses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi interprets hadith and Quranic verses that seem to imply temporal aspects of God's speech, affirming instead that His speech is eternal and any such language is metaphorical.

Core Idea
God's speech is eternal; any hint of silence or initiation is metaphorical.

Sanusi addresses apparent contradictions between the rational proof that God's speech is eternal and certain hadith or Quranic phrases that might suggest God was once silent or began speaking. He insists these texts must be interpreted metaphorically, not taken literally. This lesson walks through his reasoning using a famous hadith and the verse about Moses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage along with its literal translation to get a general sense of Sanusi's argument.
Note that the passage is cut off at the end; even so, the key points are clear.
2
Identify the hadith and Ibn Dihāq’s explanation
The passage cites a hadith where God says 'Be silent.' Ibn Dihāq explains that this does not mean God was silent, but rather that He does not create hearing for the people to perceive His speech.
3
Understand the metaphorical interpretation
Sanusi then applies the same principle to the verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses with speech.' He argues that this does not mean God began speaking after silence or stopped after addressing Moses; rather, it means God removed the barrier so Moses could perceive the eternal speech.
This reinforces that God’s speech is a timeless attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ورد في الحديث مما يخالف هذا الذي قررناه فمؤول ، ومنه ما ورد في الحديث : « إن الله يسمع الناس يوم القيامة قائلا يقول : يقول الله سبحانه : أنصتوا كما أنصت لكم ، أنا اليوم ظالم إن جاوزني ظلم ظالم » قال ابن دهاق : ( يرجع معنى الحديث : إلى أن الباري سبحانه وتعالى يعلم ويرى ويسمع ، ومع ذلك لا يخلق لهم سمعا لخبره بأعمالهم لا أن الله تعالى يجوز عليه أن يصمت ؛ فإن ذلك كان يكون بانعدام كلامه ، وكلامه سبحانه وتعالى قديم ، وقد تقدم ذكر الدليل القاطع على أن القديم لا ينعدم عند ذكرنا حدوث العالم ) انتهى

قلت : [ توطئة للكلام مع المعتزلة في قوله تعالى : وَكَلَّمَ اللَّهُ مُوسَى تَكْلِيمًا ] يعني أنه تجوز بإطلاق الصمت على لازمه ؛ وهو عدم إدراك ما عند الصامت من الخبر ، وبهذا تعرف أنه ليس معنى ( كلم الله موسى تكليما ) أنه ابتدأ الكلام له بعد أن كان ساكتا ، ولا أنه بعد ما كلمه انقطع كلامه وسكت ، تعالى عن ذلك علوا كبيرا ، وإنما المعنى : أنه تعالى بفضله أزال المانع عن موسى عليه السلام وخلق له ...
And what has come in the ḥadīth that contradicts what we have established is interpreted (muʾawwal). Among them is what has come in the ḥadīth: 'Indeed God will make people hear on the Day of Resurrection, a speaker saying: God, glorified be He, says: Be silent as I was silent for you. Today I am an oppressor if the oppression of an oppressor surpasses Me.' Ibn Dihāq said: 'The meaning of the ḥadīth refers to the fact that the Creator, glorified and exalted, knows, sees, and hears, and despite that He does not create for them hearing of His report of their deeds—not that God, exalted, may be silent; for that would be by the absence of His speech, and His speech, glorified and exalted, is eternal. The decisive proof has preceded that the eternal does not cease to exist when we mentioned the temporality of the world.' Ended.

I said: [An introduction to the discussion with the Muʿtazila concerning His statement, exalted: 'And Allah spoke to Moses with [direct] speech.' Meaning that He used the term 'silence' metaphorically for its necessary implication, which is the lack of perception of what the silent person has of information. By this you know that the meaning of 'Allah spoke to Moses with speech' is not that He initiated speech to him after having been silent, nor that after He spoke to him His speech ceased and He was silent—exalted is He above that greatly. Rather, the meaning is that He, exalted, by His grace removed the obstacle from Moses, peace be upon him, and created for him ...
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage deals with the interpretation of apparent anthropomorphic or temporal language in ḥadīth and the Qurʾān regarding God's speech. It begins by stating that any ḥadīth that appears to contradict the previous rational proof (that God is never silent) must be interpreted metaphorically. It then cites a ḥadīth where God says 'Be silent' to people on Judgment Day. Ibn Dihāq explains that this does not imply God Himself was silent; rather, it means God will not create in them the ability to hear His report of their deeds—i.e., they will not perceive His speech—but His speech itself is eternal and does not cease. The author adds his own clarification: the verse about Moses does not mean God began speaking after silence or stopped speaking after addressing Moses. Instead, it means God removed the barrier so Moses could perceive the eternal speech. The terms 'silence' and 'speaking' are used metaphorically for the perception of speech, not for the attribute itself.

Key Terms
الحديث
التأويل
الصمت
الكلام القديم
الإدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith where God says 'Be silent' means that God Himself was once silent.
Correction: Ibn Dihāq clarifies that this does not mean God was silent; rather, it means He did not create hearing for people to perceive His speech. God's speech is eternal and cannot cease.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Dihāq's interpretation, what is the meaning of the hadith where God says 'Be silent'?
A) God Himself becomes silent.
B) God does not create hearing for the people to perceive His speech.
C) God's speech ceases temporarily.
D) God is speaking and then becomes silent.

Key Takeaway
Any apparent temporal language about God's speech must be interpreted metaphorically because God's speech is an eternal attribute that never begins or ceases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah's speech as something that 'starts' only when you open the Qur'an? If His speech is truly eternal and never silent, why don't you feel that same awe when hearing any verse as if you are hearing it directly from the Eternal? Pledge today to recite at least one verse with the conscious belief that you are hearing the very speech of the One who never ceased speaking.
?
If you truly believed that Allah's speech never began and never ends, would you treat His commands as temporary suggestions or as eternal obligations? Which command of Allah have you been hesitating to obey because you subconsciously think it belongs to a past time? Commit today to obeying one such command, knowing it is the eternal word of the Eternal.
?
The lesson says that any appearance of silence in God is metaphorical for our lack of perception. Are you willing to admit that your own 'silence' from God is not because He is silent, but because you have not removed the obstacles? What one obstacle—distraction, sin, pride—will you remove today to perceive His eternal speech?

The Impossibility of Divine Silence
Ibn DiHaq rejects divine silence based on the eternity of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn DiHaq argues that God cannot be described with silence, based on the eternity of His speech.

Core Idea
Silence is impossible for God because His speech is eternal and cannot cease.

Hadith may suggest that God 'does not speak' in certain contexts, but Ibn DiHaq clarifies that this never implies silence. He connects the attribute of speech to its eternity, drawing on the foundational kalam proof that the eternal does not cease to exist. This lesson unpacks his concise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic slowly, then the translation. Notice the key terms: القديم (eternal), الكلام (speech), الصمت (silence).
The Arabic uses the phrase 'يرجع معنى الحديث' to indicate he is interpreting a hadith.
2
Understand the logical argument
Ibn DiHaq argues: silence would require speech to cease; but speech is eternal; and the eternal cannot cease (as proven in the proof of the world's temporality). Trace each step.
The phrase 'وقد تقدّم ذكر الدليل القاطع' refers to a prior proof in kalam.
3
Apply the principle
Consider why this matters: divine attributes are not subject to change or interruption. Reflect on how this protects the doctrine of God's perfection and the consistency of His attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالَ ابنُ دهاق : ( يرجعُ معنى الحديثِ : إلى أنَّ البارى سبحانَهُ وتعالى يعلمُ ويَرى ويسمعُ ، ومع ذلكَ لا يخلقُ لهم سمعاً لخبره بأعمالهم لا أنَّ الله تعالى يجوز عليهِ أنْ يصمت ؛ فإنَّ ذلك كانَ يكون بانعدام كلامِهِ ، وكلامه سبحانه وتعالى قديم ، وقد تقدَّمَ ذكرُ الدليل القاطع على أنَّ القديمَ لا ينعدم عند ذكرنا حدوث العالم ) انتهى
Ibn DiHaq said: The meaning of the hadith is that the Creator, glorified and exalted, knows, sees, and hears, and despite that He does not create for them hearing for [their] news of their deeds — not that Allah the Exalted may be described with silence; for that would only be due to the non-existence of His speech, and His speech, glorified and exalted, is eternal, and the decisive proof has preceded that the eternal does not cease to exist when we mention the temporality of the world.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a hadith regarding God's attributes. Ibn DiHaq clarifies that the hadith does not imply that God could ever be silent. Silence would require the non-existence of speech, but God's speech is eternal (qadīm). An eternal thing cannot cease to exist, as already proven in the argument for the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam). Thus, God's speech is everlasting and cannot be interrupted by silence.

Key Terms
القديم
الكلام
الصمت
الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can be described with silence based on certain hadith expressions.
Correction: Ibn DiHaq explains that the hadith only affirms God's knowledge, not silence. Silence would require speech to cease, but God's speech is eternal and cannot cease.

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn DiHaq say God cannot be described with silence?
A) Because silence is a defect
B) Because speech is eternal and the eternal cannot cease
C) Because God always speaks to angels
D) Because the hadith is weak

Key Takeaway
God's speech is eternal, therefore silence (its absence) is impossible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Interpretation of God's Speech
Rejects the Mu'tazilite claim that God's speech to Moses was created sounds in a tree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite interpretation of God's speech to Moses is rejected as being based on a corrupt doctrine that denies eternal speech.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite view of God's speech is built on denying His eternal attribute.

Sanusi now turns to a specific case where the Mu'tazilites reinterpret a clear Quranic verse to fit their theology. By understanding their error here, we see how their denial of eternal speech leads to distorted interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's direct refutation come through.
Notice the structure: the Mu'tazilite position is stated first, then its basis is identified as a corrupt doctrine.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The passage says the Mu'tazilites interpret God's speech to Moses as creating letters and sounds in a tree for Moses to hear. This replaces the direct, eternal speech of God.
This is a crucial point: they reduce God's speech to a created event.
3
Understand the rejection
The author states that this interpretation is built upon the Mu'tazilite doctrine of denying the eternal speech subsisting in God's essence. Because they reject God's eternal attribute of speech, they are forced to reinterpret the verse.
The passage adds that this doctrine has already been refuted, showing it is not a valid position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تأويل المعتزلة كلام الله سبحانَهُ لموسى عليه السلام بخلْقِ حروف وأصواتٍ في الشجرةِ يَسمعُ منها ما أرادَ الله تعالى أن يوصلَهُ إليهِ : فبناءً منهم على مذهبهم الفاسدِ مِنْ إنكار الكلام القديم القائم بذاتِهِ تعالى ، وقد سبق ردُّ ذلكَ عليهم

And the refutation of that has already preceded against them.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite interpretation of the event of God speaking to Moses. The Mu'tazilites, denying that God has eternal speech subsisting in His essence, reinterpreted the verse 'wa kallama Allahu Musa takliman' to mean that God created letters and sounds in a tree, and Moses heard those created sounds. The author states that this interpretation is built upon their corrupt doctrine (madhhab fasid) that denies eternal speech (al-kalam al-qadim) as an attribute of God subsisting in His essence. The author then refers to a previous refutation of this position, indicating that the matter has already been settled.
