Moses Heard God's Eternal Speech: Quranic Proof and Defense
Sanusi argues from Quran and consensus for Moses hearing eternal speech, and refutes metaphorical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse about Moses and the consensus of scholars prove that he heard God's eternal speech, and why the metaphorical interpretation is refuted by the tawkid.

Core Idea
Moses' hearing of God's speech was eternal, not created; tawkid prevents metaphorical reading.

Sanusi grounds the doctrine of divine speech in a clear Quranic proof. He shows that Moses was chosen specifically to hear God's eternal speech, not a created sound. This lesson follows the scriptural and rational argument, and then examines an objection about metaphor and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verse and its implication
Read the verse from Surah al-A'raf and note the emphasis on 'My speech'. Consider why this indicates eternal speech.
2
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Follow the author's argument: if Moses only heard created speech, his distinction would be trivial. Understand why this is a strong proof.
3
Examine the objection about metaphor
The opponent suggests 'God spoke' could mean 'He created speech'. See how the author uses tawkid to refute this.
4
Consider the final point about ijtihad
The author concedes that the example from poetry is a matter of ijtihad, not a decisive refutation. Understand the nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فالذي يدلُّ عليه قوله تعالى : ﴿ إِنِّي أَصْطَفَيْتُكَ عَلَى النَّاسِ برسالتي وبكلي ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٤٤] ، وتسميته عليه السلام بكليم الله : أَنَّهُ خُصَّ بسماع كلامِ اللهِ القديمِ القائم بذاتِهِ ، وهو الذي نُقِلَ عنِ السلفِ ، ودَرَجَ  َعليهِ الخلفُ ، ودلّتْ عليه السنَّةُ والقرآنُ ، ولو كانَ اصطفاؤُهُ بمجرَّدِ سماعِهِ كلاماً حادثاً خلقَهُ اللهُ في جسمِ مِنَ الأجسامِ . . لكانَ كلٌّ مَنْ سمعَ كلاماً مِنْ مخلوق قد شاركه في ذلكَ ؛ لأنَّ الذواتِ الحادثة وصفاتها مخلوقة الله تعالى. فإِنْ أجابوا بأنَّهُ خُصَّ بخلْقِ الكلام فيما لا يُعتادُ منه الكلام . قيل لهم : وهذا أيضاً لا خصوصيَّة فيهِ ؛ لوجودِ مثلِهِ في سائر الأنبياء ، وأيضاً : فإطلاقُ ) كلَّمَ الله موسى ) بمعنى : خَلَقَ الكلام . . مجاز ، وتوكيد الفعل بالمصدر في الآية يمنعه. فإن قلت : لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ التوكيد يدفعه ؛ لوقوعِهِ معَ المجاز. ومومنه (٢): فلا ضير عليه ؛ إذ ذاك في محل الاجتهاد.
Also: what is indicated by His statement, Exalted: 'Indeed I have chosen you over the people with My message and My speech' [al-A'raf 7:144], and his designation, peace be upon him, as the interlocutor of God: that he was singled out for hearing the eternal speech of God subsisting in His essence, which is what has been transmitted from the Salaf, and the Khalaf followed, and the Sunnah and the Quran indicate it. And if his chosenness were merely by his hearing a contingent speech created by God in some body, then everyone who heard speech from a created being would have shared with him in that, because contingent essences and their attributes are created by God Almighty. If they answer that he was singled out by the creation of speech in what is not accustomed to speech from it, it is said to them: This also has no particularity in it, because its like exists among other prophets. Also, the expression 'God spoke to Moses' meaning 'He created speech' is metaphorical, and the tawkid of the verb with the infinitive in the verse prevents it. If you say: We do not concede that tawkid prevents it, because it occurs with metaphor. And from it (the example): then no harm; for that is in the realm of ijtihad.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage provides a scriptural and rational proof that Moses heard God's eternal speech, and responds to an objection. The author begins with the Quranic verse: 'Indeed I have chosen you over the people with My message and My speech,' and notes that Moses is called 'Kalim Allah' (the interlocutor of God). This indicates that he was singled out to hear God's eternal speech subsisting in His essence. The author states that this is the view of the Salaf (early Muslims) and Khalaf (later scholars), supported by Quran and Sunnah. Then a reduction ad absurdum: if Moses' distinction were merely hearing created speech, then anyone hearing speech from a creature would share in that distinction, since all created things and their attributes are made by God. If the opponent argues that the created speech was in a non-customary place (a tree), the author replies that this also is not unique, as similar occurrences exist for other prophets. Further, taking 'kallama' to mean 'created speech' is a metaphorical interpretation, and the tawkid (emphasis by using the infinitive 'takliman') in the verse prevents such metaphorical reading. An objection follows: one might say that tawkid does not prevent metaphor because metaphor can be accompanied by tawkid (quoting a poetic example). The answer is: even so, that is a matter of ijtihad (scholarly interpretation) and not decisive.

توكيد
مجاز
اجتهاد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'kallama' can always mean 'created speech' metaphorically, even with emphasis.
Correction: The tawkid (emphatic infinitive) in the verse prevents the metaphorical reading; the author argues that such a reading is refuted by the linguistic form.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what prevents interpreting 'kallama Allahu Musa' as 'He created speech for Moses'?
A) The consensus of the Salaf.
B) The tawkid (emphasis) of the verb with the infinitive in the Quranic verse.
C) The fact that other prophets experienced similar phenomena.
D) The uniqueness of Moses' prophecy.

Key Takeaway
Moses was chosen to hear God's eternal speech, not a created sound; the tawkid in the verse forbids interpreting 'kallama' as 'created speech'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's speech is eternal and uncreated, or have you casually accepted a created view without deep conviction? How does your daily recitation of the Quran reflect this belief?
?
The author argues that tawkid prevents metaphorical interpretation. When you read the Quran, do you actively seek to understand its emphatic structures, or do you gloss over them? Will you commit to studying one verse with tawkid this week?
?
Sanusi concedes that his opponent's objection is in the realm of ijtihad. Are you willing to engage with differing scholarly opinions on divine attributes without dismissing them, yet hold firm to the truth as you understand it? How can you balance humility and conviction in your aqeedah?

Moses' Honor: Hearing Eternal Speech, Not Created Sounds
Sanusi argues that Moses' honor is hearing eternal speech, not created sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that Moses' special honor comes from hearing God's eternal speech, not created sounds, and the arguments against alternative interpretations.

Core Idea
Moses' miracle is hearing God's eternal speech, not created sounds.

This passage from Sanusi's al-Muqaddimat presents a rational and scriptural proof for the Ash'ari position that the speech of God (kalam Allah) is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence. Sanusi argues that Moses' unique title 'Kalim Allah' (the one spoken to by God) is evidence that he heard God's eternal speech, and he counters potential objections with logical and linguistic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the author's argument structure become clear.
Notice the phrase 'كلام الله القديم' (eternal speech of God).
2
Identify the main claim
Find where Sanusi states that Moses was specially distinguished. What was he distinguished by?
The claim is in the sentence beginning with 'أَنَّهُ خُصَّ'.
3
Examine the evidence
Look at the scriptural verse (7:144) and the reference to consensus of salaf and khalaf. How do these support the claim?
The verse says 'I have chosen you' and the title 'Kalim Allah' implies eternal speech.
4
Understand the objection and response
Sanusi considers an objection: perhaps God created speech in an unusual body. His response is that this still isn't unique. Follow the logic.
The objection is introduced by 'فإِنْ أجابوا'.
5
Appreciate the linguistic argument
Sanusi concludes by arguing that interpreting 'God spoke' as creating speech is a metaphor (majaz), and the emphatic structure (tawkid) prevents it. Why is that important?
The emphasis with the verbal noun shows the action is real, not metaphorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فالذي يدلُّ عليه قوله تعالى : ﴿ إِنِّي أَصْطَفَيْتُكَ عَلَى النَّاسِ برسالتي وبكلي ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٤٤] (١) ، وتسميته عليه السلام بكليم الله : أَنَّهُ خُصَّ بسماع كلامِ اللهِ القديمِ القائم بذاتِهِ ، وهو الذي نُقِلَ عنِ السلفِ ، ودَرَجَ  َعليهِ الخلفُ ، ودلّتْ عليه السنَّةُ والقرآنُ ، ولو كانَ اصطفاؤُهُ بمجرَّدِ سماعِهِ كلاماً حادثاً خلقَهُ اللهُ في جسمِ مِنَ الأجسامِ . . لكانَ كلٌّ مَنْ سمعَ كلاماً مِنْ مخلوق قد شاركه في ذلكَ ؛ لأنَّ الذواتِ الحادثة وصفاتها مخلوقة الله تعالى فإِنْ أجابوا بأنَّهُ خُصَّ بخلْقِ الكلام فيما لا يُعتادُ منه الكلام . قيل لهم : وهذا أيضاً لا خصوصيَّة فيهِ ؛ لوجودِ مثلِهِ في سائر الأنبياء ، وأيضاً : فإطلاقُ ) كلَّمَ الله موسى ) بمعنى : خَلَقَ الكلام . . مجاز ، وتوكيد الفعل بالمصدر في الآية يمنعه
And also: what is indicated by His saying, the Most High: 'Indeed I have chosen you above the people by My message and by My speech' [Al-A'raf 7:144], and his naming him, peace be upon him, as the Speaker with God (Kalim Allah): that he was specially distinguished by hearing the eternal speech of God which subsists in His essence, and this is what is transmitted from the predecessors (salaf) and upon which the successors (khalaf) proceeded, and the Sunnah and the Qur'an indicate it. And if his being chosen were merely by his hearing a created speech which God created in some body, then everyone who heard speech from a created being would have shared with him in that, because created essences and their attributes are created by God the Most High. And if they answer that he was specially distinguished by the creation of speech in that which is not customarily a source of speech, it is said to them: This also has no speciality in it, because the like of it exists among other prophets. And also: the application of 'God spoke to Moses' in the meaning 'He created speech' is a metaphor (majaz), and the emphasis of the verb by the verbal noun (tawkid al-fi'l bi-l-masdar) in the verse prevents it.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from revelation and scholarly consensus that Moses' distinction (istifa') lies in hearing God's eternal speech (kalam qadim), not a created speech. The author argues that if it were merely created speech, then anyone hearing a created sound would share that honor, negating Moses' uniqueness. He then addresses an objection: perhaps God created speech in an unusual body. The response is that this still is not unique, as such miracles occur with other prophets. Finally, he notes that interpreting 'God spoke to Moses' as 'He created speech' is a metaphor (majaz), and the emphatic structure using the verbal noun (tawkid) in the Qur'anic verse prevents such metaphorical interpretation.

Correction: This passage shows that Moses heard God's eternal speech, not created sounds, as evidenced by the verse, consensus, and linguistic structure.

If the distinction between created and eternal speech remains an abstract theory for you, then your faith in God's transcendence may be shallow. Will you, from this moment, ponder the majesty of a God whose speech is not like ours, and let that awe transform how you listen to His Book?

Refuting the Tawkid Objection and the Role of Decisive Proof
Sanusi refutes an objection about tawkid and metaphor, then addresses a circularity challenge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that emphasis (tawkid) does not prevent metaphorical interpretation, and how the decisive proof (burhan qat'i) resolves the circularity objection.

Core Idea
Tawkid alone doesn't block metaphor; the real defense is prior decisive proof about speech.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a sharp objection: if tawkid can occur with metaphor in poetry, why should it prevent metaphor in the Qur'an? He distinguishes the type of tawkid and the presence of metaphorical indicators. But more importantly, he reveals the deeper logical issue of circularity, and shows that the Sunni position rests on a prior decisive proof that speech is not merely letters and sounds. This lesson unpacks that layered argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector uses a line of poetry to argue that emphasis (tawkid) can occur with metaphor (majaz). Sanusi first responds by questioning the strength of the objection.
The objector is trying to undermine the use of the Qur'anic verse as evidence for eternal speech.
2
Distinguish between literal and conceptual tawkid
Sanusi distinguishes between conceptual emphasis (al-tawkid al-ma'nawi) and verbal emphasis. He argues that only conceptual emphasis prevents metaphor in the attribution (nisba), but the dispute is about the source of speech, not the predicate.
3
Analyze the poetic example
Sanusi explains that the poetic line is a derivative metaphor (isti'ara tabi'iyya) and has an explicit indicator (qarina) of metaphor, unlike the Qur'anic verse. Therefore, the analogy fails.
The key point is that metaphor is built on forgetting the likeness, so emphasis exaggerates the identification.
4
Recognize the circularity objection and final answer
A further objection: Sanusi's answer seems circular because the opponent denies the premise. Sanusi counters that the Sunni proof is based on a prior decisive proof (burhan qat'i) that speech is not confined to letters and sounds, making the verse literal evidence.
This shows that the dispute ultimately rests on foundational epistemological commitments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ التوكيد يدفعه ; لوقوعِهِ معَ المجاز ومنه : فلا ضير عليه ; إذ ذاك في محل الاجتهاد بَكَى الْخَرُّ مِنْ عَوْفٍ وَأَنْكَرَ جِلْدَهُ وَعَجَّتْ عَجِيجاً مِنْ جُذَامَ الْمَطَارِفُ سلَّمنا دفع التوكيد المجاز ، لكن إنَّما يدفعُهُ في الآية أن لو وقع بالمعنوي الذي يدفعُ توهم المجاز في النسبة; إذْ فيها وقع النزاع في الآية، لا في المسندِ ؛ لأنَّ الكلامَ حقيقةً قد وقع ، وإِنَّما النزاعُ : ممَّنْ وقعَ ؟ قلتُ : الجواب عنِ الأَوَّلِ : أنَّ البيتَ مِنْ بابِ الاستعارة التبعية لوقوعها في الفعل ، والاستعارة مطلقاً مبنيّة على تناسي التشبيه ، حتى قال فيها طائفةٌ مِنْ علماء البيانِ : إنَّها حقيقة لغوية ، فصح التوكيد فيها للمبالغة في دخولِ المشبَّه في جنس المشبه به ، والآية لا قرينة فيها على الاستعارة ، بخلافِ البيتِ ؛ فإنَّ قرينة الاستعارة فيه : إسناد العجيج إلى ما لا يتأتى منه حقيقةً . إلا أنَّهُ لا يُسلَّمُ هذا الجوابُ مِنْ ورودِ الاعتراض عليه بالمصادرة عن المطلوب ; إذ الخصم يدَّعي أنَّ الكلام ليس إلا الحروف والأصوات ، وقد اسند في الآية إلى مَنْ لا يتأتّى منه ، فهو عندَهُ كإسنادِ العجيج في البيتِ إلى المطارفِ ، للكنَّ أهلَ السنَّةِ رضيَ اللهُ إِنَّما استدلُّوا بالآيةِ بعدَ أنْ قامَ لهم الْبُرْهَانُ الْقَطْعِيُّ عَلَى عَدَمِ انحصار الكلام في الحروفِ والأصواتِ ، فصح الاستدلال بها ، ولا يُعترَضُ بالبيتِ لما سبق ، وأيضاً فادّعاء هدم علماء اللسان بمجرَّدِ بيت شعري يحتمل أموراً . . لا يخفى ضعفه
If you say: We do not concede that the emphasis (tawkid) prevents it, because it occurs with metaphor (majaz) and among it [there is an example]: there is no harm in that, since that is in the realm of ijtihad. [Poetry:] 'The silk garment wept from 'Awf and denied its skin, and the decorated cloaks wailed a wailing from Judham.' We concede that the emphasis prevents the metaphor, but it only prevents it in the verse if it were the conceptual emphasis (al-ma'nawi) which prevents the suspicion of metaphor in the predicate attribution (al-nisba); because the dispute in the verse is about that, not about the predicate (al-musnad), because the speech (kalam) has actually occurred, and the dispute is only from whom it occurred. I say: The answer to the first is that the line of poetry is of the type of derivative metaphor (al-isti'ara al-tabi'iyya) because it occurs in a verb, and metaphor absolutely is built on forgetting the likeness, to the extent that a group of scholars of eloquence said about it: it is a linguistic reality (haqiqa lughawiyya). So the emphasis is valid in it for exaggeration in the entry of the compared into the genus of the compared-to. And the verse has no indication of metaphor, unlike the line of poetry, for the indication of metaphor in it is the attribution of wailing to that from which it cannot really come. However, this answer is not conceded because the objection upon it is circular (musadara 'ala al-matlub); for the opponent claims that speech is nothing but letters and sounds, and in the verse it is attributed to one from whom it cannot really come, so according to him it is like the attribution of wailing in the line of poetry to the cloaks. But the Ahl al-Sunna, may Allah be pleased with them, only used the verse as evidence after a decisive proof (burhan qat'i) had been established for them that speech is not confined to letters and sounds, so using the verse as evidence is valid, and one cannot object with the line of poetry due to what preceded. Also, claiming to demolish the scholars of linguistics by a mere line of poetry that allows multiple interpretations... its weakness is not hidden.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage handles an objection about the role of emphasis (tawkid) in preventing metaphorical interpretation. The objector argues that emphasis can occur with metaphor, citing a poetic example. The response distinguishes the kind of emphasis (conceptual vs. literal) and argues the verse lacks metaphorical context. The author then addresses a deeper objection: the opponent's claim that speech is only letters and sounds leads to a circularity, but the Sunni position is based on a prior decisive proof that speech is not so confined, thus the verse is valid evidence. The passage ends by dismissing objections based on poetry.

Key Terms
توكيد
مجاز
استعارة تبعية
برهان قطعي
مصادرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that poetic metaphors undermine the Qur'anic usage; the passage shows the difference.
Correction: The poetry is metaphorical with clear indicators, while the Qur'anic verse has no such indicators and is supported by a decisive proof that speech is not merely sounds.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the poetic example not analogous to the Qur'anic verse?
A) The verse uses conceptual emphasis while the poetry uses verbal emphasis
B) The verse has a clear indication of metaphor while the poetry does not
C) The poetry is metaphorical while the verse has no such indication
D) The verse is about speech while the poetry is about cloth

Key Takeaway
Sanusi defends the Qur'anic verse by showing the poetic example is metaphorical while the verse is literal, and the Sunni position is founded on a decisive proof that speech is not limited to sounds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse that attributes speech to Allah, do you truly believe He speaks with a real speech beyond letters and sounds, or do you allow yourself the comfortable notion that it is just metaphorical? Are you willing to accept the decisive proof even if it unsettles your rational assumptions?
?
The opponent reduced divine speech to mere sounds. In your own life, where have you reduced Allah's attributes to fit your limited understanding? What would it cost you to let a decisive proof reshape your belief?
?
Sanusi's method shows the importance of establishing principles before interpreting texts. When you encounter a difficult verse or hadith, do you first ground yourself in sound theological proof, or do you let your immediate perception dictate the meaning? Will you commit to seeking the decisive proof before forming your interpretation?

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Interpretation of 'Kallama'
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that 'kallama' means 'khalaqa al-kalam'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that the Mu'tazilite interpretation of 'kallama' as 'created speech' is metaphorical and leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
The dispute is not about the relation but the very meaning of speech.

Sanusi now addresses a second objection from the Mu'tazilites regarding the attribute of speech. He clarifies that both parties agree speech is attributed to God literally, but the Mu'tazilites redefine 'speech' itself as 'created speech' and the verb 'kallama' as 'to create speech'. This move, Sanusi argues, is a metaphorical usage that undermines their own position. Let's follow his careful dismantling of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the point of contention
Sanusi first denies that the dispute is merely about the relation (nisba) of speech to God; rather, it is about the predicate (musnad) — what speech actually is.
This reframes the debate.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite position
The Mu'tazilites agree that attributing speech to God is literal (haqiqa), but they reinterpret the speech itself as 'created speech' and the verb 'kallama' as 'khalaqa al-kalam' (created speech).
They make the speaker the creator of speech.
3
See Sanusi's objection
Sanusi argues that using 'kallama' to mean 'created speech' is clearly metaphorical (majaz). Emphasizing it with the verbal noun would not change that.
4
Consider the hypothetical shift
If the Mu'tazilites insist their usage is literal, then the dispute becomes linguistic, and they would have to admit that only God is a speaker in reality, since only He creates.
This follows from their own definition.
5
Dismiss their principle
Sanusi rejects their appeal to their corrupt principle about the effect of originated power (qudra haditha) as baseless.
Their principle is unsound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الثاني : منْعُ أنَّ النزاعَ إنَّما هو في النسبةِ لا المسندِ ، وذلكَ أنَّ المعتزلة موافقون على أنَّ إسناد الكلامِ إِلى اللهِ تعالى حقيقةٌ لا مجاز ، وأنَّهُ هو الذي كلَّمَ موسى لا غيرُه ، لكن تأوّلوا الكلامَ المسند إليهِ على معنى الخلْقِ للكلام ، ومعنى ( كلَّمَ ) عندهم : خلق الكلام ، والمتكلّم عندَهم : الخالق للكلام ، ولا شكَّ أنَّ استعمالَ ( كلَّمَ ( بمعنى ( خلقَ الكلامَ ) مجاز ، فتوكيده بالمصدر يدفعه وإن زعم المعتزلة أنَّ ) كلَّمَ ( بمعنى ( خلق ) هو الحقيقة ، وغيرَه مجاز . . كان النزاعُ معَهم لغوياً ، ويلزمُهم أن لا مُتكلّم حقيقةً إلا الله تعالى ؛ إذ لا خالق سواه ، ومنعهم لذلك بمقتضى أصلهم الفاسد في تأثير القدرةِ الحادثة في مقدورها لا يُسْمَعُ ؛ لفسادِهِ
And the answer to the second [objection]: denial that the dispute is only about the relation and not the predicate. That is because the Mu'tazilites agree that attributing speech to God Almighty is literal not metaphorical, and that He is the one who spoke to Moses, not another. But they interpreted the speech attributed to Him as meaning the creation of speech, and the meaning of 'kallama' with them is 'created speech', and the speaker with them is the creator of speech. There is no doubt that using 'kallama' in the meaning of 'created speech' is metaphorical, so emphasizing it with the verbal noun repels it. And if the Mu'tazilites claim that 'kallama' in the meaning of 'khalaqa' is literal, and otherwise metaphorical, then the dispute with them becomes linguistic, and it would necessitate that there is no speaker in reality except God Almighty, since there is no creator besides Him. And their denial of that based on their corrupt principle about the effect of the originated power in its object is not to be listened to, due to its corruption.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a second objection. Sanusi first clarifies the point of contention: the disagreement is not merely about the relation (nisba) of speech to God, but about the predicate (musnad) itself — what speech is. He notes that the Mu'tazilites agree that attributing speech to God is literal (haqiqa), but they then reinterpret the speech itself as 'created speech', and the verb 'kallama' as 'khalaqa al-kalam' (created speech). This, Sanusi argues, is a metaphorical (majaz) usage. If they insist it is literal, then the dispute reduces to a terminological one, and it would entail that only God can be called a speaker (mutakallim) in reality, since only He creates. Their reliance on their flawed principle of originated power (qudra haditha) affecting its object is dismissed as corrupt.

Key Terms
كلام نفسي
خلق
مجاز
حقيقة
المتكلم
القدرة الحادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the dispute with the Mu'tazilites is only about the grammatical relation of speech to God, not about the nature of speech itself.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the real point of contention is the predicate (musnad)—what speech is—not the relation (nisba).

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi say the Mu'tazilites mean by 'kallama' when attributed to God?
A) They mean God spoke directly with His eternal speech.
B) They mean God created speech.
C) They mean it is metaphorical.
D) They deny that God speaks at all.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that the Mu'tazilite reinterpretation of 'kallama' as 'created speech' is either metaphorical or leads to absurd consequences, and their underlying principle is corrupt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you truly believe you are hearing the literal speech of God, or have you unconsciously reduced it to a metaphor? Examine your heart: has this lesson exposed any complacency in how you approach the Qur'an? What would change if you took the reality of God's spoken word more seriously today?
?
Sanusi dismissed the Mu'tazilite principle as corrupt. Now, think about your own life: are there comfortable assumptions you hold about God that, if examined honestly, would lead to absurd consequences? Are you willing to abandon them for the sake of truth, even if it means admitting you were wrong?
?
The Mu'tazilites' logic implied that only God is a true speaker because He alone creates. Yet you speak every day. How does that fact—that your speech is a gift from the Creator—change the way you use your words? Will you speak with more humility and gratitude, knowing that your voice is a sign of His creative power?

Eternal Speech and Literal Interpretation
Sanusi concludes that denying eternal speech is false; literal meaning is obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi insists on taking scriptural attributions of speech to Allah in their literal sense, and the consequences of denying eternal speech.

Core Idea
Denying eternal speech is both rationally and textually false; its literal meaning is obligatory.

Sanusi concludes his discussion on divine speech with a powerful summary. He reinforces that the verse about Moses hearing speech was only to strengthen the proof for eternal speech. Now he makes two key points: denial of eternal speech is clearly false, and once established, any attribution of speech to Allah must be taken literally. This lesson unpacks these final, decisive claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's concluding tone come through.
Notice the phrase 'وبالجملة' signals a summary.
2
Identify the two main claims
Sanusi makes two connected claims: (1) denying eternal speech is false, (2) when speech is attributed to Allah, its literal meaning must be believed. Find where each appears.
The first claim is about the error of restricting speech to letters and sounds.
3
Understand the dual evidence
Sanusi says denial is 'واضح البطلان عقلاً ونقلاً' — clearly false by reason and revelation. Reflect on why both types of evidence are needed.
Rational proof ('عقلاً') and transmitted proof ('نقلاً') together establish the truth.
4
Grasp the obligation of literal meaning
Sanusi declares that when the Book or Sunnah attributes speech to Allah, it is obligatory to take it in its apparent meaning (ظاهر) as His eternal speech. This is a key methodological rule.
The word 'ظاهر' means the straightforward, literal meaning.
5
Note the warning against innovation
Sanusi strongly states that removing the word from its correct apparent meaning without necessity is an innovation (بدعة) and violates consensus. Why is this so serious?
This warning shows the high stakes of interpreting divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : فنحن لم نذكر هذه الآية إلا على سبيل التقوية لإثباتِ الكلامِ النفسي القديمِ بسماع موسى عليه السلام له ، وإلا فإنكار الكلام النفسي وحصرُهُ في الحروفِ والأصوات . . واضحُ البطلان عقلاً ونقلاً ، وإذا ثبت الكلامُ النفسي ، ووُجِدَ في الكتاب أو السنَّةِ إِسنادُ الكلام إليه تعالى .. وجبَ اعتقاد ظاهرِهِ ، وأنَّ المراد كلامه القديم القائم بذاتِهِ، والتعرُّضُ لإخراج اللفظ عن ظاهره الصحيحِ مِنْ غيرِ مُوحِب .. بدعةٌ ومخالفة لإجماع الصحابة وتابعيهم بإحسان .
In summary: We only mentioned this verse as a means of strengthening the proof of the eternal, self-subsisting speech by Moses' hearing it. Otherwise, the denial of the self-subsisting speech and restricting it to letters and sounds is clearly false rationally and by transmission. And if the self-subsisting speech is established, and in the Book or the Sunnah an attribution of speech to Him Almighty is found, then it is obligatory to believe in its apparent meaning, and that what is intended is His eternal speech subsisting in His essence. And to attempt to remove the word from its correct apparent meaning without any necessity is an innovation and a violation of the consensus of the Companions and their righteous successors.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a concluding summary. Sanusi explains that the verse about Moses hearing speech was only cited to strengthen the proof for eternal speech (kalam nafsi qadim). He asserts that denying this eternal speech and restricting speech to letters and sounds is clearly false both rationally (aqlan) and from revealed texts (naqlan). Once eternal speech is proven, any scriptural attribution of speech to God must be taken in its apparent meaning (zahir), i.e., as His eternal speech subsisting in His essence. To divert from this apparent meaning without a valid necessity (mujib) is an innovation (bid'a) and contradicts the consensus (ijma') of the Companions and their righteous successors.

Key Terms
كلام نفسي
ظاهر
بدعة
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only rational proof is needed to establish the meaning of divine attributes.
Correction: Sanusi emphasizes that the denial of eternal speech is false both rationally and by transmitted evidence ('عقلاً ونقلاً'), and that scriptural attributions must be taken literally.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi say about removing a word from its apparent meaning without necessity?
A) It is permissible if it leads to a deeper understanding.
B) It is an innovation and violation of consensus.
C) It is recommended for scholars.
D) It is obligatory to interpret metaphorically.

Key Takeaway
Once eternal speech is established, every scriptural attribution of speech to Allah must be understood in its literal sense as His eternal speech, and deviating without necessity is a serious innovation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran or hear a hadith describing Allah's speech, do you ever catch yourself mentally explaining away the apparent meaning because it challenges your rational assumptions? What necessity (mujib) do you have to do so, and are you willing to submit to the consensus of the Companions instead?
?
The Salaf took Allah's speech literally. Is there any area of your belief about Allah's attributes where you have tolerated doubt or reinterpretation? Will you repent from that today and affirm that His speech is eternal and real, even if it humbles your intellect?
?
If you were to stand before Allah and He asked you why you deviated from the apparent meaning of His speech without a valid necessity, what would you say? Are you prepared to face that question with the answer that you followed the path of the Salaf?

The Direct Speech of God to Moses: Refuting the Tawkid Objection
Sanusi argues for the immediate understanding of divine speech in the Moses verses and refutes the tawkid objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that the verses about Moses entail direct divine speech, and how he refutes the objection based on tawkid (emphasis).

Core Idea
The immediate understanding of God's speech to Moses is direct, and no tawkid objection overrides it.

This passage presents Sanusi's core argument for the doctrine of divine speech as an eternal attribute subsisting in God. He begins with the natural, unforced understanding of two Quranic verses about Moses. Then he anticipates and refutes a subtle grammatical objection based on the concept of tawkid (emphasis), showing that the apparent meaning remains intact.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation once to get the overall flow of the argument. Don't analyse yet.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very technical style with references to earlier arguments.
2
Identify the two verses and their immediate understanding
Identify the verses from Surah an-Nisa (4:164) and Surah al-A'raf (7:144). Note how Sanusi says the immediate understanding (mubtadir) is that God spoke directly with His eternal speech.
The term 'mubtadir ila adh-dhihn' is key here.
3
Understand the tawkid objection
The objector argues that the emphasis (tawkid) in the verse only affirms the predicate (musnad), not the relation (nisba). That is, it confirms that speaking happened, but not the directness. Grasp this subtle grammatical point.
The objection challenges whether the verse proves direct speech.
4
Follow Sanusi's two-part response
First, the only reason to divert from the apparent meaning is the false assumption that speech is only letters/sounds—already refuted. Second, even if tawkid doesn't affirm the relation, it doesn't require a contrary meaning, so the apparent meaning stands due to no valid diverting factor (sarif).
This demonstrates the principle that the apparent meaning is authoritative unless there is a clear diverting factor.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument is important for the Ash'ari position: it defends the doctrine that God's speech is eternal and subsisting in Him, not created or consisting of letters and sounds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا شك أنَّ المتبادر إلى الذهن لغةً وعرفاً مِنْ قولِهِ تعالى : ( وَكَلَّمَ الله موسى ) [النساء : ١٦٤] مِنْ غيرِ نظر إلى التوكيد.. أَنَّهُ كَلَّمَهُ بغيرِ واسطة ، بل بكلامِهِ القديمِ القائمِ بهِ ، وكذلك قوله : ( إِنِّي أَصْطَفَيْتُكَ عَلَى النَّاسِ بِرِسَالَتِي وَبِكَلَامِي ) [الأعراف : ١٤٤] إنَّما يتبادر إلى الذهن مِنْ هذهِ الإضافة الكلامُ القائمُ بهِ جلَّ وعلا ، لا سيما مع ما اقترنَتْ بهِ مِنِ اصطفاء موسى عليه السلام بها على الناس ، ولا مُوجِبَ لصرفِ اللفظ عن ظاهره إلا توهُمُ انحصار الكلام في الحروف والأصوات ، وقيامها بذاتِهِ تعالى محال ، فتعيَّنَ التأويل . وجوابُهُ : أَنَّهُ قد سبق بطلان هذا التوهم ، فتعيَّنَ الإيمانُ بالظاهرِ ؛ إذ لا عاضد للمرجوح ، وأيضاً : فقول المعترض : إِنَّ التوكيد في الآيةِ إنَّما يُحقِّقُ المسند ، وليس فيه وقع النزاع ، بل في النسبةِ (١) ، نقولُ : على تقدير تسليمِهِ : إن لم يُحقِّقِ النسبة فلم يقتض خلاف ظاهرِها (٢) ، فتعيَّنَ الظاهرُ ؛ لعدم الصارفِ عنه قوله : ( لاستلزام جميعِ ما ذُكِرَ التغير والحدوثَ ) قد ذكرنا وجْهَ ذلك في جميعِها مُفصَّلاً ، وبالله التوفيق (٣)
There is no doubt that what immediately comes to the mind linguistically and customarily from His saying 'And Allah spoke to Moses' (4:164) without considering the emphasis is that He spoke to him without an intermediary, but with His eternal speech subsisting in Him. And similarly His saying 'I have chosen you over the people with My messages and with My speech' (7:144) – what immediately comes to the mind from this attribution is the speech subsisting in Him, majesty and exalted, especially with what is associated with it of the choosing of Moses upon him peace over the people. And there is no reason to divert the word from its apparent meaning except the assumption that speech is restricted to letters and sounds, and their subsisting in His essence is impossible, so interpretation becomes necessary. And its answer: That the falsehood of this assumption has already preceded, so belief in the apparent meaning becomes necessary, since there is no supporter for the preponderated [view]. Also: the objector's statement that 'the emphasis in the verse only affirms the predicate, and there is no place for dispute in it, but rather in the relation' – we say: assuming its acceptance, if it does not affirm the relation, then it does not require [the meaning to be] contrary to its apparent meaning, so the apparent meaning is established due to the absence of a [valid] diverting factor. His statement: 'Because all that was mentioned entails change and temporality' – we have mentioned the explanation of that for all of them in detail. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage contains an argument and an objection-and-answer. Sanusi first argues that the immediate, natural understanding (mubtadir ila al-dhihn) of the verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses' (4:164) is that God spoke to Moses directly, without an intermediary, with His eternal speech. Similarly, the verse 'I have chosen you... with My speech' (7:144) indicates speech subsisting in God. The only reason to divert from this apparent meaning is the erroneous assumption that speech is limited to letters and sounds, which cannot exist in God. However, this assumption has already been refuted. Then an objection is raised: the emphasis (tawkid) in the verse only confirms the predicate (musnad) and does not affect the relation (nisba) – i.e., it doesn't specify who or what the speech is attributed to. Sanusi answers: even if the emphasis does not affirm the relation, it also does not require a meaning contrary to the apparent one. Since there is no valid factor (sarif) to divert from the apparent meaning, it must be accepted. Finally, a brief reference is made to the point about change and temporality, which was explained earlier in detail.

Key Terms
تبادر
توكيد
مسند
نسبة
ظاهر
صارف
كلام نفسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The emphasis (tawkid) in the verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses' indicates that the speech is not directly from God.
Correction: Sanusi explains that tawkid only affirms the predicate (the fact of speaking), not the relation (how the speech is attributed). It does not require a meaning contrary to the apparent directness.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the only reason to divert from the apparent meaning of 'And Allah spoke to Moses'?
A) The existence of a valid metaphorical interpretation.
B) The assumption that speech is restricted to letters and sounds.
C) The emphasis (tawkid) in the verse.
D) The consensus of the Companions.

Key Takeaway
The apparent meaning of the Quranic verses about Moses indicates direct divine speech, and the objection based on tawkid does not provide a valid reason to interpret otherwise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the words of Allah in the Quran, how strong is your instinct to accept the immediate, apparent meaning before philosophy or theology enters? What assumptions do you bring that might cause you to divert from the clear text?
?
The objector in this passage challenges the directness of God's speech to Moses. Are there areas in your own understanding of revelation where you are tempted to say 'this cannot mean what it says' because of a prior commitment? What would it cost you to surrender that commitment?
?
God's speech to Moses was so direct that Moses was chosen over all people because of it. Do you treat God's words to you—through the Quran—as that immediate and personal, or do you keep them at arm's length with layers of interpretation? Ask yourself: when was the last time you felt God speaking directly to you through a verse?

Objection and Answer on the Interpretation of God's Speech
Sanusi answers an objection about interpreting the verse on God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the apparent meaning of the verse about God's speech against the objection that it implies change and temporality.

Core Idea
God's speech is an eternal attribute, not confined to letters and sounds.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a key objection raised by those who argue that God's speech cannot be directly attributed to Him. The objector claims that since speech consists of letters and sounds, it cannot subsist in God, so the verse must be interpreted figuratively. Sanusi responds by reaffirming the apparent meaning and refuting the assumption behind the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the overall argument. Note the flow from objection to answer.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
Notice that Sanusi first states the objector's view, then gives his general answer, and then addresses a specific point about emphasis.
3
Understand key terms
Focus on terms like الظاهر, التأويل, المرجوح, and التوكيد. These are central to the argument.
4
Analyze the argument about emphasis
Sanusi responds to the claim that the emphasis in the verse only confirms the predicate, not the attribution. See how he argues that even if accepted, it doesn't require abandoning the apparent meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واسطة، بل بكلامه القديم القائم به، وكذلك قوله: ﴿ إِنِّي أَصْطَفَيْتُكَ عَلَى النَّاسِ بِرِسَالَتِي وَبِكَلَامِي ﴾ إنما يتبادر إلى الذهن من هذه الإضافة الكلام القائم به جل وعلا، لا سيما مع ما اقترنت به من اصطفاء موسى عليه السلام بها على الناس، ولا موجب لصرف اللفظ عن ظاهره إلا توهم انحصار الكلام في الحروف والأصوات، وقيامها بذاته تعالى محال، فتعين التأويل. وجوابه: أنه قد سبق بطلان هذا التوهم، فتعين الإيمان بالظاهر؛ إذ لا عاضد للمرجوح. وأيضاً: فقول المعترض: إن التوكيد في الآية إنما يحقق المسند، وليس فيه وقع النزاع، بل في النسبة، نقول: على تقدير تسليمه: إن لم يحقق النسبة فلم يقتض خلاف ظاهرها، فتعين الظاهر؛ لعدم الصارف عنه. قوله: (لاستلزام جميع ما ذكر التغير والحدوث) قد ذكرنا وجه ذلك في جميعها مفصلاً، وبالله التوفيق.
Rather, it is by His eternal speech subsisting in Him, and likewise His saying: 'Indeed, I have chosen you over the people with My message and My speech' – what immediately comes to mind from this attribution is the speech subsisting in Him, may He be exalted, especially with what is connected to it of Moses' being chosen by it over the people. And there is no cause to turn the word from its apparent meaning except the assumption that speech is confined to letters and sounds, and their subsisting in His essence is impossible, so interpretation is necessitated. And its answer: that the falsity of this assumption has already preceded, so belief in the apparent meaning is necessitated, since there is no support for the weaker [interpretation]. And also: the objector's statement that the emphasis in the verse only confirms the predicate, and the point of contention is not in it, but rather in the attribution – we say: assuming its acceptance, if it does not confirm the attribution, then it does not require going against its apparent meaning, so the apparent meaning is necessitated due to the absence of a diverting factor. His statement: 'Because everything mentioned entails change and temporality' – we have already mentioned the explanation of that in all of them in detail, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit regarding the interpretation of divine speech. The objector (likely Mu'tazila) argues that since God's speech cannot consist of letters and sounds (as that would entail change in Him), the verse about Moses receiving God's speech must be interpreted figuratively, i.e., the speech is attributed to God only indirectly. Sanusi first states the apparent meaning: the verse immediately brings to mind the speech subsisting in God's essence, especially because Moses was chosen by it, which supports a direct attribution. He then identifies the sole cause of the objection: the false assumption that speech is limited to letters and sounds, which cannot subsist in God. Since this assumption has been refuted earlier, we must adhere to the apparent meaning, as there is no support for the weaker figurative interpretation. Next, he addresses a specific objection about the emphatic construction (التوكيد) in the verse: the objector claims that the emphasis only confirms the predicate (the act of speaking) but does not settle the attribution (whether the speech is directly from God or from the tree). Sanusi responds that even if we accept this, the emphasis does not require going against the apparent meaning, so the apparent meaning stands for lack of a diverting factor. Finally, he notes that the objector's claim that all these considerations entail change and temporality has already been answered in detail.

Key Terms
الكلام القديم
الحروف والأصوات
التأويل
الظاهر
المرجوح
التوكيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that rejecting the idea of God's speech being composed of letters and sounds amounts to denying God's attribute of speech altogether.
Correction: Sanusi shows that speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in God, not a physical sound, so the attribute is affirmed.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the objector gives for interpreting the verse figuratively?
A) The verse is ambiguous.
B) Speech is confined to letters and sounds, which cannot subsist in God.
C) Moses did not actually hear God.
D) The emphasis in the verse does not confirm attribution.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi defends the apparent meaning of the verse by refuting the assumption that speech is limited to letters and sounds, affirming that God's speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does Sanusi's refutation challenge your own default way of thinking about God's attributes? Will you now examine other verses where your assumptions might lead you away from the apparent meaning?
?
The objector rejected the apparent meaning because of an assumption that speech is limited to letters and sounds. What would it take for you to accept the apparent meaning when no valid reason to divert exists? Can you identify one specific teaching you find difficult and apply this principle?
?
Sanusi insists that because the false assumption has been refuted, we must believe in the apparent meaning. Is there a verse or hadith that you have interpreted figuratively due to a similar assumption (e.g., about God's mercy, anger, or other attributes)? Will you now reconsider your interpretation in light of this principle, even if it feels uncomfortable? What would change in your relationship with Allah if you took these verses at their apparent meaning?

Unity of the Divine Attributes
Sanusi states the necessity of unity for each attribute and notes consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that each divine attribute is singular in kind, not multiple, and that this is agreed upon by Ahl al-Sunna except for knowledge and speech.

Core Idea
Each attribute of Allah is one, not many.

Sanusi begins by establishing a fundamental principle about the attributes of Allah: they are each one in number. There is not, for example, multiple powers or multiple wills. This may seem obvious, but it guards against certain theological deviations. The passage also notes a specific point of disagreement regarding knowledge and speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm. Notice the emphatic wording 'يجب لهذه الصفات الوحدة'.
2
Identify the ruling being stated
Sanusi says 'Unity is necessary for these attributes.' What does he mean by unity? He gives examples: one power, one will, one knowledge.
The phrase 'وكذا ما بعدها' means 'and likewise what follows' i.e., the other attributes.
3
Note the consensus and exception
The passage ends by noting that Ahl al-Sunna agree on the unity of all attributes except knowledge and speech. Ask yourself why these two might be exceptional.
This connects to debates about whether knowledge and speech are identical or distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تنمئة أحكام الصفات
ثم نقول: يجب لهذه الصفات الوحدة، فتكون قدرة واحدة، وإرادة واحدة، وعلماً واحداً، وكذا ما بعدها. ويجب لها عدم النهاية في متعلقاتها؛ فتتعلق القدرة والإرادة بكل ممكن، والعلم والكلام بجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي، وهي كل واجب وجائز ومستحيل، والسمع والبصر والإدراك - على القول به - بكل موجود.
ذكر في هذا الفصل حكمين من أحكام الصفات: أحدهما: وجوب الوحدة لكل واحدة منها. والثاني: وجوب عموم التعلق لما تعلق منها في كل ما تصلح له. فقولي: (ويجب لها عدم النهاية) أي: للمتعلق منها، وهو ما عدا الحياة.
أما الوحدة في الصفات: فهي مما لا خلاف فيها عند أهل السنة في جميعها إلا العلم والكلام.
Chapter on the elaboration of the rulings of the attributes.
Then we say: Unity is necessary for these attributes, so [there is] one power, one will, one knowledge, and likewise what follows them. And it is necessary for them to have no limit in their objects of relation; so power and will relate to every possible thing, and knowledge and speech to all divisions of rational judgment, which are every necessary, possible, and impossible, and hearing, sight, and perception – according to the view that holds it – to every existent.
He mentioned in this chapter two rulings from the rulings of the attributes: one: the necessity of unity for each of them; and the second: the necessity of universal relation for that which relates from them in everything suitable for it. So my saying: 'And it is necessary for them to have no limit' – meaning for that which relates from them, which is other than life.
As for unity in the attributes: it is a matter about which there is no disagreement among Ahl al-Sunna in all of them except knowledge and speech.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of two rulings on the divine attributes. Sanusi begins by stating that each attribute must be one (i.e., there is only one power, one will, one knowledge, etc.), and that each attribute has no limit in its scope of relation. He then specifies the scopes: power and will relate to every possible thing (ممكن); knowledge and speech relate to all three rational judgments (necessary, possible, and impossible); and hearing, sight, and perception (if affirmed) relate to every existing thing. The author notes that these two rulings are the subject of the chapter: (1) the necessity of unity for each attribute, and (2) the necessity of universal application (عموم التعلق) – each attribute applies to everything that is suitable for it. He clarifies that when he says 'no limit', he means for the relational aspect of the attributes, excluding life (since life is not relational). Finally, he notes that among Ahl al-Sunna, there is consensus on the unity of all attributes except knowledge and speech – presumably because some schools (like the Ash'aris) distinguish between the two, but the text does not elaborate here.

Misconception: Some might think that 'unity of attributes' means the attributes are identical to each other (e.g., knowledge = power).
Correction: Sanusi means each attribute is one in number (only one power, not multiple powers), not that all attributes are one and the same.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on which attributes is there disagreement among Ahl al-Sunna regarding unity?
A) Power and will

D) Life and perception

Key Takeaway
Each divine attribute is one in kind, and this is agreed upon except for knowledge and speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The unity of Allah's will means that His will is unchanging and singular. Is there a desire in your heart that contradicts what you know to be His will? Will you submit to it and ask Him to change your desire rather than hoping He changes His will?
?
Scholars debate whether Allah's knowledge and speech are distinct, yet you know that both are attributes of perfect unity. How does this knowledge challenge your intellectual pride when you encounter theological disagreements? Will you strive to learn with humility, trusting that His unity transcends human categories?

Universal Scope of the Divine Attributes
Sanusi states the necessity of unlimited scope for relational attributes and lists their objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that each divine attribute has an unlimited scope of relation, applying to everything that is suitable for it, such as power relating to all possible things.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes relate to everything they can, without limit.

After establishing that each attribute is one, Sanusi turns to their scope: they are not limited in what they relate to. Power and will relate to every possible thing, knowledge and speech relate to all rational judgments, and sensory attributes relate to all existents. He also clarifies that this ruling applies only to attributes that have a relational aspect, excluding life.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for universal scope
Read the part beginning with 'ويجب لها عدم النهاية في متعلقاتها' and try to understand the list that follows.
2
Identify the categories of relation
Sanusi gives three categories: (1) power and will relate to every possible thing, (2) knowledge and speech relate to all rational judgments (necessary, possible, impossible), (3) hearing, sight, and perception relate to every existent.
Notice the careful distinction: not all attributes relate to the same kind of objects.
3
Understand the exception for life
The passage clarifies that 'no limit' refers to attributes that have a relational aspect, and life is excluded because it is not relational in the same way.
This refines the ruling to avoid overgeneralization.

Two Rulings on Divine Attributes: Unity and Universality
Sanusi states the two rulings on divine attributes: unity and universality of relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two necessary rulings on divine attributes: their unity and the universality of their relation to all suitable objects.

Core Idea
Every divine attribute is one and relates to all suitable objects without limit.

In this passage, Sanusi presents two fundamental rulings that apply to all of Allah's attributes. Before discussing each attribute individually, he establishes these overarching principles: unity and comprehensive relation. Understanding these will help you grasp why each attribute is singular and why its scope is unlimited.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two rulings
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Look for the two 'hukms' (rulings) that Sanusi explicitly mentions. The passage clearly states: 'one: the obligation of unity for each one of them, and the second: the obligation of generality of relation.'
The rulings are mentioned at the end of the passage as a summary.
2
Understand the meaning of unity for attributes
Sanusi states that each attribute is one: power is one, will is one, knowledge is one. Reflect on why this is important in Islamic theology – it affirms that Allah does not have multiple powers or wills.
Unity here means numerical oneness, not abstract unity.
3
Understand the universality of relation
The second ruling is that each attribute relates to all objects suitable for it without limit. For example, power relates to every possible thing. Notice how Sanusi distinguishes between attributes: power and will relate to possibles, knowledge and speech to all rational judgments, and hearing/sight/perception to all existents.
4
Reflect on the exception: knowledge and speech
Sanusi notes that there is disagreement among Ahl al-Sunnah about the unity of knowledge and speech. This is a preview of a later discussion. Consider why some might argue about their unity.
This shows that even foundational principles can have nuances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تنمئة أحكام الصفات
ثم نقولُ : يجبُ لهذهِ الصفاتِ الوَحدةُ ، فتكون قدرةً واحدةً ، وإرادةً واحدةً ، وعلماً واحداً ، وكذا ما بعدها ويجب لها عدم النهاية في متعلقاتِها ؛ فتتعلَّق القدرة والإرادة بكل ممكن ، والعلم والكلام بجميع أقسام الحكمِ العقلي ، وهي كلُّ واجب وجائز ومستحيل ، والسمعُ والبصر والإدراك - على القولِ بهِ - بكلِّ موجود .
ذكر في هذا الفصلِ حُكمينِ مِنْ أحكام الصفاتِ : أحدهما : وجوبُ الوَحدة لكل واحدة منها

أما الوحدة في الصفاتِ : فهي ممَّا لا خلاف فيها عند أهل السنة في جميعها إلا العلم والكلام.
Section on elaborating the rulings of the attributes. Then we say: Unity is necessary for these attributes, so power is one, will is one, knowledge is one, and likewise what follows. And non-finiteness in their objects is necessary for them; so power and will are related to every possible thing, and knowledge and speech to all divisions of rational judgment, which are every obligatory, possible and impossible, and hearing, sight and perception - according to the view holding it - to every existent. In this section, two rulings from the rulings of the attributes have been mentioned: one: the obligation of unity for each one of them, and the second: the obligation of generality of relation for what relates from them in everything that is suitable for it. So my saying: (And non-finiteness is necessary for them) means: for what relates from them, which is other than life. As for unity in the attributes: it is something about which there is no disagreement among Ahl al-Sunnah in all of them except knowledge and speech.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a classification of two rulings concerning the divine attributes. First, the obligation of unity: each attribute (like power, will, knowledge) is one, not multiple. Second, the obligation of universality of relation: each attribute relates to all objects suitable for it without limit. For example, power and will relate to every possible thing; knowledge and speech relate to all three rational judgments (necessary, possible, impossible); hearing, sight, and perception (according to the accepted view) relate to every existent. The author clarifies that 'non-finiteness' refers to the objects of the attributes, except the attribute of life, which does not have objects in this sense. Finally, he notes that the unity of attributes is agreed upon by Ahl al-Sunnah, except for knowledge and speech, which have some disagreement (to be discussed next).

The Disagreement on Divine Knowledge and the Majority's Two Refutations
Sanusi presents Abu Sahl's view and the majority's two refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main refutations the majority used against Abu Sahl's claim that God has multiple eternal knowledges without limit.

Core Idea
The majority's two refutations: infinite regress and consensus.

Sanusi now moves from logic to a specific theological dispute about God's knowledge. Abu Sahl al-Sa'luqi, an Ash'ari theologian, proposed that God possesses infinite eternal knowledges, matching the infinity of objects known. The majority responded with two concise arguments. This lesson presents their refutations as a clear example of how theological claims are evaluated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two refutations
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice that the majority gives two numbered reasons to reject Abu Sahl's view. Mark each one.
The first refutation is about impossibility; the second is about consensus.
2
Understand the first refutation: infinite regress
The first argument says that if infinite knowledges exist, then an infinite number of things would simultaneously exist, which is rationally impossible. Why would that be impossible? The author relies on the well-known kalam principle that an actual infinite is impossible in the world.
This argument is later critiqued by Ibn al-Tilimsani, but here it is presented as a valid refutation.
3
Understand the second refutation: consensus
The second argument states that only two positions are known: affirming one eternal knowledge or denying knowledge altogether. The idea of infinite eternal knowledges falls outside this consensus, so it is unanimously rejected.
Consensus (ijma') is a strong proof in Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the structure of the argument
Notice how the passage first states the minority view, then gives two counterarguments. This is a classic dialectical format in kalam texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا العلم : فخالف فيهِ أبو سهل الصعلوكيُّ مِنَ الأشعريَّةِ ، وأثبت الله تعالى علوماً لا نهاية لعددِها، كما أنَّ مُتعلَّقاتِها كذلك ، وردَّ عليه الجمهور بوجهين :
أحدهما : أَنَّهُ يلزمُ على قولِهِ دخولُ ما لا نهاية له في الوجودِ ، وهو محال
الثاني : أنَّهُ مخالف للإجماع ؛ لأنَّ القائل قائلانِ : قائل بإثباتِ العلم القديمِ معَ وَحدتِهِ ، وقائل بنفيهِ ، أمَّا ثبوتُ علوم قديمةٍ لا نهاية لها . . فمُجمَعٌ على بطلانِهِ قالَ ابنُ التِّلِمْسانيّ : ( والردُّ الأَوَّلُ فيهِ نظر ؛ فإِنَّ الذي قام الدليلُ على استحالتِهِ : وجود حوادث لا نهاية لها ، وبيَّنوا الاستحالة فيها بوجوه لا تطَّردُ معَ فَرْضِ القدم ؛ كتقديرِ خروج بعضها عن الجملة (١) ، ونسبة الجملتين ، ولزوم تطرُّقِ الأقل والأكثر لما لا يتناهى ، فإِنَّ فَرْضَ نفْي الواجب محال ، بخلاف الحادثِ وكذلك الاستدلال بالجمْعِ بين عدم النهاية والانقضاء لا يطرد هنا ؛ لوجوبها (۲) ، وكذلك الاستدلال بأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ مسبوق بعدمِ نفسِهِ (١). فالكل مسبوق بالعدمِ ، كلُّ ذلك لا يمكن تقريره هنا )
As for knowledge: Abu Sahl al-Sa'luqi from the Ash'aris disagreed about it, and he affirmed for Allah Most High knowledges without limit in number, just as their objects are also without limit. The majority refuted him with two points: The first: that his statement entails the entry of what has no limit into existence, which is impossible. The second: that it contradicts the consensus; because the speakers are two: one affirming the eternal knowledge with its unity, and one denying it. As for the affirmation of multiple eternal knowledges without limit, it is unanimously agreed upon its falsehood. Ibn al-Tilimsani said: (The first refutation is subject to consideration; for what the proof established as impossible is the existence of temporal events without limit, and they demonstrated the impossibility in them by ways that do not apply with the assumption of eternity; such as estimating the exit of some from the totality (1), and the ratio of the two totalities, and the necessary occurrence of the lesser and greater for what is infinite. For assuming the negation of the Necessary is impossible, unlike the temporal. Similarly, the inference from the combination of infinity and termination does not apply here, because of their necessity (2). Similarly, the inference that each one is preceded by its own non-existence (1) — so all are preceded by non-existence. All of that cannot be established here.)
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-response regarding the divine attribute of knowledge. The objection comes from Abu Sahl al-Sa'luqi, an Ash'ari, who claimed that God has multiple eternal knowledges, each without limit. The majority (al-jumhur) refuted him with two arguments: (1) the claim entails an infinite number of things existing, which is impossible; (2) it contradicts consensus, as the only valid positions are either affirming a single eternal knowledge or denying it altogether. Then Ibn al-Tilimsani critiques the first argument, pointing out that the standard proof against infinite temporal events (e.g., the impossibility of infinite regress, the argument of cutting off a part, ratio of two infinites) does not apply to eternal entities. The arguments rely on assumptions like temporal succession or the impossibility of a part being separate, which do not hold for the eternal. Therefore, the first refutation is not decisive against Abu Sahl's claim. The author thus shows the nuanced nature of theological argumentation.

Key Terms
العلم
أبو سهل الصعلوكي
الجمهور
الإجماع
القدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the majority's first refutation (infinite regress) is universally accepted without nuance.
Correction: This passage does not include the nuance; later Ibn al-Tilimsani will show that the argument against infinite temporal events does not necessarily apply to eternal entities. But here it is presented as a valid refutation.

Checkpoint
What is the second refutation used by the majority against Abu Sahl?
A) It entails an infinite regress in time
B) It contradicts the consensus of scholars
C) It denies God's omnipotence
D) It leads to dualism

Key Takeaway
The majority refutes Abu Sahl by arguing that infinite eternal knowledges are both impossible in themselves and opposed to scholarly consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Take a moment to identify an area where you might be following your own opinion over the consensus, and resolve to check yourself.

Ibn al-Tilimsani's Critique of the First Refutation
Ibn al-Tilimsani critiques the first refutation as inapplicable to eternal entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn al-Tilimsani argues that the standard arguments against infinite temporal events do not apply to eternal entities, challenging the first refutation.

Core Idea
Arguments against infinite temporal events fail for eternal entities.

After presenting the majority's refutations, Sanusi includes a critical remark from Ibn al-Tilimsani, a later Ash'ari scholar. Ibn al-Tilimsani examines the first refutation—the impossibility of an infinite number of things existing—and finds it flawed. He explains that the standard proofs used to reject infinite temporal events (like cutting off a part, ratios of infinities, etc.) do not work when the series is assumed eternal. This lesson unpacks his nuanced objection.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn al-Tilimsani's statement
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice that he is specifically critiquing the first refutation ('al-radd al-awwal').
He says 'fīhi naẓar' meaning it is open to scrutiny.
2
Identify the standard arguments he lists
He mentions ways used to show the impossibility of infinite temporal events: 'estimating the exit of some from the totality', 'ratio of the two totalities', 'the lesser and greater for what is infinite', and 'each one preceded by its own non-existence'. Recognize these as objections to an actual infinite in time.
These are classical kalam arguments against infinite temporal regress.
3
Understand his point: these arguments do not apply to the eternal
Ibn al-Tilimsani argues that these proofs rely on assumptions specific to temporal events (e.g., hawaadith), such as being preceded by non-existence. For eternal beings (qadeem), those assumptions do not hold, so the first refutation fails.
This is a sophisticated logical move distinguishing between temporal and eternal infinities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا العلم : فخالف فيهِ أبو سهل الصعلوكيُّ مِنَ الأشعريَّةِ ، وأثبت الله تعالى علوماً لا نهاية لعددِها، كما أنَّ مُتعلَّقاتِها كذلك ، وردَّ عليه الجمهور بوجهين :
أحدهما : أَنَّهُ يلزمُ على قولِهِ دخولُ ما لا نهاية له في الوجودِ ، وهو محال
الثاني : أنَّهُ مخالف للإجماع ؛ لأنَّ القائل قائلانِ : قائل بإثباتِ العلم القديمِ معَ وَحدتِهِ ، وقائل بنفيهِ ، أمَّا ثبوتُ علوم قديمةٍ لا نهاية لها . . فمُجمَعٌ على بطلانِهِ قالَ ابنُ التِّلِمْسانيّ : ( والردُّ الأَوَّلُ فيهِ نظر ؛ فإِنَّ الذي قام الدليلُ على استحالتِهِ : وجود حوادث لا نهاية لها ، وبيَّنوا الاستحالة فيها بوجوه لا تطَّردُ معَ فَرْضِ القدم ؛ كتقديرِ خروج بعضها عن الجملة (١) ، ونسبة الجملتين ، ولزوم تطرُّقِ الأقل والأكثر لما لا يتناهى ، فإِنَّ فَرْضَ نفْي الواجب محال ، بخلاف الحادثِ وكذلك الاستدلال بالجمْعِ بين عدم النهاية والانقضاء لا يطرد هنا ؛ لوجوبها (۲) ، وكذلك الاستدلال بأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ مسبوق بعدمِ نفسِهِ (١). فالكل مسبوق بالعدمِ ، كلُّ ذلك لا يمكن تقريره هنا )
As for knowledge: Abu Sahl al-Sa'luqi from the Ash'aris disagreed about it, and he affirmed for Allah Most High knowledges without limit in number, just as their objects are also without limit. The majority refuted him with two points: The first: that his statement entails the entry of what has no limit into existence, which is impossible. The second: that it contradicts the consensus; because the speakers are two: one affirming the eternal knowledge with its unity, and one denying it. As for the affirmation of multiple eternal knowledges without limit, it is unanimously agreed upon its falsehood. Ibn al-Tilimsani said: (The first refutation is subject to consideration; for what the proof established as impossible is the existence of temporal events without limit, and they demonstrated the impossibility in them by ways that do not apply with the assumption of eternity; such as estimating the exit of some from the totality (1), and the ratio of the two totalities, and the necessary occurrence of the lesser and greater for what is infinite. For assuming the negation of the Necessary is impossible, unlike the temporal. Similarly, the inference from the combination of infinity and termination does not apply here, because of their necessity (2). Similarly, the inference that each one is preceded by its own non-existence (1) — so all are preceded by non-existence. All of that cannot be established here.)
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-response regarding the divine attribute of knowledge. The objection comes from Abu Sahl al-Sa'luqi, an Ash'ari, who claimed that God has multiple eternal knowledges, each without limit. The majority (al-jumhur) refuted him with two arguments: (1) the claim entails an infinite number of things existing, which is impossible; (2) it contradicts consensus, as the only valid positions are either affirming a single eternal knowledge or denying it altogether. Then Ibn al-Tilimsani critiques the first argument, pointing out that the standard proof against infinite temporal events (e.g., the impossibility of infinite regress, the argument of cutting off a part, ratio of two infinites) does not apply to eternal entities. The arguments rely on assumptions like temporal succession or the impossibility of a part being separate, which do not hold for the eternal. Therefore, the first refutation is not decisive against Abu Sahl's claim. The author thus shows the nuanced nature of theological argumentation.

Key Terms
ابن التلمساني
الرد
الحوادث
القدم
الاستحالة
النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the impossibility of an infinite series of events automatically extends to any kind of infinite, including eternal entities.
Correction: Ibn al-Tilimsani explains that the arguments against infinite temporal events depend on their temporality; they cannot be applied without adjustment to the eternal.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Tilimsani, why is the first refutation not decisive?
A) Because Abu Sahl's view is supported by consensus
B) Because the arguments against infinite temporal events do not apply to eternal entities
C) Because the majority's reasoning is logically flawed
D) Because the first refutation is not attributed to any scholar

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Tilimsani shows that the classic proofs against infinite temporal series cannot be transferred uncritically to eternal entities, thus the majority's first refutation requires reconsideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, Ibn al-Tilimsani challenges an argument that seemed sound by exposing its hidden assumption—that it applies only to temporal things. Where in your own faith or life have you accepted an argument about God without examining whether it truly applies to the Eternal? Are you willing to re-examine those assumptions today?
?
The lesson forces us to distinguish between the temporary and the eternal. Take a moment: what are the top three things you treat as permanent in your daily striving? Are they truly eternal, or will they fade? How does this distinction change what you should prioritize?
?
Ibn al-Tilimsani's critique is not just academic—it guards against a sloppy understanding of Allah's attributes. Do you ever find yourself thinking of Allah's knowledge or power as if it were like human knowledge, limited by time? What specific step can you take today to remind yourself of His eternal nature?

The Ash'ari Refutation of Infinite Divine Sciences
Sanusi records the majority's two proofs against Abu Sahl's claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs the Ash'ari majority used to refute Abu Sahl's claim that Allah has infinite sciences.

Core Idea
Infinite divine sciences are impossible by reason and consensus.

Sanusi records a key sectarian debate within the Ash'ari school. A deviant member, Abu Sahl al-Su'luki, claimed that Allah has an infinite number of sciences corresponding to infinite knowable objects. The majority rejected this with two powerful arguments: one rational and one based on consensus. In this lesson, we examine both proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the structure: the position, then the refutation with two proofs.
The passage is a clear enumeration of arguments.
2
Identify the two proofs
Look for the first proof starting with 'أحدهما' and the second with 'الثاني'. Understand each argument in its own terms.
The first proof is rational, the second is based on consensus.
3
Reflect on the consensus argument
Note that the majority limits valid positions to two: one eternal knowledge or its negation. Any third position (infinite sciences) is unanimously rejected.
This shows how consensus functions in theological argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا العلم : فخالف فيهِ أبو سهل الصعلوكيُّ مِنَ الأشعريَّةِ ، وأثبت الله تعالى علوماً لا نهاية لعددِها ، كما أنَّ مُتعلقاتِها كذلك ، وردَّ عليه الجمهور بوجهين : أحدهما : أَنَّهُ يلزمُ على قولِهِ دخولُ ما لا نهاية له في الوجودِ ، وهو محال الثاني : أنَّهُ مخالف للإجماع ؛ لأنَّ القائل قائلانِ : قائل بإثباتِ العلم القديمِ معَ وَحدتِهِ ، وقائل بنفيهِ ، أمَّا ثبوتُ علوم قديمةٍ لا نهاية لها . . فمُجمَعٌ على بطلانِهِ
As for knowledge (al-ʿilm): Abu Sahl al-Ṣuʿlūkī from the Ashʿariyya disagreed concerning it, and affirmed that Allah the Exalted has infinite sciences in number, as their objects of attachment are likewise infinite. The majority refuted him with two proofs: First, that his statement entails the entry of what has no end into existence, and that is impossible. Second, that it contradicts consensus (ijmāʿ); because the [valid] claimants are two: a claimant of the eternal knowledge with its unity, and a claimant of its negation. As for affirming infinite eternal sciences, there is consensus on its falsity.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a record of a theological disagreement about the nature of divine knowledge. Sanusi reports that Abu Sahl al-Ṣuʿlūkī, a member of the Ashʿarī school, held that God has an infinite number of sciences (ʿulūm), meaning attributes of knowledge, corresponding to the infinite number of possible objects of knowledge. The majority of Ashʿarīs rejected this. The refutation is presented in two arguments. The first argument is that accepting infinite sciences would require infinite things to exist in actuality, which is impossible (muḥāl) because an actual infinite is impossible in the created order, and positing it in God is also rejected by this reasoning. The second argument is based on consensus (ijmāʿ): there are only two valid positions on divine knowledge—either affirming a single eternal knowledge (the Ashʿarī view) or denying it entirely (the Muʿtazilī view). Affirming multiple eternal sciences without limit is a position that the community (ummah) has agreed is false. Thus the majority's proof relies on both rational impossibility and transmitted consensus.

Key Terms
العلم
أبو سهل الصعلوكي
الجمهور
الإجماع
المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think infinite sciences are possible for an infinite God.
Correction: The majority argues that an actual infinite is impossible in existence, and consensus rejects it.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof the majority uses against Abu Sahl's claim of infinite divine sciences?
A) It leads to absurdities in the order of creation.
B) It contradicts the consensus of the early Muslims.
C) It denies the unity of God.
D) It implies temporal change in God.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari majority refutes Abu Sahl's claim of infinite divine sciences using rational impossibility and consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own knowledge, it is finite and limited. The claim that God's knowledge could be broken into infinite units is refuted by reason and consensus. How does your inability to grasp God's perfect, single knowledge affect your reliance on Him? Will you today reflect on your dependence on the One whose knowledge is one and perfect?
?
The passage shows that the majority of Ash'aris did not hesitate to correct a fellow Ash'ari who deviated. Is there a belief you hold that might contradict the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna? Are you willing to examine your beliefs and accept correction if needed?
?
The proof from impossibility of actual infinite is rational. How often do you apply rigorous reasoning to your own theology, or do you accept beliefs without checking their logical coherence? What step will you take today to strengthen your understanding of the rational proofs for divine attributes?
