Defending the Unity of Divine Knowledge
Sanusi answers an objection about unity of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi resolves the apparent contradiction between the unity of God's knowledge and His knowledge of past, present, and future.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is timeless; time describes the object, not the knowledge.

A powerful objection threatens the Islamic doctrine of divine knowledge: if God's knowledge is one, how can it encompass both future and present states, which seem logically different? In this passage, Sanusi answers by clarifying that time is not a container for God's knowledge but a relation to the known object. This distinction preserves both the unity and the comprehensiveness of divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection (beginning with 'فإن قيل') and the answer. Notice the dilemma it poses: knowledge of the future entails non-existence, knowledge of the present entails existence.
The objection assumes that if knowledge is one, it cannot attach to contradictory states.
2
Identify Sanusi's key move
Sanusi answers that God knows each thing in relation to its specified time and place, and that the relation to time is an attribute of the knowledge, not a temporal container.
The phrase 'فالإضافة إلى الزمان صفة للعلم، لا ظرف للعلم' is the crux.
3
Understand the refutation of the error
The passage ends by noting that the source of the error is in how we speak about the known object. Realise that human language describes knowledge in temporal terms, but this is a limitation of expression, not of God's attribute.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
The common misunderstanding is that unity of knowledge cannot accommodate different temporal states. Sanusi shows that the relation to time is in the object, not the knower.
This lesson guards against anthropomorphic thinking about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : كيف يستقيمُ القولُ بوَحدة العلم معَ أَنَّهُ تعالى عالم بما سيكون وبالكائن ، والعلم بما سيكون مغاير للعلم بالكائن ، لأنَّ العلم بما سيكون يستلزم عدم ذلك المعلوم ، والعلم بكونه يستلزم وجودَه ، فلو كان عينه لزمَ أنْ يكونَ أحدهما تعلَّق بالشيء على خلافِ ما هو عليه ؟ فالجواب : أنَّ البارى تعالى في أزلِهِ يعلمُ وجودَ الشيء مضافاً إلى وقتِهِ المعيَّن كما يعلمُهُ مضافاً إلى محله المعيَّنِ ، ويعلمُ أَنَّهُ معدوم قبل وجودِهِ ، وإنْ كانَ ممَّا لا يبقى فيعلمُ عدمَهُ بعد وجودِهِ ، فليس علمُهُ مظروفاً بالزمان ، بل علمُهُ تعلَّق بإيجاد الموجودِ مضافاً إلى الزمانِ فالإضافة إلى الزمانِ صفةٌ للعلم ، لا ظرف للعلم ، فليس علمُهُ زمانياً فيُوصَفَ بالماضي والحاضر والمستقبل . وإِنَّما منشأ هذا الغلط : مِنْ حيثُ الإخبار عن ذلكَ المتعلَّقِ
For if it is said: How can the statement of the unity of knowledge be sound, when He the Exalted knows what will be and what is existent, and knowledge of what will be is different from knowledge of what is existent, because knowledge of what will be entails the non-existence of that known object, and knowledge of its being existent entails its existence; so if they were identical, one of them would have attached to the thing contrary to its actual state? Then the answer: The Creator the Exalted, in His eternity, knows the existence of a thing relative to its specified time just as He knows it relative to its specified place, and He knows that it is non-existent before its existence, and if it is something that does not persist, He knows its non-existence after its existence. So His knowledge is not contained by time; rather, His knowledge attaches to the existence of the existent relative to time. So the relation to time is a description of the knowledge, not a container for knowledge. Therefore His knowledge is not temporal such that it is described by past, present, and future. The source of this error is from how one informs about that object of knowledge.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (fa-in qīla... fa-l-jawāb) defending the unity of God's knowledge. The objection raises a dilemma: If God's knowledge is one, how can it simultaneously know future events (what will be) and present events (what is)? These seem to be different because future knowledge implies non-existence of the object, while present knowledge implies its existence. If the knowledge is the same, one of the two attachments would contradict the actual state of the object. Sanusi's answer (from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi or similar source) is that God in His eternity (azal) knows the existence of a thing relative to its specified time, just as He knows it relative to its specified place. He knows that it is non-existent before its time, and if it perishes, He knows its non-existence after. So God's knowledge is not 'contained' (mazrūf) in time; time is not a container for knowledge. Instead, the relation to time is a description (ṣifa) of the knowledge—that is, the knowledge itself is timeless, but it includes the aspect of temporal relation as part of its content. Thus God's knowledge does not change with time; it is eternal, and the temporal qualification belongs to the known object (al-maʿlūm), not the knowledge itself. The confusion arises from mistaking the way we describe God's knowledge (in terms of past/present/future) for a feature of the knowledge itself.

Key Terms
وحدة العلم
ما سيكون
الكائن
الزمان
المظروف
الصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That unity of divine knowledge requires God's knowledge to change with time or to be multiple.
Correction: God's knowledge is eternally one; temporal relations are properties of the known objects, not of the knowledge itself.

Checkpoint
According to the answer provided, how does God's single knowledge relate to the future and present without contradiction?
A) God's knowledge changes with time.
B) The relation to time is a description of the knowledge, not a container.
C) God has multiple knowledges for different times.
D) The future and present are really the same in God's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is one and timeless; temporal distinctions apply to the objects known, not to the knowledge itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You often think of God as 'watching over you' in time, but this lesson says God's knowledge is timeless. What would change in your daily awareness if you truly lived knowing that God sees your entire life as one reality? Can you identify one habit that would shift?
?
The error described is confusing the way we talk about God's knowledge with a limitation in God. In what area of your faith do you find yourself imposing human limits on God? What would it look like to correct that today, starting with your next thought or action?

6 min
The Unity of Divine Knowledge
Sanusi answers an objection against the unity of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge is not divided into past, present, and future, and how the verbal report is distinct from knowledge itself.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is timeless; temporal labels belong only to our reports, not to His knowledge.

This passage presents a common objection against the unity of divine knowledge: if God knows future events and present events, those knowledges seem different because one relates to something non-existent and the other to something existent. Sanusi answers by clarifying that God's knowledge is not temporal—He knows each thing in its specific time without change. The error comes from confusing the temporal verbal report with the timeless knowledge. Let's work through the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part where the objector claims that knowledge of future and present are distinct because they relate to non-existent and existent objects respectively.
Notice the objector's reasoning: if they were identical, one would have to relate to a thing contrary to its actual state.
2
Understand the answer
Sanusi explains that God's knowledge is not contained in time; He knows things attached to their specific time without implying multiplicity in His knowledge.
Key phrase: 'فليس علمه مظروفاً بالزمان' (His knowledge is not contained in time).
3
Trace the error
The error arises from mixing up the verbal report (which is temporal) with knowledge itself. Past, present, future are designations of the report, not of the knowledge.
The passage explicitly states: 'وإنما منشأ هذا الغلط من حيث الإخبار' (The origin of this error is from the aspect of reporting).
4
Examine the example
The example of Zayd's arrival shows that if you have lasting knowledge of a future event, you do not need new knowledge when it occurs. The object of knowledge remains the same.
This illustrates that knowledge of 'what will be' and 'what is' are the same knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : كيف يستقيمُ القولُ بوَحدة العلم معَ أَنَّهُ تعالى عالم بما سيكون وبالكائن ، والعلم بما سيكون مغاير للعلم بالكائن ، لأنَّ العلم بما سيكون يستلزم عدم ذلك المعلوم ، والعلم بكونه يستلزم وجودَه ، فلو كان عينه لزمَ أنْ يكونَ أحدهما تعلَّق بالشيء على خلافِ ما هو عليه ؟ فالجواب : أنَّ البارى تعالى في أزلِهِ يعلمُ وجودَ الشيء مضافاً إلى وقتِهِ المعيَّنِ كما يعلمُهُ مضافاً إلى محله المعيَّنِ ، ويعلمُ أَنَّهُ معدوم قبل وجودِهِ ، وإنْ كانَ ممَّا لا يبقى فيعلمُ عدمَهُ بعد وجودِهِ ، فليس علمُهُ مظروفاً بالزمان ، بل علمُهُ تعلَّق بإيجاد الموجودِ مضافاً إلى الزمانِ فالإضافة إلى الزمانِ صفةٌ للعلم ، لا ظرف للعلم ، فليس علمُهُ زمانياً فيُوصَفَ بالماضي والحاضر والمستقبل . وإِنَّما منشأ هذا الغلط : مِنْ حيثُ الإخبار عن ذلكَ المتعلَّقِ المخصوص بالقولِ اللفظي ، فإنْ تقدَّمَ زمنُ الإخبارِ عنه عن زمن وجودِ ذلكَ الفعل .. سُمِّيَ الإخبارُ مستقبلاً ، وإنْ تأَخَّرَ سُمِّيَ ماضياً ، وإِنْ قارنَ سُمِّيَ حالاً ، فالماضي والمستقبل والحالُ تسميات تَعرِضُ باعتبار الإخبار عنه ، أمَّا تعلُّق العلم بوجودِهِ في الزمن المعيَّنِ : فشيء واحد . ويُقرِّرُ ذلكَ : أنا لو قدَّرنا علمنا بقدوم زيد عند طلوع الشمسِ مِنْ كذا بإنباء صادقٍ ، وقدَّرنا دوام ذلك العلمِ مِنْ غيرِ أَنْ يَعرِضَ لنا سهو أو غفلة لم نحتج عند قدومه إلى تجدُّدِ علم بقدومه ، بل ما وقع هو ما علمناه قبلَ أنْ يقعَ ، فمُتعلَّقُ العلم بما سيكون والكائن هو شيء واحدٌ ؛ وهو قدوم زيد في وقتِ كذا . هذا ما يتعلق بالعلم على وجه الاختصار .
If it is said: How can the statement of the unity of knowledge be sound, given that He, exalted, knows what will be and what is, and knowledge of what will be is different from knowledge of what is, because knowledge of what will be necessitates the non-existence of that known object, and knowledge of it being necessitates its existence? So if they were identical, it would necessitate that one of them is related to a thing contrary to its actual state? The answer: That the Eternal Creator, may He be exalted, in His eternity knows the existence of a thing attached to its specific time just as He knows it attached to its specific locus, and He knows that it is non-existent before its existence, and if it is among things that do not endure, He knows its non-existence after its existence. So His knowledge is not contained in time, rather His knowledge is related to the bringing into existence of the existent attached to time; so the attachment to time is a description of the knowledge, not a container for knowledge. So His knowledge is not temporal so that it might be described as past, present, and future. Rather, the origin of this error is from the aspect of reporting about that particular object by verbal speech. If the time of the report precedes the time of the occurrence of that action, the report is called future; if it comes after, it is called past; if it coincides, it is called present. So past, present, and future are designations that occur based on the report about it. As for the attachment of knowledge to its existence in the specific time, it is one thing. And that is established by: If we suppose our knowledge of Zayd's arrival at sunrise from such-and-such through a truthful report, and we suppose the continuation of that knowledge without our being overtaken by forgetfulness or inattention, we would not need at his arrival a renewal of knowledge of his arrival. Rather, what occurs is what we knew before it occurred. So the object of knowledge of what will be and what is is one thing: it is Zayd's arrival at the time of such-and-such. This is what pertains to knowledge in brief.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the unity of divine knowledge. The objector argues that knowledge of future events and knowledge of present events are distinct because the former relates to a non-existent object while the latter relates to an existent object; if they were identical, one would have to relate to a thing contrary to its actual state. The answer clarifies that God's knowledge is not temporal—He knows things in their specific times without His knowledge changing. The error arises from conflating the verbal report (which is temporal) with the knowledge itself. The example of Zayd's arrival illustrates that knowledge of a future event does not change when the event actually occurs; the object of knowledge is the same. Thus, divine knowledge is one, and temporal distinctions apply only to the report, not to the knowledge.

Key Terms
علم
زمان
إخبار
ماضي
مستقبل
حال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine knowledge changes or is multiple because it relates to different times.
Correction: God's knowledge is timeless and unchanging; temporal distinctions apply only to the verbal report, not to the knowledge itself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the source of the error regarding the unity of divine knowledge?
A) Thinking God's knowledge is temporal
B) Confusing the verbal report with the knowledge itself
C) Denying God's omniscience
D) Assuming knowledge of the future implies ignorance

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge is one and timeless; temporal distinctions like past, present, and future belong only to the verbal report, not to God's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of your entire life is one, unchanging, and timeless, then what can possibly change His decree? How does that awareness affect the way you pray for something to happen or not happen? Do you truly submit to His knowledge, or do you still act as if your pleas can surprise Him?
?
You often think of God as knowing your past sins and your future deeds. But does your mind imagine Him 'remembering' your past and 'anticipating' your future? After this lesson, can you honestly say that you have stopped projecting human temporality onto God? What would it take for you to internalize that His knowledge is utterly beyond time?
?
When you sin, you might assume that God's knowledge of that sin is 'new' or that He now knows something He did not know before. How does the unity of divine knowledge correct that assumption? And if His knowledge never changes, what does that imply about the permanence of His forgiveness after sincere repentance? Write down one practical change you will make in your daily awareness of God's omniscience.

Divine Knowledge and Temporal Distinctions
Sanusi explains how divine knowledge relates to past, present, and future without change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine knowledge relates to past, present, and future without undergoing change.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is one and unchanging; time labels are only about reporting.

This lesson explores how divine knowledge relates to time. Sanusi clarifies that past, present, and future are not categories in God's knowledge but arise from the act of reporting. Through a thought experiment, he shows that God's knowledge of an event is one and unchanging, whether the event is yet to occur or has already happened.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the definitions of past, present, future.
The passage uses a technical style; focus on the key terms.
2
Understand the designations
Understand that past, present, future are designations based on the relation of the report to the event, not on God's knowledge itself.
These are 'عارض' (accidental) designations, not essential.
3
Analyze the thought experiment
Follow the thought experiment about Zayd's coming. See how it demonstrates that knowledge does not change when the event occurs.
The experiment assumes a true report and continuous knowledge.
4
Reflect on the implication
Reflect on why this matters for divine attributes: it preserves God's immutability.
God's knowledge is perfect and does not gain or lose information.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المخصوص بالقول اللفظي ، فإن تقدَّم زمن الإخبار عنه عن زمن وجود ذلك الفعل .. سُمِّي الإخبار مستقبلاً ، وإن تأخَّر سُمِّي ماضياً ، وإن قارن سُمِّي حالاً ، فالماضي والمستقبل والحال تسميات تعرض باعتبار الإخبار عنه ، أما تعلُّق العلم بوجوده في الزمن المعيَّن : فشيء واحد ويقرِّر ذلك : أنا لو قدَّرنا علمنا بقدوم زيد عند طلوع الشمس من كذا بإنباء صادق ، وقدَّرنا دوام ذلك العلم من غير أن يعرض لنا لم نحتج عند قدومه إلى تجدُّد علم بقدومه ، بل ما وقع هو ما علمناه قبل أن يقع ، فمُتعلَّق العلم بما سيكون والكائن هو شيء واحد ؛ وهو قدوم زيد في وقت كذا هذا ما يتعلق بالعلم على وجه الاختصار .
The specific [action] by verbal statement: if the time of reporting precedes the time of the existence of that action, the report is called future; if it follows, it is called past; if it coincides, it is called present. So past, future, and present are designations that arise by virtue of reporting about it. As for the relation of knowledge to its existence in a specific time, it is one thing. He establishes that: if we suppose our knowledge of Zayd's coming at sunrise from such-and-such by a true report, and we suppose the continuance of that knowledge without any change occurring to us, we would not need at the time of his coming a new knowledge of his coming. Rather, what occurred is exactly what we knew before it occurred. So the object of knowledge of what will be and what is is one thing: namely, Zayd's coming at a particular time. This is what pertains to knowledge in brief.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of how divine knowledge relates to temporal events without change. It begins by defining the designations past, present, and future as relative to the act of reporting: if the report precedes the event it is future, if it follows it is past, if it coincides it is present. The key point is that these designations are not inherent to the event or to God's knowledge, but are merely based on the temporal relation of the report. Then the author establishes that the object of knowledge itself, such as Zayd's coming at a specific time, is one and the same regardless of when it is known. Using a thought experiment: if we had a true report of Zayd's coming and our knowledge continued unchanged, we would not need a new piece of knowledge at the actual moment of his coming—what happens is exactly what we already knew. Thus divine pre-science does not imply change; the object of knowledge is one. The passage concludes that this is a summary of the matter concerning knowledge.

Misconception: God's knowledge changes when events move from future to past.
Correction: The passage teaches that past, present, future are designations based on reporting, not on God's knowledge itself. God's knowledge of an event is one and unchanging regardless of time.

Checkpoint

A) Because the events themselves change over time
B) Because of the relation of the report to the event
C) Because God's knowledge changes
D) Because of the inherent nature of time

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is immutable; temporal labels like past and future apply only to our reporting, not to His knowledge.

Unity of Divine Speech (Kalam) in Sunni Theology
Sanusi defines divine speech as one eternal attribute with multiple aspects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to the Sunni position, God's speech is one eternal attribute that encompasses all specific types of speech, such as command and prohibition, which are identical in essence.

Core Idea
God's speech is one; command and prohibition are the same eternal reality.

This passage from Sanusi presents the foundational Sunni (Ash'ari) doctrine of divine speech. Before we explore disagreements, we must grasp the core claim: that God's speech is a single, eternal attribute that manifests in various forms like commands and prohibitions, but these are not separate entities. Understanding this unity is crucial for avoiding misconceptions about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the main claim
Look for the assertion that divine speech is one and eternal. Then find the list of types of speech (command, prohibition, news, etc.).
3
Understand the identity statement
Focus on the sentence: 'the very essence of His command is the very essence of His prohibition'. This means all these types are not separate attributes but are the same one speech.
This is the key point that distinguishes the Sunni view from others.
4
Note the opposing view
Sanusi mentions that 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Kullabi held that these are multiple. This will be discussed later, but for now, note that the mainstream Sunni stance is unity.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does it matter that God's speech is one? How does this protect the concept of tawhid? The commentary will help clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام : فالذي عليه أكثر أهل السنة : أنه كلام واحد متعلق متعلقات الكلام ، وهو مع وحدته وقدمه : ونهي ، وخبر واستخبار ، ووعد ووعيد ، ونداء ، وغير ذلك من معاني الكلام ، وليس كل واحد من هذه معنى يقوم بالذات ليس هو الآخر ، بل عين أمره تعالى هو عين نهيه وعين خبره وعين غير ذلك من معاني الكلام وذهب عبد الله بن سعيد الكلابي إلى تعدده على ما سيأتي تحقيق قوله بعد إن شاء الله تعالى ، هذا ما يتعلق بوحدة الصفات .
As for speech: the position of most of the People of the Sunnah is that it is one speech related to all objects of speech, and with its unity and eternity it is: prohibition, news, interrogation, promise, threat, call, and other meanings of speech. Not each of these is a meaning subsisting in the essence that is not the other. Rather, the very essence of His command is the very essence of His prohibition, and the very essence of His news, and the very essence of other meanings of speech. 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Kullabi held that it is multiple, as will come later in the verification of his statement, God willing. This is what pertains to the unity of attributes.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunni (Ash'ari) position on the attribute of divine speech (الكلام). The main assertion is that God's speech is one eternal attribute (كلام واحد قديم) that comprises all the specific types of speech such as command, prohibition, news, interrogation, promise, threat, and call. These are not separate, distinct meanings or attributes subsisting in the divine essence; rather, each is identical with the one speech. In other words, the essence of God's command is the same essence as His prohibition and His news. The author then notes that 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Kullabi (known for the Kullabi school) held that these are multiple attributes, but this will be discussed later. The passage ends by stating that this is what concerns the unity of the attributes.

The Universality of Divine Power and Will
Sanusi explains the universality of power and will with a crucial qualification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attributes of power and will relate to all possible things, including those that never occur, and why the qualification 'with respect to its essence' is necessary.

Core Idea
Divine power and will extend to every possible thing, even those known not to happen.

Sanusi deepens our understanding of divine power and will by explaining their universal scope. While it may seem contradictory that God's power and will relate to things that He knows will never happen, this passage clarifies that such things are possible in themselves and thus remain objects of power and will. The key is the distinction between intrinsic possibility and extrinsic impossibility due to divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The passage states that power and will relate to every possible thing. Understand what this means: power is the attribute by which every possible thing can be brought into existence, and will is the attribute by which every possible thing is specified.
Note that 'every possible thing' includes things that never occur.
2
Understand the qualification 'with respect to its essence'
The author adds this qualification to include things that are possible only through another, not by themselves. The example given is an event that God knows will not occur. Ask yourself: Why is this qualification needed?
It preserves the intrinsic possibility of such events, even though their occurrence is impossible due to God's knowledge.
3
Recognize the disagreement among scholars
The passage ends by noting a disagreement about whether the relation of power applies to what God knows will not occur. This shows that the issue was debated among theologians.
The text is cut off, but the core point is that the intrinsic possibility remains, so the object is still an object of power and will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان عموم تعلقات الصفات التي لها تعلق وأما عموم التعلق لها : فمعناه : أن كل صفة من الصفات المتعلقة فهي تتعلق بجميع ما تصلح له ، وقد فسرنا ذلك في أصل العقيدة : تعلقات صفات التأثير ( القدرة والإرادة ) فقولنا : ( فتتعلق القدرة والإرادة بكل ممكن ) معناه : أن القدرة صفة يتأتى بها إيجاد كل ممكن ، والإرادة صفة يتأتى بها تخصيص كل ممكن بالنظر إلى ذاته وإنما قلنا : ( بالنظر إلى ذاته ) ليدخل ما لا يتأتى إيجاده ولا تخصيصه من الممكنات لكن لا بالنظر إلى ذاته ، بل بالنظر إلى غيره ؛ وذلك كتعلق علم الله تعالى بعدم وقوعه ؛ فإنه وإن استحال معه وقوع الممكن .. لا يمنع من كونه متعلقاً للقدرة والإرادة عند المحققين ، كما لا يمنعه ذلك من وصفه بالإمكان وقد اختلف في إطلاق تعلق القدرة على ما علم الله تعالى أنه
Explanation of the universality of the relations of attributes that have relation. As for the universality of relation: its meaning is that every one of the related attributes relates to everything for which it is suitable. We have explained this in the foundation of the creed: relations of the attributes of effect (power and will). So our statement: (Power and will relate to every possible thing) means that power is an attribute by which the bringing into existence of every possible thing is accomplished, and will is an attribute by which the specification of every possible thing with respect to its essence is accomplished. We only said (with respect to its essence) to include that whose bringing into existence and specification is not accomplished from possible things, but not with respect to its essence, rather with respect to something else. That is like the relation of God's knowledge to its non-occurrence. For although the occurrence of the possible is impossible with it, that does not prevent it from being an object of power and will according to the verifiers, just as that does not prevent it from being described as possible. And there is disagreement concerning applying the relation of power to that which God knows that
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of the universality (عموم) of the objects of divine attributes that have a relation to creation, specifically power (القدرة) and will (الإرادة). It begins by stating that each attribute relates to everything that is suitable for it. The author then clarifies the meaning of the statement 'power and will relate to every possible thing (كل ممكن)': power is the attribute by which any possible thing can be brought into existence, and will is the attribute by which any possible thing is specified (i.e., distinguished from others) in terms of its essence. The qualification 'with respect to its essence (بالنظر إلى ذاته)' is crucial: it allows us to include things that cannot be brought into existence or specified by themselves, but only in relation to something else. The example given is God's knowledge that something will not occur. Although the occurrence of a possible thing becomes impossible in light of that knowledge (because God knows it will not happen), the thing itself remains a possible object of power and will. The verifiers (المحققون) hold that the impossibility is not intrinsic, so the thing is still possible in itself and thus an object of power and will. The passage ends noting a disagreement about applying the relation of power to that which God knows will not happen (likely referring to whether the relation is actual or potential).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Things God knows will not happen are impossible even in themselves.
Correction: They remain possible in themselves (intrinsically possible), but their occurrence is precluded by God's knowledge. They are still objects of power and will.

Checkpoint

A) To exclude things that are impossible

D) To restrict power to things that actually exist

Key Takeaway
Divine power and will extend to all possible things, including those known not to occur, because they are possible in themselves and the extrinsic impossibility does not remove their intrinsic possibility.

The Unity of God’s Attribute of Speech
Sanusi affirms the unity of divine speech, rejecting Ibn Kullab's multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Ash'ari theology, God's speech is one eternal attribute, and that His command, prohibition, and report are identical in essence, not multiple.

Core Idea
God’s command, prohibition, and report are one and the same essence.

Sanusi now addresses a crucial point about the divine attribute of speech. While we often distinguish between commands, prohibitions, and reports, he insists that in God, all these are fundamentally one and the same attribute. This passage establishes the Ash'ari position against the view of Ibn Kullab, who held that they are multiple.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the strong affirmation 'عين أمره تعالى هو عين نهيه' and how it equates command and prohibition.
Pay attention to the word 'عين' which means 'essence' or 'very self'.
2
Identify the claim
Sanusi asserts that the essence of command is the essence of prohibition and report. What does this imply about the nature of God's speech?
Consider how this differs from our ordinary experience of speech.
3
Contrast with Ibn Kullab
The passage mentions that Ibn Kullab held the view of multiplicity. Why might someone think that command and prohibition are distinct attributes?
Think about the apparent differences in meaning between a command and a prohibition.
4
Reflect on the deferral
Sanusi says he will verify Ibn Kullab's statement later. Why might he bring it up now without a full refutation?
This shows the need to first establish the correct position before addressing errors.

This passage concludes a discussion on the unity of divine attributes, specifically the attribute of speech. The author states that God's command ('amr'), prohibition ('nahy'), report ('khabar'), and other forms of speech are all identical in essence ('ayn')—they are not separate attributes or multiple instances. Then he notes that Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Kullabi held the view that the divine speech is multiple (i.e., the command is distinct from the prohibition, etc.), but the author defers the full refutation to a later section. This establishes the Ash'ari position that the attribute of speech is one eternal attribute with different aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read God's command to pray and His prohibition of sin, do you treat them as separate divine directives or as manifestations of one eternal attribute? How does this understanding affect the weight you give to each?
?
If God's speech is one essence, then every word He speaks carries the same divine authority. Which of God's commands do you tend to take less seriously, and how can you realign your heart to honor His speech equally?
?
The author says God's command and prohibition are identical in essence. How does this truth challenge the way you sometimes view the Qur'an as a collection of separate rules rather than a single unified revelation?

Divine Attributes

The Generality of Divine Attributes' Relations
Sanusi defines the generality of relation for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the generality of relation for divine attributes, particularly power and will.

Core Idea
Every relational divine attribute relates to everything it is suitable for.

This lesson establishes the foundational principle that attributes like power and will have a universal scope. Sanusi defines the generality of relation and clarifies that power and will relate to every possible thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of generality
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi defines 'generality of relation' and gives the example of power and will.
Notice that he emphasizes 'every attribute among the relational attributes'.
2
Understand the explanation of power and will
Focus on the statement that power and will relate to every possible thing. Understand what 'possible thing' means here.
3
Note the qualification 'with respect to its essence'
Sanusi adds a qualification about 'with respect to its essence'. This will be crucial for the next lesson.
Don't worry about fully understanding the nuance yet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان عموم تعلقاتِ الصفاتِ التي لها تعلُّق ] وأما عموم التعلَّقِ لها : فمعناه : أنَّ كلَّ صفةٍ مِنَ الصفاتِ المتعلّقة فهي تتعلق بجميع ما تصلح له ، وقد فسرنا ذلك في أصل العقيدة : تعلقات صفاتِ التأثير ( القدرة والإرادة ) ] فقولنا : ( فتتعلَّقُ القدرة والإرادة بكل ممكن ) معناه : أنَّ القدرة صفةٌ يتأتى بها إيجاد كلَّ ممكن ، والإرادة صفةٌ يتأتى بها تخصيص كل ممكن بالنظر إلى ذاتِهِ • وإنما قلنا : ( بالنظر إلى ذاتِهِ ( ليدخل ما لا يتأتى إيجاده ولا تخصيصُهُ مِنَ الممكناتِ لكن لا بالنظر إلى ذاتِهِ ، بل بالنظر إلى غيره ؛ وذلك كتعلَّقِ علم الله تعالى بعدم وقوعِهِ ؛ فَإِنَّهُ وإِنِ استحالَ معَهُ وقوع الممكن .. لا يَمنعُ مِنْ كونِهِ مُتعلقاً للقدرة والإرادة عندَ المحققينَ ، كما لا يَمنعُهُ ذلكَ مِنْ وصفِهِ بالإمكانِ وقد اختُلِفَ في إطلاقِ تعلُّقِ القدرة على ما علمَ اللهُ تعالى أَنَّهُ لا يقع ـ كإيمان أبي لهب مثلاً ـ على قولين(۱) ، وقد وَفَّقَ الغزالي بينهما على معنى : أَنَّ مَنْ قالَ بالتعلقِ فبالنظر إلى إمكانِهِ في ذاتِهِ ، ومَنْ قالَ بنفي التعلقِ فبالنظر إلى تعلقِ العلم بعدم وقوعه(٢) . واستدلَّ مَنْ قالَ بتعلُّقِ القدرة بهذا النوع : بأنَّه لو لم تتعلَّقِ القدرة بالشيء لأجل تعلَّقِ العلم بعدم وقوعه.. للزِمَ ألا يكون للقدرة متعلق ، والتالي باطل بالإجماع ، فالمقدَّمُ مثلُهُ وبيانُ الملازمة : أنَّ الممكن : إمَّا واجب الوقوع إن تعلق علم الله تعالى بوقوعِهِ ، أو مستحيلُهُ إنْ تعلَّقَ علمُهُ جلَّ وعلا بعدم وقوعِهِ ، فلو منعت الاستحالةُ العارضةُ مِنْ تعلَّقِ القدرة.. لمنع منه الوجوب العارضُ ؛ إذْ هما في المنع مِنْ تعلَّقِ القدرة سواء ويدخلُ في الممكناتِ التي تتعلَّقُ بها قدرة الله تعالى وإرادته : الممكنات الصادرة عن الحيوانات بالاختيار ؛ فإنَّها عند أهل السنَّةِ صادرةٌ بمخض قدرة الله تعالى وإرادتِهِ ، لا تأثير للحيوان في شيء منها ، وقد خالفتِ المعتزلة في ذلك ، وسيأتي الردُّ عليهم إِنْ شاءَ اللهُ تعالى
Statement of the generality of the relations of the attributes that have relation. As for the generality of relation to them: its meaning is that every attribute among the relational attributes relates to everything it is suitable for. And we have explained that in the foundation of the creed: the relations of the attributes of influence (power and will). So our statement: 'Power and will relate to every possible thing' means that power is an attribute by which every possible thing can be brought into existence, and will is an attribute by which every possible thing can be specified with respect to its essence. And we only said 'with respect to its essence' to include what cannot be brought into existence nor specified from among possible things, but not with respect to its essence, rather with respect to other than it; and that is like the relation of God's knowledge to its non-occurrence; for even if the occurrence of the possible is impossible with it, it does not prevent it from being related to power and will according to the verifiers, just as that does not prevent it from being described as possible. And there is disagreement regarding the application of the relation of power to what God knows will not occur—like the faith of Abu Lahab for example—on two opinions. And al-Ghazali reconciled between them on the meaning that whoever said it relates, it is with regard to its possibility in itself, and whoever denied relation, it is with regard to the knowledge of its non-occurrence. And he who said that power relates to this type argued: if power were not related to a thing because of the knowledge of its non-occurrence, then power would have no object of relation, and the consequent is false by consensus, so the antecedent is likewise false. And the demonstration of the implication: the possible is either necessarily occurring if God's knowledge relates to its occurrence, or impossible if His knowledge relates to its non-occurrence. So if the accidental impossibility prevents power from relating, then the accidental necessity would also prevent it; for they are equal in preventing power from relating. And included among the possible things to which God's power and will relate are the possible things that issue from animals by choice; for they, according to the People of the Sunnah, issue solely by God's power and will, with no effect of the animal in any of them. And the Mu'tazila have opposed this, and the refutation of them will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage explains the principle that every attribute of God that has a relation (like power, will, knowledge) relates to all that it is suitable for. The author focuses on the attributes of power (qudra) and will (irāda), stating that they relate to every possible thing (kull mumkin). However, an important nuance is added: 'with respect to its essence' (bi al-naẓar ilā dhātihi). This is to include things that are impossible in terms of actual occurrence due to God's foreknowledge (like Abu Lahab's unbelief), but are possible in themselves. The passage then addresses a disagreement: whether power relates to such things. Two views exist, and al-Ghazali reconciles them by noting that relation can be considered possible (ṣulūḥī) or actual (bi al-fiʿl). The author then provides a proof for the view that power does relate: if power were not related to something because God knows it won't happen, then power would have no object at all, which is absurd. The proof uses the premise that every possible is either necessarily occurring or necessarily non-occurring due to God's knowledge; if impossibility prevents relation, then necessity would also prevent it, but power does relate to necessary things in terms of possibility, so it must also relate to impossibilities in terms of possibility. Finally, the passage extends this to animal actions: they are also under God's power and will, not produced by the animals themselves, contrary to the Mu'tazilites. The author promises later refutation.

Key Terms
عموم التعلق
الممكن
القدرة
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi mean by 'generality of relation' for divine attributes?
A) Each attribute relates to some things it is suitable for.
B) Each attribute relates to everything it is suitable for.
C) Power and will relate to possible things only.
D) Attributes have no relation.

Key Takeaway
The generality of relation means that each relational attribute, like power and will, relates to everything it is suitable for.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day, do you consciously recognize that God's power and will encompass every possible outcome? How does that change your attitude towards future events?
?
If God's will specifies every possible thing, what does that mean for your personal choices? Do you surrender your will to His, or do you resist?
?
Think of a situation where you felt constrained by circumstances. Does this lesson remind you that God's power relates to all possibles? How can you actively rely on that truth today?

Power and Will: Relating to All Possibles, Including Those Known Not to Occur
Sanusi explains the qualification and the disagreement on power's relation to things known not to occur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why power and will relate to things that God knows will not occur, and the reconciliation by al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
God's power relates to all possibles even if He knows they will not happen.

This lesson explores a nuanced issue: does God's power relate to things like Abu Lahab's faith, which He knows will never occur? Sanusi explains the qualification 'with respect to its essence' and presents the disagreement, including al-Ghazali's reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the qualification 'with respect to its essence'
Read how Sanusi uses this phrase to include things that cannot be actualized due to God's knowledge, but are possible in themselves.
This is key to the whole discussion.
2
Learn about the case of things known not to occur
Focus on the example of Abu Lahab and the two opinions mentioned.
3
Study the two opinions and Ghazali's reconciliation
Read how al-Ghazali reconciles the views by considering possibility in itself versus actual occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's power cannot extend to things He knows will not happen because they become impossible.
Correction: The passage explains that such things are still possible in themselves and God's power relates to them in that sense; only actual occurrence is impossible due to knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali's reconciliation, why do some say power relates to things like Abu Lahab's faith?
A) Because God's knowledge does not prevent power.
B) Because it is possible in itself.
C) Because Abu Lahab's faith could happen.
D) Because the denied view is wrong.

Key Takeaway
God's power relates to all possibles in terms of their intrinsic possibility, regardless of His knowledge of their non-occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

How does the teaching that possibility remains intact despite foreknowledge transform your view of repentance and divine mercy? What step can you take today that reflects trust in God's absolute power over your future?

The Proof for Power's Relation to All Possibles
Sanusi provides a logical proof for the relation of power to all possibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God's power must relate to all possible things, including those He knows will not occur.

Core Idea
If power did not relate to some possibles, it would have no object at all.

Sanusi presents a proof for the view that power relates to things known not to occur. The proof uses a reductio ad absurdum: if power were not related due to knowledge of non-occurrence, then it would have no object, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Carefully read the argument Sanusi presents. It is a logical proof with a conditional and its implication.
2
Understand the implication and the premises
Identify the premise: if power were not related to a thing because of knowledge of non-occurrence, then power would have no object. Then the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false.
3
Connect to the concept of accidental necessity
Notice how Sanusi uses the idea that if accidental impossibility prevents relation, then accidental necessity would also prevent it, but that is not the case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان عموم تعلقاتِ الصفاتِ التي لها تعلُّق ] وأما عموم التعلَّقِ لها : فمعناه : أنَّ كلَّ صفةٍ مِنَ الصفاتِ المتعلّقة فهي تتعلق بجميع ما تصلح له ، وقد فسرنا ذلك في أصل العقيدة : تعلقات صفاتِ التأثير ( القدرة والإرادة ) ] فقولنا : ( فتتعلَّقُ القدرة والإرادة بكل ممكن ) معناه : أنَّ القدرة صفةٌ يتأتى بها إيجاد كلَّ ممكن ، والإرادة صفةٌ يتأتى بها تخصيص كل ممكن بالنظر إلى ذاتِهِ • وإنما قلنا : ( بالنظر إلى ذاتِهِ ( ليدخل ما لا يتأتى إيجاده ولا تخصيصُهُ مِنَ الممكناتِ لكن لا بالنظر إلى ذاتِهِ ، بل بالنظر إلى غيره ؛ وذلك كتعلَّقِ علم الله تعالى بعدم وقوعِهِ ؛ فَإِنَّهُ وإِنِ استحالَ معَهُ وقوع الممكن .. لا يَمنعُ مِنْ كونِهِ مُتعلقاً للقدرة والإرادة عندَ المحققينَ ، كما لا يَمنعُهُ ذلكَ مِنْ وصفِهِ بالإمكانِ وقد اختُلِفَ في إطلاقِ تعلُّقِ القدرة على ما علمَ اللهُ تعالى أَنَّهُ لا يقع ـ كإيمان أبي لهب مثلاً ـ على قولين(۱) ، وقد وَفَّقَ الغزالي بينهما على معنى : أَنَّ مَنْ قالَ بالتعلقِ فبالنظر إلى إمكانِهِ في ذاتِهِ ، ومَنْ قالَ بنفي التعلقِ فبالنظر إلى تعلقِ العلم بعدم وقوعه(٢) . واستدلَّ مَنْ قالَ بتعلُّقِ القدرة بهذا النوع : بأنَّه لو لم تتعلَّقِ القدرة بالشيء لأجل تعلَّقِ العلم بعدم وقوعه.. للزِمَ ألا يكون للقدرة متعلق ، والتالي باطل بالإجماع ، فالمقدَّمُ مثلُهُ وبيانُ الملازمة : أنَّ الممكن : إمَّا واجب الوقوع إن تعلق علم الله تعالى بوقوعِهِ ، أو مستحيلُهُ إنْ تعلَّقَ علمُهُ جلَّ وعلا بعدم وقوعِهِ ، فلو منعت الاستحالةُ العارضةُ مِنْ تعلَّقِ القدرة.. لمنع منه الوجوب العارضُ ؛ إذْ هما في المنع مِنْ تعلَّقِ القدرة سواء ويدخلُ في الممكناتِ التي تتعلَّقُ بها قدرة الله تعالى وإرادته : الممكنات الصادرة عن الحيوانات بالاختيار ؛ فإنَّها عند أهل السنَّةِ صادرةٌ بمخض قدرة الله تعالى وإرادتِهِ ، لا تأثير للحيوان في شيء منها ، وقد خالفتِ المعتزلة في ذلك ، وسيأتي الردُّ عليهم إِنْ شاءَ اللهُ تعالى

As for the generality of relation to them: its meaning is that every attribute among the relational attributes relates to everything it is suitable for. And we have explained that in the foundation of the creed: the relations of the attributes of influence (power and will). So our statement: 'Power and will relate to every possible thing' means that power is an attribute by which every possible thing can be brought into existence, and will is an attribute by which every possible thing can be specified with respect to its essence. And we only said 'with respect to its essence' to include what cannot be brought into existence nor specified from among possible things, but not with respect to its essence, rather with respect to other than it; and that is like the relation of God's knowledge to its non-occurrence; for even if the occurrence of the possible is impossible with it, it does not prevent it from being related to power and will according to the verifiers, just as that does not prevent it from being described as possible. And there is disagreement regarding the application of the relation of power to what God knows will not occur—like the faith of Abu Lahab for example—on two opinions. And al-Ghazali reconciled between them on the meaning that whoever said it relates, it is with regard to its possibility in itself, and whoever denied relation, it is with regard to the knowledge of its non-occurrence. And he who said that power relates to this type argued: if power were not related to a thing because of the knowledge of its non-occurrence, then power would have no object of relation, and the consequent is false by consensus, so the antecedent is likewise false. And the demonstration of the implication: the possible is either necessarily occurring if God's knowledge relates to its occurrence, or impossible if His knowledge relates to its non-occurrence. So if the accidental impossibility prevents power from relating, then the accidental necessity would also prevent it; for they are equal in preventing power from relating. And included among the possible things to which God's power and will relate are the possible things that issue from animals by choice; for they, according to the People of the Sunnah, issue solely by God's power and will, with no effect of the animal in any of them. And the Mu'tazila have opposed this, and the refutation of them will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage explains the principle that every attribute of God that has a relation (like power, will, knowledge) relates to all that it is suitable for. The author focuses on the attributes of power (qudra) and will (irāda), stating that they relate to every possible thing (kull mumkin). However, an important nuance is added: 'with respect to its essence' (bi al-naẓar ilā dhātihi). This is to include things that are impossible in terms of actual occurrence due to God's foreknowledge (like Abu Lahab's unbelief), but are possible in themselves. The passage then addresses a disagreement: whether power relates to such things. Two views exist, and al-Ghazali reconciles them by noting that relation can be considered possible (ṣulūḥī) or actual (bi al-fiʿl). The author then provides a proof for the view that power does relate: if power were not related to something because God knows it won't happen, then power would have no object at all, which is absurd. The proof uses the premise that every possible is either necessarily occurring or necessarily non-occurring due to God's knowledge; if impossibility prevents relation, then necessity would also prevent it, but power does relate to necessary things in terms of possibility, so it must also relate to impossibilities in terms of possibility. Finally, the passage extends this to animal actions: they are also under God's power and will, not produced by the animals themselves, contrary to the Mu'tazilites. The author promises later refutation.

Key Terms
الملازمة
الوجوب العارض
الاستحالة العارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the proof?
A) Power does not relate to things known not to occur.
B) Power must relate to all possibles, including those known not to occur.
C) Power has no object.
D) Accidental impossibility prevents power.

Key Takeaway
God's power must relate to all possibles, because otherwise it would have no object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power relates to all possibles, including the 'impossible' in your life, what is one situation you have considered hopeless? Will you now ask Him for it, trusting that His power can bring it about even if your mind says it cannot?
?
When you pray, do you subconsciously limit what you ask for based on your own assessment of possibilities? How would your prayer life change if you sincerely believed God's power extends to every possible outcome, regardless of how unlikely it seems?
?
If accidental impossibility due to God's knowledge does not prevent His power from relating, then your past failures or sins—though known to Him—do not make their rectification impossible. What have you stopped praying for because you thought it was already destined not to happen? Will you renew that plea today?

Divine Power Over Animal Actions
Sanusi includes animal actions under divine power and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that animal actions are under God's power and will, according to the People of the Sunnah.

Core Idea
Animals' actions are created solely by God's power, not by the animals.

This lesson extends the principle of divine power to the actions of animals. Sanusi affirms that all actions of animals are directly brought about by God's power and will, contrary to the Mu'tazila who attribute some effect to the animals.

3

Two Opinions on Divine Power and the Impossible
Sanusi presents two opinions on power's attachment to impossible events and Ghazali's reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opinions on whether divine power attaches to things that are known not to occur, and how al-Ghazali reconciled them by distinguishing intrinsic possibility from knowledge of non-occurrence.

Core Idea
Divine power's attachment depends on whether we consider intrinsic possibility or divine knowledge.

Sanusi presents a subtle theological dispute: does God's power (qudra) encompass things that His knowledge tells will never happen, like the faith of Abu Lahab? Two scholars disagree, but al-Ghazali shows they are looking at different aspects. This lesson unpacks their positions and the reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Note the example of Abu Lahab's faith, which is known not to occur.
The example is crucial—it grounds the abstract discussion.
2
Identify the two opinions
The passage states there are two opinions. Find what each opinion affirms or denies about divine power's attachment.
3
Understand Ghazali's reconciliation
Ghazali says both are correct from different perspectives. Understand the distinction between intrinsic possibility (imkan dhati) and knowledge-relative impossibility.
This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this debate matters for understanding God's attributes. It protects God's power while honoring His knowledge.
This connects to broader themes in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقع ـ كإيمان أبي لهب مثلاً ـ على قولين ، وقد وَفَّقَ الغزالي بينهما على معنى : أَنَّ مَنْ قالَ بالتعلقِ فبالنظر إلى إمكانِهِ في ذاتِهِ ، ومَنْ قالَ بنفي التعلقِ فبالنظر إلى تعلقِ العلم بعدم وقوعه .
It does not occur—like the faith of Abu Lahab, for example—upon two opinions. And al-Ghazali reconciled between them on the meaning that whoever said about attachment (talluq) [of power] then it is with consideration to its possibility in itself, and whoever said about negation of attachment then it is with consideration to the attachment of knowledge to its non-occurrence.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute about whether divine power (qudra) attaches to things that are known not to occur, such as the faith of Abu Lahab which God knows will never happen. There are two opinions: one says power does attach to such things because they are intrinsically possible (mumkin bi-dhatihi); the other says power does not attach because God's knowledge of their non-occurrence renders them impossible (mumtani‘) from that perspective. al-Ghazali reconciles them by explaining that the first opinion considers the intrinsic possibility (imkan dhati) of the thing, while the second considers the external factor of God's knowledge. Thus both are correct from different viewpoints.

Key Terms
تعلق القدرة
إمكان ذاتي
تعلق العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two opinions are contradictory and cannot both be true.
Correction: Ghazali shows they are complementary by considering different aspects: intrinsic possibility vs. knowledge of non-occurrence.

Checkpoint

A) He said both are wrong.
B) He said one applies to power and the other to will.
C) He said they consider intrinsic possibility vs. knowledge of non-occurrence.
D) He said the opinions concern different types of possibles.

Key Takeaway
The two opinions on divine power's attachment are reconciled by considering intrinsic possibility vs. knowledge of non-occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Proof that Divine Power Attaches to the Impossible
Sanusi gives a logical proof that if power were prevented by impossibility, it would have no object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that divine power must attach to all possibles, including those known not to occur.

Core Idea
If impossibility prevented power, then power would have no object.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a concise logical argument (istidlal) to defend the Ash'ari position that divine power attaches even to things that will never happen. The proof uses a reductio ad absurdum: denying this leads to the impossible consequence that power has no object at all. Let us walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the argument is structured as a conditional proof.
The passage has two main parts: the proof statement and the explanation of the implication.
2
Identify the premise and the rejected consequent
The argument begins by supposing that divine power does not attach to something because God's knowledge of its non-occurrence makes it impossible. The conclusion that would follow is that power has no object. This consequent is rejected by consensus.
The consensus (ijma) that power has objects is a key foundation.
3
Understand the implication (mulazama)
Sanusi explains why the premise leads to the consequent: every possible is either necessarily occurring or impossible depending on God's knowledge. If impossibility prevented power, then necessity would also prevent it, leading to no objects for power.
The equality of necessity and impossibility in prevention is crucial.
4
Compare accidental necessity and impossibility
Accidental necessity (wujub 'arid) and accidental impossibility (istihalah 'aridah) are both attributes arising from God's knowledge. If one prevented power, the other would too, but we know power attaches to necessary things, so it must also attach to impossible things.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof establishes that divine power attaches to all possibles, including those that will not occur. This defends the Ash'ari view against those who limit power to actual occurrences.
This is a standard argument in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدلَّ مَنْ قالَ بتعلُّقِ القدرة بهذا النوع : بأنَّه لو لم تتعلَّقِ القدرة بالشيء لأجل تعلَّقِ العلم بعدم وقوعه.. للزِمَ ألا يكون للقدرة متعلق ، والتالي باطل بالإجماع ، فالمقدَّمُ مثلُهُ وبيانُ الملازمة : أنَّ الممكن : إمَّا واجب الوقوع إن تعلق علم الله تعالى بوقوعِهِ ، أو مستحيلُهُ إنْ تعلَّقَ علمُهُ جلَّ وعلا بعدم وقوعِهِ ، فلو منعت الاستحالةُ العارضةُ مِنْ تعلُّقِ القدرة.. لمنع منه الوجوب العارضُ ؛ إذْ هما في المنع مِنْ تعلَّقِ القدرة سواء
And the one who said about attachment of power to this type argued: that if power did not attach to a thing because of the attachment of knowledge to its non-occurrence, then it would necessitate that there be no object for power, and the consequent is false by consensus, so the antecedent is like it. And the explanation of the implication: that the possible: either necessarily occurring if God's knowledge attaches to its occurrence, or impossible if His knowledge attaches to its non-occurrence. So if the accidental impossibility prevents attachment of power, then the accidental necessity would also prevent it; since both are equal in preventing attachment of power.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage provides a proof (istidlal) for the position that power attaches even to things known not to occur. The argument runs as follows: If power did not attach to something because God's knowledge of its non-occurrence makes it impossible, then it would follow that power has no object at all (because every possible is either rendered necessary or impossible by God's knowledge). But the consequent—that power has no object—is false by consensus (ijma‘), so the premise is also false. The explanation (bayan al-mulazama) is that every possible, when considered in relation to God's knowledge, is either necessarily occurring (wajib al-wuqu‘) if God knows it will occur, or impossible (mumtani‘) if God knows it will not occur. If the impossibility that arises from knowledge prevented the attachment of power, then the necessity that arises from knowledge would also prevent it, because both are equally obstacles. But we know that power does attach to what is necessary (i.e., what God wills to occur), so impossibility also should not prevent attachment. Therefore power attaches to all possibles, even those that will not occur.

Key Terms
استدلال
ملازمة
وجوب عارض
استحالة عارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes what it wants to prove.
Correction: The proof is not circular; it starts from an accepted premise (consensus that power has objects) and derives that power must attach to all possibles.

Checkpoint
What is the consequent that is rejected by consensus in this proof?
A) Power attaches to everything.
B) Power has no object.
C) Power attaches only to possibles.
D) Knowledge prevents power.

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that divine power attaches to all possibles, regardless of actual occurrence, otherwise power would be meaningless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power reaches even the things He knows will never happen, then no situation in your life—no matter how hopeless—is beyond His ability to change. Are you living as if you truly believe that? What would change in your dua if you internalized this truth?
?
The proof shows that impossibility does not restrict God, only your limited perspective does. In what areas of your life have you decided something is 'impossible' for God to fix, effectively denying His power? Will you repent of that limited thinking and ask Him for the impossible today?
?
This lesson reveals that God's power is not diminished by what He chooses not to do. How does that affect your trust in His wisdom when He withholds something you desperately want? Can you still believe He is able—and that His refusal is from His perfect knowledge?

Animal Actions: Created by God Alone
Sanusi states the Sunni position on animal actions as pure acts of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine that all voluntary actions of animals are directly created by God, with no causal efficacy from the animal itself.

Core Idea
Every act of animals is purely from God's power, not from their choice.

Sanusi now addresses a point often disputed with the Mu'tazila: the status of animal actions. Are animals independent agents? According to Ahl al-Sunna, their voluntary acts are entirely within God's power and will. This brief but crucial passage sets the Sunni position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms 'mumkinat' and 'makhd'.
The passage is short but dense with theological implication.
2
Identify the key claim and the opposing group
What is the Sunni claim about animal actions? Who opposes it? Underline 'bi-mahd qudrat Allah' and 'la ta'thir'.
The Mu'tazila are named as the opponents.
3
Reflect on why this matters
Think about how this relates to God's attribute of power (qudra). If animals had any causal efficacy, then God's power would not be exclusive in creating events.
This is a fundamental point about tawhid in acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدخلُ في الممكناتِ التي تتعلَّقُ بها قدرة الله تعالى وإرادته : الممكنات الصادرة عن الحيوانات بالاختيار ؛ فإنَّها عند أهل السنَّةِ صادرةٌ بمخض قدرة الله تعالى وإرادتِهِ ، لا تأثير للحيوان في شيء منها ، وقد خالفتِ المعتزلة في ذلك
And there enters into the possibles to which the power of God and His will attach: the possibles emanating from animals by choice; for they are according to Ahl al-Sunna emanating purely by God's power and will, the animal has no effect in anything from them. And the Mu'tazila have opposed this.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage asserts that the voluntary actions of animals (al-hayawanat bil-ikhtiyar) are included among the possibles that are objects of God's power and will. According to Ahl al-Sunna, these actions are brought about solely (bi-mahd) by God's power and will, with no effective influence (la ta'thir) from the animal itself. The Mu'tazila disagree, holding that creatures have some causal efficacy. This sets the stage for a refutation later in the text.

Key Terms
اختيار الحيوان
مخض قدرة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Animals have free will to act independently.
Correction: According to Ahl al-Sunna, animals' voluntary acts are purely from God's power and will, with no effect from the animal itself.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunna, what is the source of animals' voluntary actions?
A) The animal's own power.
B) God's power and will alone.
C) The animal's choice.
D) Both God and the animal.

Key Takeaway
The voluntary actions of animals are directly created by God, with no causal contribution from the animal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the voluntary actions of animals are directly from God with no effect from them, what does that reveal about your own sense of independence? Can you identify a moment today when you acted as though your choice was solely your own, forgetting that it, too, is created by God? What would it look like to live every moment conscious that even your inclinations are from Him?
?
The Mu'tazila attributed some effect to the animal. Are there areas in your life where you subtly assume you have a share in creating your own success or righteousness? How might this belief undermine your reliance on God? Ask yourself: do I truly believe that my every breath, thought, and deed is purely from His power and will?
?
How does knowing that an animal's every move is a direct act of God change the way you observe creation? Does it increase your sense of awe and tawheed? When you see a bird fly or a cat pounce, can you see the hand of God directly, or do you still see the animal as the agent? Commit today to recognizing His sole power in the smallest motions around you.

Knowledge and Speech Attach to All Rational Judgments
Sanusi explains why knowledge and speech attach to all rational judgment divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge and speech are co-extensive and can relate to necessary, possible, and impossible things, and why this division is of a whole into parts.

Core Idea
Knowledge and speech are non-causal, so they encompass every rational category.

This lesson explores a subtle point: how can God's knowledge and speech relate to impossible things? Sanusi clarifies that because these attributes do not cause their objects, there is no restriction. He also explains the logical nature of the division as a whole into its parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Notice the compressed style and the key phrases.
Focus on the phrases 'كل عالم بمعلوم فإنه متكلم بمعلومه' and 'لا يؤثر في متعلقه'.
2
Identify the two reasons given
The passage states two reasons why knowledge and speech are equated in their objects: the imams' principle and the non-causal nature of both attributes.
The first reason is about co-extensiveness, the second about lack of effect.
3
Understand the pronoun and division type
Sanusi clarifies that 'they are every necessary...' refers to the divisions of rational judgment, and that this division is of a whole into its parts, not a universal into particulars.
The use of 'every' (kull) indicates parts, not particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : ( والعلم والكلام بجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي ) : إنَّما ساوى بين العلم والكلام في المتعلقِ لما ذكَرَ الأئمةُ : أَنَّ كلَّ عالم بمعلوم فإنَّهُ مُتكلّم بمعلومه ، ولمَّا كانَ كلٌّ مِنْ صفتي الكلام والعلم لا يُؤثر في مُتعلَّقِهِ .. لم يمتنع تعلقهما بكل واجب وبكل مستحيل . والضمير في قوله : ( وهي كلُّ واجب . . . ) إلى آخره : يعود على أقسام الحكم العقلي ، وقسم الحكم إليها تقسيم كل إلى أجزائِهِ ؛ وقَسْمُ بدليل إدخالِهِ لفظة ( كلٌّ ( في الأقسام ، ولو كانَ مِنْ تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئيّاتِهِ .. لقال : وهي الواجب والجائز والمستحيل
And His saying: 'And knowledge and speech with all divisions of rational judgment' – He only equated knowledge and speech in the object of attachment because the imams mentioned: that every knower of a known thing is a speaker with his known, and since each of the two attributes of speech and knowledge does not effect in its object ... they do not prevent their attachment to every necessary and every impossible. And the pronoun in His saying: 'And they are every necessary...' to its end: refers back to the divisions of rational judgment, and his division of judgment into them is a division of a whole into its parts; and it is a division by the evidence of his insertion of the word 'every' (kull) into the divisions, and if it were a division of the universal into its particulars... he would have said: and they are the necessary, the possible, and the impossible.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage explains why the author equates knowledge ('ilm) and speech (kalam) in their objects of attachment (muta‘allaq). The imams said that every knower of a known thing is also a speaker of that known thing (i.e., knowledge and speech are co-extensive). Moreover, since neither attribute causes any effect (la yu'aththir) on its object, there is no obstacle (la yamtani‘) to their attachment to every necessary (wajib) and every impossible (mumtani‘) thing. The passage then clarifies a grammatical point: the pronoun in the phrase 'and they are every necessary' refers back to 'divisions of rational judgment' (aqsam al-hukm al-‘aqli). The author's division of judgment into these divisions is a division of a whole into its parts (taqsim kull ila ajza'ihi), not a division of a universal into its particulars. The evidence is the use of the word 'every' (kull) which indicates parts; if it were a universal-particular division, he would have said 'the necessary, the possible, and the impossible' instead of 'every necessary' etc.

Key Terms
صفتا العلم والكلام
تقسيم كل إلى أجزائه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge and speech can only relate to what is possible or actual, not to impossible things.
Correction: Since knowledge and speech are non-causal, they can encompass even impossible things; there is no barrier to their attachment.

Checkpoint
Why do knowledge and speech attach to both necessary and impossible things?
A) Because they are creative attributes.
B) Because they do not cause their objects.
C) Because they are eternal.
D) Because they are specific to God.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and speech are universal in scope because they are non-causal, covering all rational judgment categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah's knowledge encompasses every impossible scenario you could ever imagine. How does this truth confront the secret thoughts you entertain—the doubts, the hypothetical sins, the excuses? Will you let that awareness purify your inner dialogue starting today?
?
The attribute of speech is non-causal: it does not force or effect. When you recite or hear Allah's words, do you sometimes expect them to mechanically produce change in your heart without your active submission? What would it look like to truly listen, not for a feeling, but to obey?
?
Knowledge and speech cover every rational category, including what is impossible for Allah (like injustice). Does your heart truly accept that Allah's knowledge of injustice does not make Him unjust? Or do you still, in moments of trial, doubt His justice? Confess that struggle now and ask Him to strengthen your trust.

God's Attributes Are Unbounded
Sanusi explains that God's hearing, sight, and perception apply to all existents, unlike human senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's attributes of hearing, sight, and perception apply to all existents, while human senses are limited by custom, not by rational necessity.

Core Idea
God's senses are absolute; human limits are just habitual.

Sanusi explains a key distinction: what is true for created senses is not necessarily true for the Creator. While our hearing and sight only perceive certain kinds of things, God's hearing and sight encompass everything that exists. The limitation we observe in ourselves is not a rational necessity but a divine custom—something God could change. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding divine attributes as absolute and unrestricted.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text aloud to get a feel for its rhythm. Then follow along with the translation.
Notice the phrase 'على القول به' which indicates a scholarly difference on perception.
2
Identify the three attributes
The passage lists hearing, sight, and perception. Note that perception is included only according to some scholars.
3
Contrast human and divine scope
The passage explicitly contrasts the scope of these attributes for God (every existent) versus for humans (specific to some existents). Understand why this difference exists: it's due to custom, not rationality.
4
Focus on the consensus about sight
The passage ends with a consensus of Ahl al-Sunna that God's sight can attach to every existent. Reflect on the significance of consensus in settling theological points.
Consensus (ijma') is a source of authority in Sunni Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولُه : ( والسمع والبصر والإدراك ـ على القولِ بهِ ـ بكل موجود ) يعني : أنَّ هذهِ الصفاتِ الثلاثَ في حق الله تعالى تتعلَّقُ بكل موجود ، وإنْ كانَ كلُّ واحدٍ منها في حقنا خاصاً ببعض الموجوداتِ ؛ لأنَّ ذلك الخصوص عادي لا عقلي . أمَّا البصرُ : فاتَّفَقَ أَهلُ السَّنَّةِ على جواز تعلقِهِ بكل موجود
And His saying: 'And hearing, sight, and perception – according to the opinion on it – with every existent' means: that these three attributes with respect to God attach to every existent, even though each one of them with respect to us is specific to some existents; because that specificity is customary, not rational. As for sight: Ahl al-Sunna agreed on the permissibility of its attachment to every existent.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage explains that God's attributes of hearing (sam‘), sight (basar), and perception (idrak) – the latter being accepted by some scholars – attach (tata‘allaq) to every existent (kull mawjud). In contrast, human senses are limited to specific types of objects (e.g., sight only perceives visible things). This limitation in humans is merely customary ('adi), meaning it is a habit of God's creation, not a rational necessity ('aqli). For God, there is no such limitation. Specifically, Ahl al-Sunna have a consensus (ittifaq) that God's sight can attach to every existent, implying no object is too subtle or distant for His sight.

Key Terms
سمع
بصر
إدراك
خصوص عادي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: God's attributes attach to all existents; human limitations are customary, not rational, so no such limitation applies to God.

Checkpoint

A) Because God has more senses.
B) Because the limitation in humans is rational, not customary.
C) Because the limitation in humans is customary, not rational.
D) Because God's senses are used differently.

Key Takeaway
God's attributes of hearing, sight, and perception encompass every existent, while human limitations are merely a matter of God's custom, not logical necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself doubting that God truly hears your silent prayers or sees your hidden struggles, as if His perception were limited like yours? How can you train your heart to internalize that His hearing, sight, and perception encompass every existent—including your most secret thoughts?
?

?
When you look at the world—its vastness, its hidden details—do you feel small? Or do you feel known? How does the truth that God’s sight attaches to every existent reshape your sense of being seen and valued by Him right now?
