Objects of Knowledge and Speech
Sanusi's commentator explains the shared objects of knowledge and speech and the logical division of rational judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine attributes of knowledge and speech share the same range of objects and how the division of rational judgment is structured as a whole into parts.

Core Idea
Knowledge and speech share the same objects because knowing implies speaking, and neither attribute affects its object.

This passage from Sanusi's commentator clarifies a crucial point about two of Allah's attributes: knowledge and speech. The author has stated that both attributes extend to all divisions of rational judgment. The commentator explains the reason for this equivalence and then analyzes the logical structure of the division of rational judgment into necessary, impossible, and possible. Let's read the passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist.
Pay attention to the logical terms: wajib, mustahil, ja'iz.
2
Understand the equivalence
Notice why knowledge and speech are equated in their objects: every knower is also a speaker of what he knows, and neither attribute has a causal effect on its object. This allows them to relate to both wajib and mustahil.
3
Analyze the logical division
The commentator explains that the author's use of 'kull' (every) indicates a division of a whole into parts, not a universal into particulars. Contrast the phrasing with what would be expected for a universal division.
This is a subtle logical point about classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعلقات العلم والكلامِ
وقوله : ( والعلم والكلام بجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي ) : إنَّما ساوى بين العلم والكلام في المتعلقِ لما ذكَرَ الأئمةُ : أَنَّ كلَّ عالم بمعلوم فإنَّهُ مُتكلّم بمعلومه ، ولمَّا كانَ كلٌّ مِنْ صفتي الكلام والعلم لا يُؤثر في مُتعلَّقِهِ .. لم يمتنع تعلقهما بكل واجب وبكل مستحيل . والضمير في قوله : ( وهي كلُّ واجب . . . ) إلى آخره : يعود على أقسام الحكم العقلي ، وقسم الحكم إليها تقسيم كل إلى أجزائِهِ ؛ وقَسْمُ بدليل إدخالِهِ لفظة ( كلٌّ ( في الأقسام ، ولو كانَ مِنْ تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئيّاتِهِ .. لقال : وهي الواجب والجائز والمستحيل
The attachments of knowledge and speech. And his statement: 'And knowledge and speech are with all divisions of rational judgment.' He only equated knowledge and speech in the object of attachment because the imams mentioned: that every knower of a known thing is a speaker of his known thing, and since each of the two attributes of speech and knowledge does not affect its object, there is no impediment to their attachment to every wajib and every mustahil. And the pronoun in his statement: 'And it is every wajib...' to its end refers to the divisions of rational judgment. And the division of the judgment into them is a division of a whole into its parts; and the division is evidenced by his insertion of the word 'every' in the divisions, and if it were a division of the universal into its particulars, he would have said: 'They are the wajib, the ja'iz, and the mustahil.'
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory passage about the objects of the divine attributes of knowledge (ilm) and speech (kalam). It begins by clarifying a previous statement by the author (likely Sanusi) that 'knowledge and speech are with all divisions of rational judgment.' The commentator explains that the author equated knowledge and speech in their object because every knower is also a speaker of what he knows, and these attributes do not affect their objects (i.e., they are not causative), so there is no obstacle to their attaching to both wajib (necessary being) and mustahil (impossible). The commentator then analyzes the pronoun reference in 'and it is every wajib...' pointing out that 'it' refers to the divisions of rational judgment. He then discusses the nature of the division: the author divided the rational judgment into its parts (like a whole into parts) as evidenced by using the word 'kull' (every). If the division were of a universal into its particulars, he would have said 'the wajib, the ja'iz, the mustahil.' So the commentator is explaining the precise logical classification.

Correction: The passage clearly states that neither attribute 'affects' its object; they are non-causative.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and speech are non-causative attributes that relate to all rational judgments, and the division of rational judgment into wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz is a division of a whole into parts, not a universal into particulars.

The lesson divides rational judgment into necessary, impossible, and possible. Consider a belief you hold about God: is it necessary, impossible, or possible? (e.g., that God is just). How does this classification demand that you align your actions with the necessary truths of His attributes?

The Universality of Divine Attributes
Commentator defines the scope of divine hearing, sight, and perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's attributes of hearing, sight, and perception (on the view that perception is an attribute) attach to every existing thing, and that the limitation of human senses is customary, not essential.

Core Idea
In Allah, hearing, sight, and perception apply to everything; human limitations are customary, not rational.

The commentator begins by clarifying the scope of three key divine attributes: hearing, sight, and perception. He explains that while human senses are limited to specific kinds of objects, Allah's attributes are unlimited—they attach to every existing thing. This distinction is crucial for understanding how we conceive of divine perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text from 'تعلقات السمع والبصر والإدراك' to 'عادي لا عقلي'. Notice the commentator's interpretation of the author's statement.
The phrase 'على القول به' refers to the view that perception is an attribute.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that these three attributes in Allah relate to every existing thing. Contrast this with human attributes which are specific.
3
Understand the rationale
The commentator says the specificity of human senses is 'customary' (عادي) not 'rational' (عقلي). This means it's a matter of habit, not logical necessity.
This defends the possibility of unlimited divine perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعلقاتُ السمع والبصر والإدراك ]
وقولُه : ( والسمع والبصر والإدراك ـ على القولِ بهِ ـ بكل موجود ) يعني : أنَّ هذهِ الصفاتِ الثلاثَ في حق الله تعالى تتعلَّقُ بكل موجود ، وإنْ كانَ كلُّ واحدٍ منها في حقنا خاصاً ببعض الموجوداتِ ؛ لأنَّ ذلك الخصوص عادي لا عقلي .
أمَّا البصرُ : فاتَّفَقَ أَهلُ السَّنَّةِ على جواز تعلقِهِ بكل موجود ،
واختلفوا في جواز تعلَّقِ ما عدا الرؤيةَ مِنَ الإدراكات بكل موجود ؛ فذهب القدماء منهم ؛ كعبدِ الله بن سعيد الكلابي والقلانسي : إلى أنَّ هذا العموم مختص بالرؤيةِ ، وبقية الإدراكات لا يجوز أن تعم الموجوداتِ ، ونُقِلَ عن الشيخ أبي الحسنِ مخالفتهما في ذلك ، وصارَ إلى جواز عموم كلّ إدراك لكل موجود ، ومذهب الشيخ أبي الحسنِ إمامِ أهلِ السنَّةِ وإليهِ يُنسبون سلكتُ في هذه العقيدة
ونُقِلَ عن عبدِ اللهِ بنِ سعيدٍ أَنَّهُ لَمَّا خص تعلق السمعِ بالأصواتِ ذهب إلى أنَّ الكلامَ الأزليَّ لا يصح أن يُسمَعَ ؛ يعني والله أعلمُ : بل يُدرَكُ بصفةِ العلم ، وفي قولِهِ ذلكَ مخالفة لقواطع السمعِ والشيخ أبو الحسن رضيَ اللهُ عنهُ لمَّا قالَ : ( إدراك السمع يعم كلَّ موجود ( جوّزَ تعلقه بكلام الله تعالى ، وقال بوقوع هذا الجائز على ما ورد السمع به في حقٌّ موسى عليه السلام ، وعمدة الشيخ في ذلك : ما يأتي تقريرُهُ إِنْ شاءَ الله تعالى في فصل الرؤيةِ ؛ مِنْ أَنَّ الوجود هو المصحح للرؤية .
وقد اختلف الأصحابُ في الأكوان التي هي متعلق للرؤية في وقتنا اتفاقاً : هل هي مُتعلَّق للمس أم لا ؟ فذهبَ بعضُهم إلى أنَّ إدراك اللمس يتعلَّق بها ، واحتج بأنَّ مَنْ لمس شيئاً واضطرب تحت يده أدرك
The attachments of hearing, sight, and perception. And his statement: 'And hearing, sight, and perception—on the view of it—[relate] to every existing thing.' He means: that these three attributes in the case of Allah the Exalted are attached to every existing thing, even if each one of them in our case is specific to some existing things, because that specificity is customary not rational. As for sight: the people of the Sunnah agreed upon the permissibility of its attachment to every existing thing. And they disagreed about the permissibility of attachment of perceptions other than sight to every existing thing. The ancients among them, like 'Abdallah ibn Sa'id al-Kulaybi and al-Qalanisi, held that this generality is specific to sight, and that the remaining perceptions are not permitted to encompass existing things. And it was transmitted from Shaykh Abu al-Hasan that he differed from them in that, and he held the permissibility of generality of every perception for every existing thing. And the madhhab of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan, the Imam of the people of the Sunnah and to whom they are attributed, I followed in this creed. And it was transmitted from 'Abdallah ibn Sa'id that when he specified the attachment of hearing to sounds, he held that eternal speech cannot be heard; meaning, and Allah knows best, that it is perceived by the attribute of knowledge. In that statement of his is an opposition to decisive proofs of revelation. And Shaykh Abu al-Hasan, may Allah be pleased with him, when he said: 'The perception of hearing encompasses every existing thing,' permitted its attachment to the speech of Allah the Exalted, and he said that this permissible occurs according to what revelation has reported in the case of Musa, peace be upon him. And the foundation of the Shaykh in that is what will be established, Allah willing, in the chapter on ru'ya: that existence is the rectifier for vision. And the companions disagreed about the akwan which are the object of vision in our time by agreement: are they an object of touch or not? Some of them held that the perception of touch attaches to them, and he argued that whoever touches something and it quivers under his hand perceives...
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory and argumentative passage about the objects of the divine attributes of hearing (sam'), sight (basar), and perception (idrak). It begins by interpreting the author's statement that these three attributes, on the view that perception exists as an attribute in Allah, attach to every existing thing (mawjud). The commentator explains that this is true in the case of Allah, even though these attributes in humans are specific to certain types of existing things; that specificity is due to custom, not rationality. The passage then focuses on the disagreements among Sunni scholars. It notes unanimous agreement that sight attaches to every existing thing. However, there is disagreement regarding other perceptions. The earlier scholars like Ibn Kullab and al-Qalanisi held that only sight is universal; other perceptions are not universal. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari opposed them and held that every perception is universal. The commentator states that he follows al-Ash'ari's madhhab in this creed. The passage then reports a specific opinion from Ibn Kullab: because he restricted hearing to sounds, he held that eternal speech (kalam azali) cannot be heard but is perceived by knowledge. The commentator notes that this contradicts clear revelatory proofs. Al-Ash'ari, by contrast, allowed hearing to encompass all existing things, including the speech of Allah, and affirmed that this actually occurred in the case of Musa (AS). The foundation for al-Ash'ari is the coming discussion in the chapter on ru'ya: that existence (wujud) is what makes vision possible. Finally, the passage discusses a subsidiary issue: the akwan (accidents of motion/rest) which are seen by agreement—are they also objects of touch? Some argued yes, using the proof that a person who touches something in motion perceives it, implying that touch perceives that motion.

Key Terms
سمع
بصر
إدراك
موجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because human senses are limited, Allah's senses must also be limited.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the limitation is customary, not essential, so it does not apply to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are human senses specific to certain objects?
A) Because of rational necessity.
B) Because of customary habit.
C) Because of divine decree.
D) Because of physical limitations.

Key Takeaway
Allah's hearing, sight, and perception apply to every existing thing without the customary limitations of human senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's hearing encompasses every whisper and His sight sees every hidden action, what sin would you find hardest to commit today?
?
The passage says human limitations are 'customary, not rational.' Reflect on a time you used 'human nature' as an excuse for a failing. How does this lesson challenge that excuse?
?
Allah's perception is unlimited—does your awareness of this change how you pray in secret? How will you remind yourself of this truth tomorrow?

Divine Attributes

Scholarly Disagreement on Universality of Perception
Commentator details the Sunni disagreement on the universality of perceptions other than sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement between early Sunni scholars (Ibn Kullab and al-Qalanisi) and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari over whether divine perceptions other than sight are universal, including the specific issue of hearing Allah's speech.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari held all perceptions are universal; Ibn Kullab restricted universality to sight only.

After establishing the general principle, the commentator presents a key historical debate. The early scholars of the Sunnah agreed that sight relates to everything, but they differed on whether other perceptions (like hearing and touch) also do. The commentator sides with Imam al-Ash'ari, who argued for universal perception, and contrasts this with the view of Ibn Kullab that hearing cannot perceive Allah's eternal speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the section from 'أمَّا البصرُ' to 'فصل الرؤية'
Read the Arabic passage covering the agreement on sight and the disagreement on other perceptions. Note the names and positions.
This section ends with 'ما يأتي تقريرُهُ' indicating a reference to a later chapter.
2
Identify the two positions
First, the ancients (Ibn Kullab, al-Qalanisi) held that only sight is universal. Second, al-Ash'ari held that all perceptions are universal. Note the commentator's allegiance.
The commentator says 'I followed' (سلكتُ) the madhhab of al-Ash'ari.
3
Understand the specific case of hearing
Ibn Kullab restricted hearing to sounds, so he denied that eternal speech can be heard. Al-Ash'ari allowed hearing to encompass all, including speech, and affirmed that Musa (AS) heard Allah's speech.
The basis for al-Ash'ari's view is that existence enables perception, not specific modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعلقاتُ السمع والبصر والإدراك ]
وقولُه : ( والسمع والبصر والإدراك ـ على القولِ بهِ ـ بكل موجود ) يعني : أنَّ هذهِ الصفاتِ الثلاثَ في حق الله تعالى تتعلَّقُ بكل موجود ، وإنْ كانَ كلُّ واحدٍ منها في حقنا خاصاً ببعض الموجوداتِ ؛ لأنَّ ذلك الخصوص عادي لا عقلي .
أمَّا البصرُ : فاتَّفَقَ أَهلُ السَّنَّةِ على جواز تعلقِهِ بكل موجود ،
واختلفوا في جواز تعلَّقِ ما عدا الرؤيةَ مِنَ الإدراكات بكل موجود ؛ فذهب القدماء منهم ؛ كعبدِ الله بن سعيد الكلابي والقلانسي : إلى أنَّ هذا العموم مختص بالرؤيةِ ، وبقية الإدراكات لا يجوز أن تعم الموجوداتِ ، ونُقِلَ عن الشيخ أبي الحسنِ مخالفتهما في ذلك ، وصارَ إلى جواز عموم كلّ إدراك لكل موجود ، ومذهب الشيخ أبي الحسنِ إمامِ أهلِ السنَّةِ وإليهِ يُنسبون سلكتُ في هذه العقيدة
ونُقِلَ عن عبدِ اللهِ بنِ سعيدٍ أَنَّهُ لَمَّا خص تعلق السمعِ بالأصواتِ ذهب إلى أنَّ الكلامَ الأزليَّ لا يصح أن يُسمَعَ ؛ يعني والله أعلمُ : بل يُدرَكُ بصفةِ العلم ، وفي قولِهِ ذلكَ مخالفة لقواطع السمعِ والشيخ أبو الحسن رضيَ اللهُ عنهُ لمَّا قالَ : ( إدراك السمع يعم كلَّ موجود ( جوّزَ تعلقه بكلام الله تعالى ، وقال بوقوع هذا الجائز على ما ورد السمع به في حقٌّ موسى عليه السلام ، وعمدة الشيخ في ذلك : ما يأتي تقريرُهُ إِنْ شاءَ الله تعالى في فصل الرؤيةِ ؛ مِنْ أَنَّ الوجود هو المصحح للرؤية .
وقد اختلف الأصحابُ في الأكوان التي هي متعلق للرؤية في وقتنا اتفاقاً : هل هي مُتعلَّق للمس أم لا ؟ فذهبَ بعضُهم إلى أنَّ إدراك اللمس يتعلَّق بها ، واحتج بأنَّ مَنْ لمس شيئاً واضطرب تحت يده أدرك
The attachments of hearing, sight, and perception. And his statement: 'And hearing, sight, and perception—on the view of it—[relate] to every existing thing.' He means: that these three attributes in the case of Allah the Exalted are attached to every existing thing, even if each one of them in our case is specific to some existing things, because that specificity is customary not rational. As for sight: the people of the Sunnah agreed upon the permissibility of its attachment to every existing thing. And they disagreed about the permissibility of attachment of perceptions other than sight to every existing thing. The ancients among them, like 'Abdallah ibn Sa'id al-Kulaybi and al-Qalanisi, held that this generality is specific to sight, and that the remaining perceptions are not permitted to encompass existing things. And it was transmitted from Shaykh Abu al-Hasan that he differed from them in that, and he held the permissibility of generality of every perception for every existing thing. And the madhhab of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan, the Imam of the people of the Sunnah and to whom they are attributed, I followed in this creed. And it was transmitted from 'Abdallah ibn Sa'id that when he specified the attachment of hearing to sounds, he held that eternal speech cannot be heard; meaning, and Allah knows best, that it is perceived by the attribute of knowledge. In that statement of his is an opposition to decisive proofs of revelation. And Shaykh Abu al-Hasan, may Allah be pleased with him, when he said: 'The perception of hearing encompasses every existing thing,' permitted its attachment to the speech of Allah the Exalted, and he said that this permissible occurs according to what revelation has reported in the case of Musa, peace be upon him. And the foundation of the Shaykh in that is what will be established, Allah willing, in the chapter on ru'ya: that existence is the rectifier for vision. And the companions disagreed about the akwan which are the object of vision in our time by agreement: are they an object of touch or not? Some of them held that the perception of touch attaches to them, and he argued that whoever touches something and it quivers under his hand perceives...
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory and argumentative passage about the objects of the divine attributes of hearing (sam'), sight (basar), and perception (idrak). It begins by interpreting the author's statement that these three attributes, on the view that perception exists as an attribute in Allah, attach to every existing thing (mawjud). The commentator explains that this is true in the case of Allah, even though these attributes in humans are specific to certain types of existing things; that specificity is due to custom, not rationality. The passage then focuses on the disagreements among Sunni scholars. It notes unanimous agreement that sight attaches to every existing thing. However, there is disagreement regarding other perceptions. The earlier scholars like Ibn Kullab and al-Qalanisi held that only sight is universal; other perceptions are not universal. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari opposed them and held that every perception is universal. The commentator states that he follows al-Ash'ari's madhhab in this creed. The passage then reports a specific opinion from Ibn Kullab: because he restricted hearing to sounds, he held that eternal speech (kalam azali) cannot be heard but is perceived by knowledge. The commentator notes that this contradicts clear revelatory proofs. Al-Ash'ari, by contrast, allowed hearing to encompass all existing things, including the speech of Allah, and affirmed that this actually occurred in the case of Musa (AS). The foundation for al-Ash'ari is the coming discussion in the chapter on ru'ya: that existence (wujud) is what makes vision possible. Finally, the passage discusses a subsidiary issue: the akwan (accidents of motion/rest) which are seen by agreement—are they also objects of touch? Some argued yes, using the proof that a person who touches something in motion perceives it, implying that touch perceives that motion.

Key Terms
رؤية
عبد الله بن سعيد الكلابي
القلانسي
أبو الحسن الأشعري
كلام أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hearing can only perceive sounds, so Allah's eternal speech cannot be heard.
Correction: In Allah, hearing is not limited to sounds; it is universal, so it can perceive speech.

Checkpoint
What was the view of 'Abdallah ibn Sa'id al-Kulaybi regarding hearing Allah's eternal speech?
A) It can be heard.
B) It cannot be heard, only known.
C) It can be seen.
D) It can be touched.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's view that all perceptions are universal leads to affirming that Allah's speech can be heard.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah, do you unconsciously limit His attributes to fit your human experience? For instance, do you find it easier to accept that Allah sees all than that He hears all—especially His own speech? What does your tendency reveal about your reliance on analogy versus revelation? Will you now trust that Allah's hearing is as perfect and universal as His sight, even if you cannot conceive of how?
?
If you were to adopt Ibn Kullab's position that hearing can only attach to sounds, how would that affect your conviction that Musa (AS) truly heard Allah's speech? Would you be tempted to reinterpret the revelation to fit a rational limitation? Where else in your faith do you need to let revelation expand your reason rather than your reason shrink revelation?
?
Al-Ash'ari based his view on the principle that existence itself makes perception possible. Do you live as if Allah's existence makes Him truly perceptible—in worship, in du'ā, in anticipation of the Hereafter? Or do you act as if He is distant and abstract? How would your prayer change today if you believed with certainty that Allah hears you not as a metaphor, but as a real, direct perception?
